The Red Page
The Minor English Drama
From the giants of the modern European
stage we turn to the playwright pigmies of
England. The dramatists whose plays get
published nowadays are minor dramatists,
earnest cadets assiduously drilling, while for-
eign field-marshals plan new campaigns. We
have no Strindberg, no Tchekoff, no Weder-
kind, no Hauptmann, no Hofmannsthal.
Galsworthy promised development, but has
relaxed into the conventional. Shaw was an
apt imitator of European genius, but already
his plays “date” : the sound and fury of them
will signify nothing to posterity. Granville
Barker is silent. Let us go back to the
minors who are content to do a little thing
well, almost unconscious of the urge of the
great current of experiment and revolt swirl-
ing past in Europe.
At
Here is W. S. Maugham, with his conven-
tional comedy, Penelope (Heinemann, per
Dymook’s Library, Sydney). It is the ancient
story of the wife who sickens her husband of
his infatuation for the other female by con-
tinually throwing the erring pair together.
It is a purely stage idea; and —solely on the
stage—is bound to end in the husband’s re-
pentance. Had Maugham not become in-
fected with the bacillus of popularity he
might have given us a realistic study of that
absurd theatric plot as it would, in nine cases
out of 10, result in real life. The husband
would go off with the fascinating stranger,
and would not find out till after the divorce
that all wives are practically the same w r ife.
(Incidentally, it may be mentioned that all
wives marry the same husband. Justice hav-
ing thus been done to both sexes, we may get
on with our criticism.) Maugham, it is con-
ceded, makes out a good stage case for his
plot. He has chosen his characters carefully
—and conventionally. And in the character
of the wife’s father, a delightful professor
of mathematics, he presents a stage premise
for his stage conclusion. The professor ad-
vises his daughter, who has loved her husband
not lightly but too cuddlesomely, to give her
bored husband his head :
“When every difficulty disappears, Dickie
will find half the savor of the intrigue gone.
Half your battle is won. Leave the rest to
time and Ada Ferguson. Let Ada Ferguson
sit on the arm of his chair when he wants
to read, his paper. Let him account to Ada
Ferguson for all his movements. Under
such circumstances a woman is always on
tenterhooks, and consequently she’s always
exacting. Whenever there’s a pause in the
conversation, Ada Ferguson will say, ‘Do you
care for me as much as ever you did?’ That
speech is a rope around love’s throat. When-
ever he wants to go away, Ada Ferguson
will implore him to stay five minutes longer.
Those five minutes that a man stays against
his will are the nails in love’s coffin. Each
time he leaves her Ada Ferguson will say,
‘At what time will you be back?’ That
question is the earth shovelled into love’s
grave.”
In real life the thin ice would break, but
Dickie returns repentant, just catching the
last train. Happily, Maugham is not satis-
fied with a mere stage reconciliation. The
professor points out that there are other Ada
Fergusons waiting round the corner for her
Dickie. The only way to prevent Dickie
from indulging in flirtations with half a dozen
different women is for her to be six different
women herself. Again a counsel of stage
perfection -
“Remember that man is by nature a
hunter. But how can he pursue if you’re
always flinging yourself in his arms? Even
the barndoor hen gives her lawful mate a
run for his money. ...A wise woman never
lets her husband he quite, quite sure of
her.”
He explains that his own wife had been
systematically unfaithful to him for 20 years.
She had had an affair with the Additional
Curates’ Society, and an intrigue with the
English Church Mission. She had flirted
with Christian Science, made eyes at Hom-
oeopathy, and her relations with vegetarian-
ism had left a distinct mark on her figure.
How could he help adoring a woman so de-
praved ?
The dialogue is admirable, and the whole
false thing cleverly put together.
At
The Curtain Raisers of Eden Phillpotts
(Duckworth, per Dymock’s Library), author
of a fine censored tragedy, The Secret
Woman, are —curtain raisers. There is
some fine character drawing of village types,
but no drama.
A& \
Makeshifts and Realities, two one-act
plays by Gertrude Robins (Laurie) have
more quality. “Makeshifts” is a suburban
comedy for four characters, two sisters
ready from sheer weariness of single life to
jump at marriage with any male thing, and
two male things, a nervous chemist’s assist-
ant and a bounder. Each character is de-
picted with extraordinary sympathy, and
the dialogue is actual speech. The comedy,
which hovers close to pathos, lies in the
characters themselves, not in the way they
appeal to the dramatist. The bounder
raises the hopes of each forlorn sister in
turn, only to disappoint each in the end.
And one sister confesses then that she has a
bit of a headache, while the other goes out
to get a haddock for their lodger, the
chemist’s assistant. That ' is all.
“Realities” is a sequel. The elder sister
has married the meek chemist’s assistant,
and the usual baby has arrived. The bounder
has married his own choice, witu unhappy
results. The elder sister is dowdy, old ;
the bounder’s wife has succeeded in getting
into suburban society. But when the
bounder is caught kissing the assistant’s
wife, the woman sees that her life is no make-
shift, and her husband is a man. Bot-h play-
lets would prove finely effective on the stage.
J*
Three Plays, by John Galsworthy (Duck-
worth, shows this dramatist at his best and
at his worst. “The Little Dream” looks like
a dramatic attempt of his nonage, yet the
drama'tist states that it was written after
“The Eldest Son” and before “Justice.” It
is a vague, undramatic, unpoetic allegory;
and its inclusion in this volume is inexplic-
able —almost as inexplicable as the volume of
poetry that Galsworthy unwisely published.
“The Eldest Son” takes us to the class that
Galsworthy knows so w r ell, the class of The
Country House. The mouldy problem arises:
Whether a gentleman, who has got his
mother’s lady’s maid into the usual trouble,
should marry her or not. Bill, the eldest
when Freda confesses that the usual
consequence of her intimacy with him may
be expected, though he is no longer in love
with her, is impelled, from motives of mere
decency, to marry her. The situation is
nicely paralleled by the case of an under-
keeper and a village girl. Bill’s father, a
bart., had insisted on the under-keeper “mak-
ing a decent woman” of the village girl. He
can’t have scandals in his village. But when
the same situation occurs in his own family,
he threatens his son with disinheritance.
(Estates are never entailed in modern com-
edy.) Bill, of course, intends, after marry-
ing Freda, to go to the El Dorado of all un-
happy English heroes, Canada. It is only
vanished vicars who come to Australia. But
the girl, recognising that Bill has tired of her,
refuses his offer. And her father, the head
keeper, cries with angry pride:—
“Don’t be afraid! We want none of
you! She’ll not force herself where she’s
not welcome. She may ha’ slipped her good
name, but she’ll keep her proper pride. I’ll
have no charity marriage in my family.”
The play is rather cluttered up with Bill’s
sisters, who contribute nothing but their de-
lightful chatter to the plot. But its chief
fault is that the audience know that im the
last act the girl will refuse the offered mar-
riage, and for that refusal the dramatist
hardly gives sufficient reason. When an
event in a play is assumed by the audience
to be necessary, it must be made to appear
inevitable by all the resources of the drama-
tist’s art. A great dramatist would make it
inevitable in some way dramatically effective,
because of some unexpected reason. This
Stanley Houghton has finely done in “Hindle
Wakes,” in which the same situation occurs.
Here, the girl refuses the man because she
went with him only “for a lark.” She looks
upon the episode of their holiday in exactly
the same light as the man does. The hand-
ling of the same climax is what makes “Hin-
dle Wakes” a great play, and “The Eldest
Son” a merely delightful and cleverly planned
comedy.
J*
“Justice,” the concluding, play, was re-
viewed on its appearance as a separate pub-
lication. A re-reading shows it as almost
the highwater mark of Galsworthy’s dramatic
work. This play and “Strife” place him in
the front rank of modern English play-
wrights. But, judging by “The Pigeon,” his
latest play, Galsworthy has done his best
work.
At
The Trial of Jeanne d’Arc, an historical
play by Edward Garnett (Sidgwick and
Jackson, per Dymock’s Library), is practi-
cally the actual course, in historical outline,
of the famous faked trial of Jeanne for
heresy, in 1431. The play has plenty of
horrors, but is essentially undramatic.
At
Rutherford Mayne is one of the leading
playwrights of the Irish school, but The
Drone and Other Plays (Maunsel, Dublin)
shows him to be no Synge. “The Drone” is
dull stuff, merely the conventional modern
comedy with Irish characters. Finer work
is shown in “The Turn of the Road,” in
which the characterisation is much more
deft. An Irish farmer has a son whose sole
delight is to play upon his violin. A broken-
doAvn musician, tramping the roads with his
violin, is brought home by the farpier to
serve as an object-lesson to his son of the
fate of all fiddle-players. And the son,
whose marriage depends on the wish of his
father, burns his violin. But the next day
the tramp is found dead, leaving his violin
to the son. And on the wall it hangs, a
constant temptation to the soul of the musi-
cian. But the girl w'hom he loves puts
heart into the weakling: “Take that fiddle
and do what your heart tells you to,” she
cries. “God made you a musician and not
a farmer.” “And 3mu? What would you
do?” he asks. She tells him that she will
wait for him, till he comes back rich and
famous. The farmer turns him out. but in
the epilogue the grandfather bitterly says:
“Ye thought he was wasting time and
money? D’ye think there’s nothing in this
life beyond making money above the rent?
It’s the wee things you think nothing of,
but that make your home a joy to come
back till, after a hard day’s work. And
you've sent out into the could and wet the
one that was making your home something
more than the common. D’ye think them
proud city folk will listen to his poor ould
ballads with the heart of the boy singing
through them? It’s only us, I say, as
knows the long, wild nights,, and the wet and
the rain and the mist of nights on the bog-
lands —it’s only us, I say, could listen to
him in the right way. (Sobbing.) ... . And
ye knowed, right well ye knowed, that every
string of his fiddle was keyed to the crying
of your own heart.”
And the broken father goes to the door.
“It’s a coorse night,” he mutters. “I’ll
leave the door on the hesp.”
“Red Turf” and “The Troth” are lurid
pictures of the misery of the Irish peasant,
each little play ending with a gun-shot. “The
Troth” has a striking climax. Two peasants
agree to shoot the landlord w r ho is about to
evict them. They toss a coin to decide
which of them should take the dangerous
side of the road. Two shots are heard, and
one of the men slinks back to his wife. The
news comes that the landlord has been shot
and that Moore, to whom had fallen
the dangerous position, has been caught.
Each had sworn that if captured he would
not betray the other. The wife knows from
her husband’s face that he it was who fired
the fatal shot. The husband merely says,
“Peace, woman. Moore has no wife.”
A poet not negligable, in the person of
James’L. Cuthbertson, died at Mount Gam-
bier in 1910. Born in Scotland, and spend-
ing two years at Oxford, he came to Aus-
tralia in 1875. Here he became classical
master at Geelong Grammar School, founded
and edited the “School Quarterly,” and re-
tired after 20 years’ service. The verses in-
cluded in the memorial edition, Barwon
Ballads and School Verses (Melville and
Mullen, Melb., with portrait, published by
members of the Geelong Grammar School),
have practically all appeared in the school
magazihe. The school verses have the swing
and humor that appeal to boyhood; but it
is for his “Barwon Ballads” that he will be
remembered by those interested in Australian
literature. His work betrays a fastidious
taste in phrase, an appreciation of Nature,
and a contemplative and cultured outlook.
Bv the simplest means he gets his quiet
effects, and his scrupulous attention to form
make his verses always pleasant to read.
His range is slight; but within them he pos-
sesses a sure sense of melody. A few of his
verses should occupy a modest place in the
history of our growing literature.

Federal Capital are advised, to avoid delav
to address their entries to this page Vul
sonnet must have only two rimes in the
octette, and the sestette must not close with
a riming couplet. Entries close April 30.
The latest 10 issues, or the 10 latest
issues, of the wonderful “Home llniversitv
Library of Modern Knowledge” (William*
and Norgate; Is. and 2s. 6d.), completing
the first 10 volumes, or the 70 first volumes
range over a wide tract of modern knowledge’
The Origin and Nature of Life, by John
Bailey; Chemistry, by Prof. Raphael Mel-
dola; Comparative Religion; The Navy and
Sea Power; a compact life of Napoleon, bv
Herbert Fisher, indicate the variety of sub-
jects treated each by a specialist, while the
last five, or the five last, specialise in litera-
ture and art. Prof. J. G. Robertson writes
of The Literature of Germany, G. K. Ches-
terton treats of The Victorian Age in Litera-
ture, John Bailey tells of Dr. Johnson and
His Circle, Frederick VVedmore discusses
Painters and Painting, with typical illustra-
tions of the masters, and G. Binney Uibblee
explains The Newspaper. The “bitter and
effective satire” of The Bulletin, one notes,
reminds the author of Slmplicissimus, but
is “not always able to keep up to the high
level of brilliancy and wit, which it has
elected to take as its standard.”
Norman Angell, author of The Great Illu-
sion, finds from the Balkan War new sup-
port for his theory that war does not pay.
In Peace 'Theories and the Balkan War (Mar-
shall, per Dymock’s Library) he replies to
the various attacks that have been made on
his theory; and practically admits the con-
tention of this page that his theory does not
apply to undeveloped countries such as ours.
In the defeat of the Turk he finds fresh con-
firmation of the war-illusion. “As a nation,
he has lived by the sword, and he is dying by
the sword, because the sword, the mere exercise
of force by one man or group of men upon
another, conquest, in other words, is an im-
possible form of human relationship.” Angell
regards the condition existing before the war
as not peace but war; and the Balkans are
merely preventing a brigand from carrying
on his trade, and by co-operation between their
States, are bringing not war. Well,
you can look at it in that way if you like.
“The history of the British Empire for 50
years has been a process of undoing the work
of conquest. Colonies are now neither col-
onies nor possessions. They are independent
States. Great Britain, which for centuries
has made such sacrifices to retain Ireland, is
now making great sacrifices in order to make
her secession workable.” And Angell makes
a valid point in his contention that the Bal-
kan War was due chiefly to Britain, which
had guaranteed for ever the integrity of
Turkey in Europe—and not only the present
war, but the conditions of the Balkan States
for so many years under the tyrannical rule
of Turkey. And Angell is justified in his
assertion that he has never urged that war is
impossible. It is alw T ays likely as long as
civilised nations do not use all their energies
to prevent it —as long, that is', as civilised
nations are not civilised. But we in Aus-
tralia are threatened by nations uncivilised,
and we must meet them with their own
weaoons.

has been appointed iudge of Hodder and

Henry Giles Turner tells the story of the
Eureka Stockade succinctly and impartially
in Our Little Rebellion (Whitcombe and
Tombs, Melb., 3s. 6d.). He admits that it
is a little unkind to dissipate with mere
prosaic statements the mild halo of romance
that has grown round about the miners’ re-
bellion ; but he writes with full sympathy
for their point of view, and a due acknow-
ledgment of the abuses that led up to their
sturdy protest. The main sources of his
history are the full reports furnished by the
civil _ and military officials on the goldfields
to Sir Charles Hotliam, and his comments
thereon when transmitting them to the
Secretary of State for the Colonies.
BARWON BALLADS.
WOOD MUSIC.
How golden was the hour we stood
Within the honeysuckle wood,
When motionless above us lay
The under-leaves of silver-grey,
When from the belt of dreamy blue
The white sail trembled into view,
When all around there seemed to be
The scent and murmur of the sea!
I know not if the ocean air
Can dissipate the cloud of care.
Or if the wattle’s bursting bud
Is potent to enchant the blood;
I only know the restless feet
Of time can give us seasons sweet,
I only know that all was fair
When thou standing by me there.
WINTER SUNSET ON THE AUSTRALIAN
COAST.
Wild skies and wilder waters, and the glow
Of stormy sunset on the forest capes,
And deep below the black fantastic shapes
Of boulders gleaming in tempestuous snow;
The gloomy rollers for a moment show
Upon their bellying green reflected back
2’he sullen crimson of the cloudy wrack.
Driven by the strong south-wester to and
fro ;
A thousand fraying branches interlock
Of hardy ti-tree warring with the gales,
And vocal with a glorious minstrelsy;
But louder yet, on sand and ringing rock.
Round all the island continent prevails
The mighty dispason of the sea.
AT BARWON HEADS.
Dusk purple billows to the far sea-line,
But silver-crested as they trembled o’er,
And everywhere the odor of the brine
And savor of the seaweed on the shore.
Upon the reefs and yellow sand below
The falling breakers sparkled, creaming
white,
And, wreckage-burdened, the swift under-
tow
Swept seaward with unconquerable might.
I know not how, but, standing on the height,
Upon the inviolate border of the main,
My spirit brightened, and the dread insight
And vision of the future came again.
And I beheld the Armageddon war,
The shock of iron navies on the seas,
Grey-gleaming vessels blasted from afar,
And Britain’s banner blood-red on the
breeze.
And underneath the everlasting snows,
Beside a Himalayan river-bed,
I heard our trumpet at the battle close,
And northern mothers wailing for their
dead.
The nearer drew the throbbing of the drums,
And louder yet the ruthless cannon
roared,
And thicker lay the dead beneath the gums
As on the hosts of the invader poured.
And last the Warders of the Loiver Sea,
Thq Lion’s brood, unbroken by their loss,
Arose, 'the Southern Commonwealth, the
free,
The Over-lord of all beneath the Cross...
But swift the vision vanished, and I saw
Only the purple rollers landward turn,
Only the shadows o’er the ocean draw,
And the far ruby of the lighthouse byrn.
—From “Barwon Ballads and School
Verses,” by James L. Cuthbertson.

ELECTRIC LIGHT PLANT
(Lister-Bruston System —Patented).
This plant generates electricity automatically, and does away with large and
expensive storage battery. Can be started from drawing-room or bedroom.
With the exception of keeping the plant clean, lubricating and filling the
tanks, no attention is required.
Upon switching on the lights the plant works, and when the lights are turned
off the plant stops automatically.
The set is self-contained, and is delivered ready to work.. No erection or
foundations necessary; all one has to do is to connect to the wires to the house,
shop, hall, hotel, or town lights, and fill up the tanks with oil. It is exceptionally
cheap as regards lighting costs, the user being able to enjoy all the many advantages
of electric light, at a rate per candle power cheaper than most systems of petrol
gas, acetylene, and other artificial lights, and have current always ready for lights,
fans, cooking, pumps, saws, separators, milking and chaffcutting, sewing and other
machines.
Is absolutely safe and reliable; no smell or fumes, dirt or smoke.

Contentment
IF you have an Edison phonograph you can sit back in comfort, with the
various members of your family around you, and listen with delight to
the tuneful ballads of long ago, such as “Down on the Swannee River,”
“Home, Sweet Home,” “Kathleen Mavourneen,” “Alice, Where Art Thou,”
and all the others, sung perfectly by the foremost platform artists.
Or, if your tastes tend to modern compositions, you can choose from “Thora,”
“Absent,” “Two Eyes of Grey,” “The Rosary,” etc. By way of a change
you may have a record by Harry Lauder, Billy Williams, Arthur Osmond,
Billy Murray, or Florrie Forde, Ada Jones, or some other bright lady may
help add laughter to the evening’s entertainment.
Some people prefer instrumental numbers, and for these there is a wide
choice of Records by renowned violin, mandolin, ’cello, cornet, zylophone
and piano artists, to say nothing of the big range of items by Alexander
Prince, the greatest of all concertina players, w T ho is at present delighting
Sydney audiences. And, as if these were not enough, there are records by
leading bands, raconteurs, siffleurs (whistlers), etc.
You select your programme to suit your moods when you own an Edison
Phonograph, and have every number rendered as true to life as the original,
sweet, pure and natural.
Hear the Edison Phonograph as soon as you can. If there is no dealer in
your vicinity, write to us, and we will put you in touch with one, as well
as send you latest lists of Phonographs and Records.

The American Ring Pulveriser
is designed for
Crushing and pulverising limestone, lime, coke, ore,
brickbats, sewer pipe, terracotta, glass, quartz, phos-
phate rock, sandstone, gravel, tile crockery, flint,
manganese ore, oyster shells, stove lining, shale and
all other refractory materials.
Made in Six Sizes.
The various sizes are made with a view of meeting
the requirements of the small, medium and large
consumer.
Crushingand Pulverising to Fines.
Those materials of a refractory nature can be fed to
the machine in 3in. to 6in. cubes and smaller.
.
m
The several degrees of fines are secured by installing Grate Bars containing interstices of various widths. A measured
degree of fines is secured by running the materials over a given mesh screen, returning the rejections to the Pulveriser. The
coarser the grind the greater the tonnage capacity, while the finer the grind the smaller the tonnage capacity. The more
dry the material is made, the faster and finer the machine will pulverise.
Strength and Durability.
Careful observations, based upon afitual experience, have resulted in adding to and making extremely" strong all parts
of the machine. Extended research and grinding tests year in and year out have brought about the use of Manganese Steel,
a metal that will best resist abrasion and withstand the greatest impafiL
We shall be pleased to show you these in operation in Sydney. Send for Catalogue.

Are you wearing a wrinkled maskf If you are, icon’t you let me tell you how to get rid of it?
I have done it for thousands, and I can do it for you.
Arrangements have been made with Mile.
Eleanor Lawton, World Famous Beauty
Specialist of Paris and London, to furnish
free particulars to all readers of The
Bulletin (Sydney) in regard to what is
believed to be the most remarkable method of
removing wrinkles which has ever been dis-
covered.
Mile. Lawton has been awarded nine Gold
Medals by Paris, London, Brussels, Rome
and other International Expositions on her
great Beauty discoveries. The French Gov-
ernment has honored her with a Patent
on her wonderful new process for remov-
ing wrinkles, and patents are also now pend-
ing before the United States Governments
at Washington.
Mile. Lawton is, herself, a living example
of the great power of her remarkable beauty-
making discoveries. By many she is con-
sidered the most beautiful woman in all
Paris.
She took her own wrinkles out in three
nights by her new discovery many months
ago, after plasters, face creams and massage,
masks, straps, steaming pots and worthless
anparatus had all failed to remove them, and
her skin to-day is still soft and smooth, her
face beautiful, and she has no more wrinkles.
But, best of all, her discovery not only suc-
ceeded in her own case, but it seems to have
worked even more astonishing results in the
cases of others,
On referring to her files Mile. Lawton
selected a letter from Mrs. W. Schwald as
being typical of the many like it received
every day. Mrs. Schwald writes: “I must
tell you how wonderfully your treatment has
taken out my wrinkles. There were a mul-
titude of them, and they grew constantlj
deeper and more plentiful. I was in despair,
for I had tried treatment after treatment
without the slightest success. Like yourself,
I tried plasters, massage, creams and various
advertised concoctions, but the wrinkles
stayed just the same. When I first heard of
your treatment I had little faith, but I de-
cided to make just one. more attempt. With
the very first trial I was able to remove some
of the deepest lines on my forehead. To-day
there is not a wrinkle on my face, and cer-
tainly I look and feel 10 years younger;
besides, my skin is softer and clearer, and
my complexion better than ever before in my
life. I cannot thank you or praise your treat-
ment enough, and I have recommended it to
more than a dozen friends.”
No other beauty specialist living or dead
has ever been honored by gold medals from
International Expositions, »nd patronised by
countesses and ladies of high title and rank
to the extent that Mile. Eleanor Lawton has
been.
You will never again have the opportunity
of consulting free of cost a Beauty Specialist
of such world-wide reputation as Mile.
Lawton. Therefore, be sure to cut out and
use the Free Coupon to-dav. You need send

but merely enclose the following coupon in

Love and Logic.
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I plucked the rose-bed’s rarest bud
For Nell, my queen of hearts, to wear;
But Clare, the gay, entreating stood.
And so it gemmed her rippling hair.
She vowed the flower did sweeter smell
Because she knew ’twas plucked for Nell.
By Clare’s coquettish quip made bold,
I stooped and kissed the dimpled flirt,
And said : “More dear than minted gold
The kiss your vow reserves to Bert.”
Red was Clare’s blush —and red the oath
I swore, when Nell swept past us both.
N.S.W.
BLACKBOY.
Gloom.
Give me my quill of raven plume.
For darkly I am set on thinking ;
Dim-curtained, to the point of blinking,
I shall require the drawing-room.
An Edgar-Alien Poe-like gloom
Fits fancy unto fancy linking.
Give me my quill of raven plume,
For darkly I am set on thinking.
I’ve visions grim of Ulalume,
On certain purple cushions sinking ;
And still I swear I’ve not been drinking,
As possibly you might assume.
Give me my quill of raven plume,
For darkly I am set on thinking.
MAJORCA.
WAIF.
A Rhyme of Old Boots.
This is a rhyme of ancient boots on a beau-
tiful maiden’s feet,
Who tramps each morn to her daily toil for
a bob and a bit to eat;
This is a rhyme of simple facts for fellers
like us to face
Who find our generous native land a noble
and splendid place,
Where three square meals are the usual
thing, and the wolf at the kitchen door
Is a beast unknown, while “Help yourself!”
is the word when you ask for more.
This is a rhyme of a lady not receiving her
portion yet—-
A rhyme of the girl in the old dead boots
we possibly may forget.
Let’s have a rhyme of horses, or a song of
expensive lunch,
Or a roundelay of the moments spent in
racketing with the bunch?
Let’s have a ballad of silken hose, with a
chorus we all can sing,
Or a lilting lay of a summer’s day and the
pleasures of picnicking
In the scented shade; or a carol, say, of
ineffable surf-delights,
Or a really rollicking rhyme of wild, long
musical comedy nights?
All right! But, while the sun is warm, and
the waiting joy-car hoots,
Let’s- spare a rhyme for the brave young
thing who battles in bygone boots !
The day is long, and the day is dull, and life
is absurdly sad
For the bright voung thing on her way to
work —when the bright young thing
must pad
The weary hoof on her uppers, vexed with a
world that’s not too fair
When it gives more boots to the plain old
maid than the plain old thing can wear.
In a somewhat sere, well-seasoned hat she
tackles the track ahead,
And her skirt is built on a plan that ain’t
the plan of the finely-fed ;
But —dash it, she minds her p’s and q’s,
and she does as a damsel should.
And her battered boots are the sign, you
know, of her battling maidenhood.
So let's stand up as the boots go by with
the beautiful maid in them,
And give her a cheer, while we do not fail
her kind boss to condemn!
Let us arise and give three groans for the
stout, slave-dealing soul
Who helps to keep her in olden boots with
his canny and careful dole!
Let us get up and hurl some rocks on the
sweating blighter’s roof,
And call him out in the road, and bellow a
red-hot, rude reproof
Into the ear that hates to hear the logic of
him who flutes
Of a lady who plods on a long, hard road in
old, unmended boots.
victoria.
FRANK HENTY.
The Mother.
They said it was best! The little face
That nestled beside me day and night
Lay still and smiling, but, oh, so white!
And they bore it out to its resting place,
Bore it away from my tear-veiled sight,
Out in the little casket white.
My heart was bleeding and you were gone.
My heart was torn in my aching breast
The love of my life is there at rest,
Out in the dark with my little one.. . .
The cold of the earth is within my heart,
And yet they said it was “for the best.”
But if you had seen, my love now dead
(As dead as my heart, my love, my child),
The little face and the way it smiled,
Could you have left me, could you have fled?
But you are gone. . . .and the earth is piled
On my love and the face of our little child.
They said it was best, love dead to me ;
They said it was best; and you were gone;
But if he had lived, our little one,
My love could have lived whilst I could see
The big, wide eyes where your own life
shone....
Lying there, lying there all alone!
The children play—but they are not yours ;
Our love is buried and you are fled.
I know what the world so wisely said....
It is best.... they knew by their little
laws....
But I feel the weight of the earth o’erhead
....My love is buried....my heart is
dead.
n.s.w.

Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.

Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 17, 1913.
THE REFORM OF UNCLE SAM.
The announcement that the Democratic vic-
tory in the United States will be followed
by a reduction of the tariff has filled the
Tory Foreign-trade press of Australia, and
especially of Sydney, with cold feet, or a
sort of cold enthusiasm or something of the
sort. That press doesn’t enthuse over alleged
tariff victories as it did 20 vears ago. It
has lost faith in the prospective Cobden
millenium, and the coming of the great day
when all the world will adopt the doctrine
of the Survival of the Cheapest. That pro-
spect has beepme as shadowy as the vision
which St. John had on the Isle of Patmos
-—the vision of beasts and kings, and Gog
and Magog, and 24 elders that fell down,
and seven thunders that didn’t fall down,
and three frogs, and a blasphemy which con-
tinued for 42 months. Australia has had
several years of at least partial Protection
over the whole continent, and its Freetrade
papers have made so much more money
during that period than they ever did before
that they would hate to see the situation
violently disturbed. They may not have the
courage to openly recant, but they want to
lie left to wobble darkly in p°ace and quiet-
ness. xind, in any case, the Democratic vie-
tory in the United States would hardly rouse
even St. John of Patmos to wild imagery
and painted language. Of the last 12 Pre-
sidents the Democrats have managed to
elect exactly two. Between 1861, the year
when Abraham Lincoln, the lank rail-
splitter of Illinois, took office, and 1913, the
year when Taft dropped it, their term of
tenancy at the White House has been eight
years. The Protectionists were represented
hy Lincoln, Johnson, Grant, Hayes,
Garfield, Arthur, Harrison, McKinley.
Roosevelt and Taft ; the reduced-tariff
party—for there are no Freetrade delusions
among the Democrats —by Cleveland and
now by Wilson. Until the other day the
reduced tariff party had been out of office
for 16 consecutive years. It has only had a
majority in the Senate during four vears
out of the last 32, and it is still in a hope-
less minority in Uncle Sam’s Upper House.
It has secured a majority in the House of
Representatives for the first .time since
1895. And it is perfectly well aware that it
would still be stranded on the inhospitable
shores of Opposition, and living as best it
could on the scarce and scanty prawrn of
public opprobrium, but for the fact that ex-
President Theodore Roosevelt came back
with much swelled head from his lion-shoot-
ing expedition in Africa, and “threw his hat
into the ring,” according to his own pictur-
esque expression, and threw out his chest,
and organised his famous Bull Moose party,
and split the High Protection vote so hope-
lessly that a comparatively unimportant
low tariff minority was able to crawl into
office sideways.
The Democratic or Comparatively Low
Tariff party in the United States has its
stronghold in the South where the old slave-
owning push hung out, and flogged its friend
Sambo before the war. The old slave-own-
ing push, in its good old slave-owning days,
didn’t manufacture anything worth mention-
ing. It was devoted to the job of dragging
raw material off the surface of the earth.
It possessed a great, ignorant, hulking, en-
slaved black proletariat, and because the
nigger was there to do all the hard work it
came to be regarded as a shame and a dis-
grace for a white man to be seen doing
manual labor. The position was the same
as it is in India or Burmah to-day. and as
it was in Portugal just before the downfall,
and as it always has been where a cheap de-
graded colored race was mixed on a large
scale with a white community. But the
nigger of the Southern States, just because
he was ignorant, uncultured and hulking,
and because he had no prospects in life, and
because he was a nigger, was quite unfit for
any manufacturing industry which called
for high intelligence. As he couldn’t manu-
facture because of his ignorance, and the
white man couldn’t do it lest he should lose
caste, the Southern States imported every-
thing save raw material, and they even im-
ported most of that. They knew practically
nothing save cotton picking. They were so
far behind in the human hustle that they
could, liardly make a gun or a pair of boots
for their troops when the great struggle
came about, and they didn’t even know how
to make iron rails with which to keep their
lines in repair. As importers they believed
in buying in the cheapest market, and so a
sort of Freetrade tradition grew up in the
South —a tradition which lingers in the
hookworm country to this day.
* * * #
The plea advanced for reduced duties is
that they will tend to diminish the cost of
living. It is quite possible that they will
have that effect. Binghi, the old-time Aus-
tralian aboriginal of 200 years ago, had no
customs or excise duties whatsoever, and his
cost of living, expressed in terms of cash,
was nil. At the same time, Binghi wasn’t
a great success. There is, of course, in every
country, a section of the community which
doesn’t know how much worse off it might
easily be, and which thinks almost any kind
of change will be a short cut to Paradise.
The citizen of the United States has griev-
ances, in which respect he is like many other
citizens. But so far as material comfort
goes—so far as concerns the sordid matters
of beer and boots and bread—he is so much
better off than most people that he isn’t
likely to tear his fiscal system to pieces reck-
lessly. In 1911 the Freetrade Government
of Britain was moved to inquire into wages
and prices in the U.S. as compared with
those in England and Wales (where condi-
tions are distinctly better than in Scotland
and Ireland). Its report was that the
skilled artisan earned 235. 3d. in the States
where he earned 10s. in England, and that
his hours were 4 per cent. less. If his wage
was calculated per hour he earned 245. in
the same time that it took the Englishman
to earn 10s., so in the matter of pay he was
almost 2\ times better off. At the same
time he paid 10s. in rent where the English-
man paid 55., and he paid about 14s. for the
victuals which cost the Englishman 10s.
Obviously Jonathan finds living a great
deal more costly than John Bull does, but
he gains far more in excess wages than he
pays in excess of living expenses. He might
be able, if he adopted Freetrade, to reduce
his expenses to Bull’s level, but if he did
it by reducing his wages to Bull’s level he
would have reason to be sorry for himself—
“durned sorry,” as he would probably ex-
press it.
Two or three times in the course of its
history the U.S. citizen has been beguiled
by stories of the cheap living to be found in
low tariff countries, and has made experi-
ments on his own account. He has never
found any joy in the experiments, and every
time he has gone back to the old ways he
has shoved the old tariff a few notches
higher, If the new Democratic Govern-
ment, with its purely accidental majority,
does start to knock bits off the tariff, which
is by no means certain, it isn’t likely to do
very much in that line, and its success will
almost certainly be the matter of a dav.
Circumstances are against it. As the Bri-
tish Board of Trade put the case;—
The average American dietary is on the whole more
varied and more liberal than the British. Besides
this, there is a much wider difference between the
family incomes in the two countries than between
the amounts spent on food The cost of food and
rent combined would be 52 per cent, higher for the
workman in the United States, yet his money earn-
ings are more than 225 per cent, as high as those of
the workman in the United Kingdom. Thus, in the
former country there is left a considerable marg’n
of income over expenditure, which can be, and is, uti-
lised in accordance with a higher standard of living
than is possible for the corresponding classes in this
country.
That testimony, supplied by the Freetrade
Government of the world’s boss Freetrade
country, explains why the fiscal doctrine of
the old shoddy slave-owners of the South is
never likely to get a permanent hold on the
United States. It was the doctrine of a
nigger country, and it was only fit for nig-
gers, and a republic that has grown tired of
niggers has no use for it.
MAORILAND’S STILT-WALKERS.
An astounding sight is the later Maoriland
politician when he mounts the stilts of Im-
perialism. Richard Seddon set the pace at
the time of the Boer war when so many
people became mentally overheated, and the
writer remembers him leaping in the poli-
tical atmosphere one night at the beginning
of the trouble, defying the whole flaming
Universe to “put ’em up.” Here’s a bit of
the outburst from an old note-book: —
The chance of a foreign foe landing a force on
Maoriland’s shores is practically an impossibility.
There is no nation extant which could land a body
of men who would not be wiped out within 24 hours.
Then there was the sight of eight or nine
contingents of swaddies beipg rushed for-
ward to the bellow of “On to the Defence of
the Empire !” Napoleon inspiring his troops
under the walls of Moscow, or Hannibal
pursuing the Duke of Wellington over the
Grampian plains was a circumstance to the
local politician springing his rattle in these
strenuous days. Subsequently came the
shrill crow of Joseph Ward at the Imperial
Conferences, what time he periodically and
dramatically splashed the miniature Domin-
ion into vast Imperial affairs as if it were
the hand of Providence blotting out the
great problems of the age. But Joseph’s
highest altitude on the stilts was reached
when certain wire-pullers suddenly startled
the nation’s ear with the yell: “The Ger-
mans are Upon Us! Dreadnoughts, for
God’s sake !” louseph leaped into the clouds
before anybody else could climb on a mere
cow-shed, and shrieked at Bull : “Have one
with Me!” No inquiry as to what the real
position was ; no consultation with the people
who would have to face the tremendous bill
—nothing. Just the whole perpendicular
bound that has made the self-advertising
politician of Maoriland, and the complacency
of his countrymen, pitiable. It is not cor-
rect to attribute Ward’s subsequent defeat
at the polls to the Dreadnought contribution,
or the manner of it. The baronetcy that
was part and parcel of the job certainly did
raise resentment, but the turning-down of
louseph was probable in any case. Never-
theless, if there was any lesson in the
Dreadnought affair, it has been thrown away
on those now in power.
* * * *
Promptly after lifting the seals of office,
Minister James Allen was handed the
stilts of imperial fervor, and hurried off to
London, the central theatre of the Empire,
where an influential and fashionable audi-
ence can always be relied upon to lend its
good-humored patronage. Allen attained
his object by double-banking. Whereas
Seddon made his coup by pouring into
Africa something less than a dozen contin-
gents and threatening more, and Ward stag-
gered under the spot light with a borrowed
Dreadnought to lay at the feet of a dazed
Empire. Allen brought down the house with
his tableau of more ships on one side and
an expeditionary force of 8000 men
for Empire service on the other. When the
Maorilanders at home understood all that
Allen had committed them to, there was
something very like the surprised yelp of a
newly-trodden-on dog. Allen cabled an ex-
planation to the effect that the force would
iperely consist of volunteers. But as these
volunteers would naturally come out of the
ranks of the home defence force raised under
the compulsory training system, the oppon-
ents of the system continued to yelp, with
assistance from press and other political
enemies of the Government. Concerning
the assurance of Allen that Maoriland
would never see the Empire stuck for a
battleship or two, what could be more free
and easy than the way the obligation was
placed before Bull :
The battleship Maoriland represents the Dominion’s
offer to the Empire, and if the Empire or the
Mother Country [the whole Dam family, in fact]
at any time should want more from Maoriland—if
there is really a need for it—you can come to
Maoriland and ask for it; or you need not even come
and ask; what is necessary will be given. For we
in the oversea Dominions are determined —may I
venture to say, even more determined than you
people in England — to keep the flag flying. It means
so much for us, all over the seas of the world. (Loud
and prolonged tears.)
A moving spectacle; but disturbing for the
next enthusiastic Maoriland parishioner that
drops in. Soon there wall be nothing left
for the peregrinating politician from M.L. to
offer Bull. With 8000 or so of the land
forces ear-marked, and a dozen or so of
battleships thrown in, there will be onlv the
permanent and reproductive works of the
country to fall back upon. And as they
have already been pawned to Uncle Cohen,
that resource is hollow and void.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Nation that Gets There and the One
that Gets Left..
In more ways than one the Balkan war
supplies a high mound of mental strain and
brain fag, on which the Australian may sit
buried in thought about his own future. So
far the victory in the aggregate has been to
the few, while the many have got licked
almost from one end of the campaign to the
other. Yet the few were divided into four
widely-separated armies; they were under
four separate commands ; and they were so
badly severed by ancient quarrels that more
than once they hung together with a good
deal of difficulty. Thus when the Greeks
and Bulgarians attacked Salonika the de-
feated Boss Turk put the city up to auction,
in a manner of speaking, and surrendered
it to the Greek general because he offered
better terms than the Bulgarian one. The
ensuing rage was so terrible that the two
Governments had to rush to the spot and
tear the allies apart, lest they should make
a copious slaughter of each other. For a
day or two they were the maddest friends
conceivable, and each was almost ready to
hire the common enemy to help him against
his brother. In the same way Chukri
Pasha tried to surrender Adrianonle to the
commander of the newly-arrived Servian
contingent, in order to make trouble between
him and the Head Bulgarian, and trouble of
some sort appears to have been very nnr
rowly averted. Yet with all drawbacks Vr
Confederates have won all along the
ail save the Montenegrins, who, though lec 7
oned the best lighting race in Europe, haven’t'
won anything up to time of writing. AIZ!
it lias been a victory for the armies tha
were best-trained and best-prepared Th
Bulgarians, Servians and Greeks, but esped
ally the Bulgarians, had toiled desperaWh,
at the work of military training, and when
the Bulgarian war machine was thrown
against the vast, shambling, squalid, unreadv
levies of the Unspeakable, it was bad for t\l
Unspeakable, despite all his entrenchment,
and fortifications and fighting record Th
Unspeakable began badly. The ex-Sultan
Abdul Hamid, was something between a
coward and a maniac. For 33 years he
never quitted his capital. He fled secretlv
from one bedroom to another, so that the
midnight assassin might not know where h
find him. Every night he kept the whole
of his vast palace in a blaze of light lest
someone should be lurking in the shadows
behind the doormat. He bought about 3000
revolvers, so that he might be ready to shoot
at suspicious characters, and at the end he
didn’t shoot even once. He fell at last
because, though much the larger part of the
army and some of the best generals were on
his side, he couldn’t bring himself to give anv
one general the command, lest too much
power should make him dangerous. And it
has been reckoned that his hired—but often
unpaid—spies numbered as many as 50,000
Certainly several million letters from these
individuals, all denunciations of alleged con-
spirators, were found among the wonderful
old man’s papers when he fell from his high
estate, which fall come about when a Hebrew
officer deposed him in an untidy room, where
he was accompanied by two revolvers, six
cheap American clocks, a tin candlestick, a
hurdy-gurdy, several bottles of medicine and
a small boy. Naturally a man of that sort
left a disreputable army behind him. The
mere fact that the soldiers’ wages had
largely gone to pay the salaries of spies en-
sured that. Abdul’s successors started to
reform things by discharging his officers
wholesale —practically all the old officers had
risen from the ranks and could fight much
better than they could read, while spelling
was quite beyond them —and replacing them
with “gentlemen” who were supposed to be
able to shoot in several languages. In the
midst of this transition the wonderful little
Bulgarian war machine struck the Ottoman
Empire in the stomach and doubled it up,
The well-trained, but not so well-trained,
Greeks and Servians also did good work, but
the Bulgarian was the only one of the Con-
federates who never met defeat. As for the
luckless Montenegrin, he never seemed to
meet anything else. Montenegro has a fine
military system on paper, but in practice it
sticks too closely to the old system of the
Scottish Highlander—the kilt and the yell
and the rush. Something like half the adult
manhood of the little nation sacrificed itself
in the heroic struggle to take Scutari,
which isn’t much of a place after all.
For once the disorganised Turk was up
against an enemy more primitive than him-
self, and, as a consequence, the latter days
of the war find Montenegro the only one of
the Confederates which hasn’t declared a
dividend. Unless its allies stand by it, the
best fighting race in Europe will back out of
the struggle and clamor, a ruined and dead-
broke community, without 2d. to show for
its tremendous exertions. The whole Balkan
row is a lesson in the great art of being
prepared. Other things being equal—often
other thimrs being unequal—it is the thor-
oughly-trained army that Gets There, and
the ill-trained one, even though composed of
better raw material, that Gets Left.

The Non-Australian Conscience.
The Australasian Congregational Union in
its triennial gathering at Adelaide has been
having a bad attack of what is called in
England the “Noncomformist conscience.”
As there isn’t anything in Australia to con-
form to, in this country the article is apt to
take the form of plain anti-Australianism.
In the eyes of the Congregational Confer-
ence at Adelaide the Defence System of the
Commonwealth is condemned because “the
rights of conscience have not been ade-
quately maintained.” J. C. Kirby and A.
G. Collinson talked for nationalism, and
the latter raised an uproar when he told the
divines present that “the conscience clause
was for shirkers.” But the bleat of the
Wowsers drowned the arguments of the men,
and the Union gave its voice for shirk.
Which seems to burst up the last and only
tradition worth a trousers button in the col-
lection-plate which was left to the Congre-
gationalism With some sort of decent show
of truth these pious folk claim that they
are the direct spiritual descendants of the
stern and angry Puritans who fought under
Cromwell at Naseby and Dunbar, and were
in the habit of hunting kings and princes
round the landscape of their sins to prod
them with pikes or remove their heads with
a meat-chopper. The English Puritan
w r asn’t cheerful, but he had a grim notion of
protecting his rights with a knife and a gun,
and he didn’t shirk to any extent even when
Prince Kupebt’s draught horses were jump-
ing on him. In fact, he had conscientious
objections to sitting down to sing psalms
while there was an enemy of his liberty
walking about in a plumed hat, and he pre-
ferred to raise his thunderous chant to the
Lord while the blood of some _ man of sin
was still spurting over his whiskers, hol-
lowing the tradition in some sort of way,
the Congregational people have always been
right up in front when there was any big
political scrap on. and, in the main, for 2W
years they took the right side. They were
the backbone of whatever earnestness there
was about early Yankee nationalism, and
they supplied the sinews and thews in the
mob, aggregation or concourse behind LIN-
COLN when he loaded his gun to shoot at the
aggravated form of State Frights repre-
sented by Jeff Davis. Yet the Australian
fold of these militant and warlike sheep has
declared against the use of the gun for tn
protection of Australia, and on the yer.-
same day it decided not to discuss question
that were purely political. The old Oongi -
gationalist made his politics his religion an
his religion his politics, and that was tn
very best that could be said about him.
variety of the article lately sitting in
laide appears to have so much conscien
that it hasn’t got room for any other bless
thing, and it is not surprising in the fiftht
that fact to know that thing which was on


Oliver Cromwell and his Ironsides is now
only a tender and unappreciated Wowse in
a few half-empty churches.
The Progress of the Peril.
Fkom an ex-Australian in Singapore -
The Yellow Peril moves steadily south, encouraged
by British administration; and the meaning of it
doesn’t seem to be seen by anybody in authority
except perhaps the Dutch, who are using every
effort to keep the undesirables out of Holland’s
valuable East Indian possessions.
Two hundred years ago the Malay Peninsula,
which flings its long Asiatic arm down towards Aus-
tralia, was inhabited almost entirely by the native
Malay, a handsome, indolent savage, not unlike the
Maori, satisfied with his lazy life, his native arts and
crafts, and the very small amount of cultivation
necessary for his existence. Now, the British person
has opened up the country, introduced British capi-
tal, and, of course, British customs wherever they
could be transplanted; and mainly through the great
development of the tin and rubber industries, he has
converted Malaya into one of the wealthiest depen-
dencies, for its size, of the Empire.
But in accomplishing this the British have culti-
vated a factor that must, if present conditions con-
tinue, eventually control politically, as it now does
financially and commercially, the whole Peninsula.
In the days when the Malay Rajahs were supreme,
the Chinaman was but a small item of the popula-
tion. Now in numbers he exceeds all other races, in
wealth he is the ruling power, and in arrogance he
is steadily approaching that stage when he will re-
gard himself as the superior of the white. Immigra-
tion from China has increased from year to year;
and the immigrant has mostly settled down, married
and become a permanency—and his descendants are
British subjects. In Singapore, Kuala Lumpur,
Penang and other towns, hundreds of fine motor-
cars whizz past, carriages bowl along, motor-cycles
dodge through the crowded thoroughfares; and
their owners, in nine cases out of ten, are Chinamen.
Most of the shops and native houses, as well as many
European bungalows, are their property; the Chinese
shopkeeper secures all the Asiatic and a great deal
of the European patronage; the Chinese merchant
(several of him of the millionaire variety) controls
most of the business; and the suave Chinese clerk is
to be found in all Government departments and
European firms. Briefly, in a land to which he is
really alien, the Chinaman has become numerically,
financially and commercially the real power.
Without the Chinaman it is probably true that
Malaya could not have been developed so quickly,
though the potentialities of the Indian coolie are not
to be gainsaid; but whether the Celestial will not
aim presently at political domination is the problem
which is going to have a far-reaching effect, not only
on British prestige in the “Far East,” but on the
foreign difficulties which Australasia will have
to face. Many keen observers declare that the Chinese
are a peaceable race, and. with full scope for their
undoubted commercial instincts, will desire nothing
more than pre-eminence in trade. The writer, how-
ever, has come into contact with many educated
Chinese, themselves naturalised British citizens, and
has discovered that they chafe under the political
control of the white, and should the time come when
they would have to make choice, their adopted
country would be thrown over promptly and their
support go to the yellow race. What saves European
control for the present is that there are so many
almost distinct races of Chinese in the Peninsula,
speaking dialects so diverse as to render conversation
between two sections impossible except through the
easily acquired vernacular of the Malay. Each body
of Chinese, however, possesses a powerful guild, and
once the officials of these various guilds understand
one another, the consolidation of the factions of the
yellow race will present no difficulties. Moreover,
the foreign-living Chinese, through the medium of
papers published in their own tongue, follow closely
the trend of affairs in Republican China; and when
the great Chinese nation becomes a power to be
reckoned with, as it must do. it is certain that, in
the event of complications in Europe, China would be
able to over-run the Malay Peninsula, which with its
wonderful tin deposits and valuable rubber planta-
tions, its railways, docks and fine public buildings,
would become politically a dependency of the Re-
public. The Jap, too, is beginning to find many
opportunities in this “open-to-all” territory; and,
particularly in the native state of Johore. the brown
man is securing a firm foothold. No love is lost
between the Chinaman and his island neighbor;
but the Jap would not hesitate to unite with the
Chinaman against the white whom he so fervently
imitates and hates. .
So there lies, for future British supremacy in these
possessions, a grave danger from the over-running of
the country. Government officials are themselves
privately aware of this, but it is a subject which
officialdom, in true British fashion, prefers to ignore,
while the administrative policy, the educational
facilities, and even the courts of lustice foster its
growth From the Australian point of view it seems
to me that a policy which provides stepping-stones
for the natural invaders of the island-continent is
deserving of a serious protest to the Imperial authori-
ties. But any protest may be unavailing. It pro-
bably will be. All that Australia can rely upon
getting done it must do itself; and surely when Aus-
tralia considers the menace that creeps nearer and
nearer it will be more resolute and more energetic
in those preparations for Defence which, sooner or
later, can alone save its “white” policy.
How Protection Lowers Prices.
Messrs. Cuming, Smith and Co., of Vic-
toria* lately published an interesting piece
of information as to how the present mongrel
tariff “protects,” and also as to how prices
are kept down by the “healthy competition of
1 reetrade.” About 1909 the firm, whose
main business is artificial manure manufac-
ture, started a wood-distillation job at Yarra
Junction (Vic.). One of the chief products
of that industry is formalin, which, up to
1910, had been sold by the chief, and practi-
cally sole, importing house in Melbourne at
10s. per gallon. To the few large consumers
—the N. S. Wales and Vic. State Govern-
ments, and one or two wholesale chemists —-
the new Australian producers quoted 7s. 6d.
per gallon. The importers quoted 7s. 3d. in
reply; but the consumers stood by the local
industry and gave it the orders. Then a pro-
tective duty was asked for, the firm knowing
the ways of importers, and a whole 15 per-
cent. ad val. was given, in 1911. Quotations
being, again called for, the Australian firm
mentioned its bed-rock payable price—7s. 6d.
The importer asked 6s. 6d. —3s. 6d. less than
he had quoted when there was no local in-
dustry—plus the 15 per cent. duty. The
importer got the orders, and Cuming,
Smith’s production of formalin practically
ceased. Unless the duty is raised high
enough to be protective, the local manufac-
turers will be compelled to turn their atten-
tion and machinery to something else, where-
upon, of course, the importer will raise his
price once more to the old 10s., plus some-
thing to pay him for the trouble he went to
to smash the Australian industry. Just
here it may be noted that Cuming, Smith
and Co. employ 150 men at Yarra Junction;
the importer employs two men, a boy and
an occasional carter.
The Anti-Arbitration Yell.
The Bulletin’s Stipendiary Optimist
writes: —
In the last couple of months N. S. Wales has been-
afflicted with a series of strikes. This writer has
no intention of posing as an apologist for strikes. He
considers that the Laborite who strikes, while there
is still the least faint hope of redress from the Courts
is a traitor to his party and to his industrial creed.
I urther, even if there is no hope of redress from thei
Courts, the Laborite who strikes while his party is
in power is a fool, for he thereby declares in effect
that his crowd doesn’t know how to provide the right
kind of Court. The benefits achieved by the em-
ployees, as a result of the Sydney Gas and Ferrv
strikes, would inevitably have accrued shortly from
the Courts, for the reforms which the men demanded
were reasonable. The Darling Harbor railway goods
porters could have gained the points which they
scored last week by legitimate means. And in those
events the Labor cause would have been spared a lot
of discredit, and the Arbitration principle would have
been protected from a flood of Tory obloquy. But
Toryism, in its spirited efforts to cast mud at its
opponents, has overdone things, and in its attempts
to show that Labor has been unprecedentedly lawless
and bloodthirsty in the past few weeks, it has made
an even more glaring ass of itself than usual. The
S. M. Herald said (17/4/’l3) that “this was a time
when there was danger of class-hatred running into
anarchy, and when the red fires of revolution were in
a fair way of being lighted.” William McMillan,
Sydney’s most easily perturbed linen-draper, called a
day or so later, for “a strong Executive, which may
save the country from anarchy and insurrection.”
And Bruce Smith proclaimed that the country was
“no longer fit for a respectable man to live in.”
Do the leaders of Liberalism really believe that this
country is in a worse way, industrially speaking,
than (say) England was in the last century, or in the
century before that, when Arbitration was unknown?
Consider, first, the conduct of the local men. They
struck; and to that extent they were grossly at
fault. But their guilt ended there. They did not
riot or spill blood, or seek to bring about a state of
anarchy. In fact, they took peculiar care to let
the community down as lightly as possible. The
ferry-strikers agreed to man the Government boats;
the railway-strikers to handle milk, on the ground
that it was necessary for children and invalids; and
so on. Never, in an industrial war, was more re-
straint shown. Now, consider the sort of thing that
went on in good old Britain in the good old pre-
Arbitration period. Benjamin Franklin relates that
while he was in London “a desperate battle, with
firearms, raged from eight in the evening till five in
the morning, and many were slain.” He saw “saw-
yers destroying saw-mills, coal-heavers pulling down
the houses of coal-dealers, sailors on strike unrigging
all the outward-bound merchantmen, and closing the
Port of London till their pay was raised, the very
tailors marching in their thousands to ov~-«w'> Par-
liament.” George Eliot gives, in “Felix Holt,”
what has always been accepted as a meticulously
accurate portrait of the Early Victorian worker.
There was no restraint about him. He struck, and
struck often, and on those occasions there were riots
that mostly ended in bloodshed and death. What
insanity it is foy the local party of reaction to sug-
gest that we shOudd hark back in this century to the
anarchic state of affairs which produced the tragedies
of the pre-Reform Bill days! And what a libel it
constitutes in the great body of Australian working-
men to talk of them as revolutionaries and reckless
blood-spillers ? The modern working-man is fre-
quently, taken in the mass, a blighted idiot; but a
dangerous criminal he emphatically is not. The
political party which yells for a reversion to indus-
trial anarchy, because industrial laws have not always
been infallible in their working, and which libels a
large class in the effort to buttress its hopeless case,
does itself no good. All it does is to expose the
rabidly Tory sentiments which in calmer moments it
is so careful to disclaim

The One and Onlv Solution of the Trust
Problem.
Scores of wild old Freetraders started to
hurry distractedly in from the woods, so to
speak, at the first intimation that the
Fisher Administration purposed, belatedly,
to do the right thing by Australia’s absurd
tariff. Already they may be heard yammer-
ing enragedly in countless pubs and such-
like resorts, and the burden of their remarks
is to this effect: The trusts are responsible
for the increased cost of living; but the
tariff is solely responsible for the trusts:
wherefore, if the tariff is raised, there will
be more trusts, and the populace will shortly
be insolvent. A. H. Conroy, of N. S.
Wales, a Freetrader of the later Geebung
period, put it this way the other day: —
The Labor party, by supporting a policy of Pro-
tection, created the very trusts so much criticised.
They would not legislate against trusts in the only
manner possible, by removing the tariff where it
was high enough to create trusts.
The foundation or heart or innards of this
crude mis-statement consists in the oft-
repeated but utterly incorrect suggestions
(1) that tariffs produce trusts, and (2)
that without high tariffs there can be no
trusts. The fact is, as this paper has
pointed out before, Freetrade England to-
day is as much the home of trusts as the
Protectionist United States. There are
two main differences, broadly speaking,
between the trust game as it is played in
the States and the same game as it is
played in the United Kingdom. In the
U.S. the trust is above the Law. There, as
in Australia, the federal system of autono-
mous States enables the trust magnates, by
registering in one of those States, which is
prepared to put self-interest before patriot-
ism, to defy the national Parliament.
Britain, being in the enjoyment of a unified
system of government, is in a position to
keep a powerful hand on its trusts from one
end of the Kingdom to the other. In the
U.S. wages are high, in the majority of
industries, and the trusts make most of their
vast dividends by fleecing the consumer. In
Britain wages are low, and the profits of
the trusts are largely achieved by the sweat-
ing of employees. The scandal of the wages
paid in the lower ranks of the British and
Irish railway services is still fresh in the
public mind. Britain, despite the alleged
inestimable Freetrade boon which it pos-
sesses, has immense trusts in the soap,
tobacco, cement and wall-paper trades, and
in the calico printing, fine cotton spinning,
thread manufacturing and bleaching indus-
tries. The huge dyeing concerns of Brad-
ford and Yorkshire have been formed into
a trust of late years. In brief, combines,
vends, trusts —call them by any fancy name
that comes to mind —are as rampant in the
land of Cobden as elsewhere. The moment
the cheaper sort of British workman fairly
grasps the fact that the Coats and the
Glucksteins of his native land are march-
ing to preposterous wealth over his and his
family’s half-starved bodies, there will be
trouble. Where Britain will score in that
critical hour is in the circumstance
that it will have ready to its hand
a means of instantly redressing such
wrongs as have grown intolerable. It
will not be possible then for any interested
person to shelter himself, behind the Consti-
tution of Yorkshire, or to keep up the divi-
dends of his concern by a calm reliance on
the State Rights of Kent. The Central Par-
liament, in London, possessing the same com-
plete power to regulate the monopolist which
Germany possesses to regulate one of its Car-
tels, may be relied on, for the sake of the
public peace, to exercise that power without
hesitation. The United States is less for-
tunately situated, inasmuch as when its day
of trouble comes it will be no more capable
of putting effective anti-trust legislation on
its Statute books than it is at the present
time. Australia has a useful, in fact, price-
less, lesson to learn from the above facts. It
is not that it should declare for Freetrade,
because in England the trusts, for the pre-
sent, are enabled to sell their products at a
low figure; and it is not that it should con-
tinue to leave its national Parliament power-
less in regard to most of the Commonwealth’s
trade and commerce, because wages in the
only other country where such a state of
things prevails, are high. Such propositions
are for none but unthinking persons who
have been hibernating in distant forests for
vears and years. The obvious scheme is for
Australia to adopt the better features of
both Britain and the U.S., namely the cen-
tralised Parliamentary control of the one
country and the effective tariff wall of the
other. Under such conditions the trust
menace or horror could not live in these
parts a month.
A remarkable admission of class privileges
was made last week by Coroner Cole, of
Melbourne, in the matter of an inquest on
the body of a navy ofiicer’s daughter. The
circumstances of her sudden death must
have called imperatively for an inquest. Ihe
medical man or men who viewed the body
notified the Coroner, and that accommo-
dating gentleman in due course gave a per-
mit for the burial of the body without fur-
ther ado. As to whether he held a formal
inquiry, with a duly empanelled jury to
bring in a verdict, no statement has ap-
peared in print. An advertisement of the
young lady’s decease was published in the
Melbourne morning dailies, but they made
no reference to the event. The evening
Herald, however, published the fact that
“An inquest was held to-day by Dr. R. H.
Cole, the Coroner, but the result was not
disclosed. Questioned subsequently, Dr.
Cole stated that he had given a promise to
the father of deceased that he would not
make public the outcome of the inquiry’’—
the names of the parties being given. With
the desire of any parent to throw a veil of
secrecy over a domestic tragedy this paper is
in full sympathy. To the action of the
Coroner in conspiring with an exalted citi-
zen —because he is exalted —to evade the law
which practically commands ample publica-
tion of inquests, in the public interests to
such dangerous concessions to individual
members of the ruling class, this paper is
violently antipathetic. As for _ Coroner
Cole’s frank admission of his willingness to
strain the law in favor of a personage, when
he probably wouldn’t think of so doing
in favor of a mere person, it can only be re-
garded as evidence of Snobbery having got
Officialdom by the throat. When an ordin-
ary citizen dies under inquestious circum-
stances, the police are notified. The Coroner
and the newspapers are notified by the
police, and the evidence given at the coronial
inquiry is published by the newspapers. Cer-
tainly the intention of the law is that it
shall be fully published somewhere, and the
newspapers ' meet the requirement. In
every case, or nearly so, the relatives of
deceased would keep the whole story dark
if they could, but the Coroner and the police
turn the limelight on it. Public interests
demand publicity, in a general way. If a
cause of sudden death is hushed up to
oblige a personage in a case where publicity
would seem to be uncalled for, every mere
person who wants a case hushed un is en-
titled to the same consideration. The wife
who has poisoned her husband, but can make
it seem quite plain that he poisoned him-
self, is entitled to similar consideration. A
Star-Chamber inquest, whereof the Coroner
denies information to a newspaper reporter,
is an outrage. A Coroner who commits it
to oblige a big-wig. without the least appar-
ent consciousness of his indiscretion, is a
shocking example of ingrained Snobbery in
official places.
There might be a good deal more enthus-
iasm over the N.S.W. Government’s vague
proposal to run a State ferry in Sydney to
connect Circular Quay and Milson’s Point
trams and trains, if the one suburban ferry
which the State does run to connect trams
and trains weren’t such a shocking failure.
At the Spit the Government ferries its tram
passengers across Middle Harbor for noth-
ing, and the service would be a disgrace to
an aboriginal management. The ferry or
punt is supposed to be a connection between
the tram that ends at the Spit and the other
one that carries the passengers on to Manly!;
but never, unless by accident, does the punt
run to meet the trams, and as often as not
there’s a wait of anything up to half an hour
on one side of the stream or the other. So
a through journey from Sydney to Manly,
which ought to take 50 minutes, often runs
into twice that time, and the traffic which
ought to be helping to pay the interest bill
goes to the Manly Steamship Co. Hun-
dreds of complaints have been made, of
course; but all the satisfaction anybody
ever gets is that the Kailway Department
runs the trams, while the Works Depart-
ment runs the punt —as though that should
make any difference.
An uproar has been raging in London, par-
ticularly in the Morning Post, over Aus-
tralian wine. On the whole, Australian
vintages have not scored any large adver-
tisement, and the owners and vendors of the
goods got pretty much what they deserve.
The fatal habit of imitating Dago liquors
and labelling a South Aus. product with a
name peculiar to the product of a European
district has a lot to do with the reputation,
or the lack of it, of the goods we have to
sell. Had the earliest growers of Austra-
lian wine been content to produce the best
liquid of one kind which their own vines
and the local sunshine would yield,
and when they had brought the thing to
comparative perfection had marketed it
under the district name, it is certain that
at least one or two vintages of the Com-
monwealth would by this time have had a
big vogue. Instead, every grower has at-
tempted to produce an imitation of every
blessed variety of wine grown on the planet,
and the result, after 50 years or so, is the
contempt of the proud and the sneers of the
wine-wise. It is the same old foreign-trade
idea that has done so much harm in other
directions; only here we have done more
permanent damage to a promising industry
by importing names instead of goods. A
tariff wall of decent height may set one
matter right, but no sort of legislation can
deal with the blind folly which attaches a
doubtful Portuguese or French label to an
Australian article. The Australian article
is produced as a substitute, marketed as a
substitute, and sneered at as a substitute.
And the fact that the imitation may be bet-
ter than the original doesn’t seem to matter
tuppence.
