“0.K.” : The terms “dirty snob” and “cad,”
yelled at a caustic commentator by Lloyd
George and others in the House of Com-
mons, hardly strike one as being applicable
to the offender. Lloyd Geor'ge did a specu-
lative deal in the American Marconi Co.’s
shares on the strength, apparently, of a Bri-
tish Government contract with the British
Marconi Co. When examined by a com-
mittee of inquiry into the honesty of said
contract, Lloyd George declared that he
had bought his Marconi shares as an invest-
ment. “Not having much cash, he avoided
speculations,” he said. But, under cross-
examination, he admitted to having promptly
sold 857 of the first 1000 shares he bought
at a profit of £750. When a Unionist M.P.
—one Kebty-Fletcher —sneeringly asked
Lloyd George in the House whether his
salary of £SOOO didn’t suffice to preserve
him from the temptation of Stock Exchange
speculation, the old canting cries of “snob”
and “cad” were hurled at the nasty ques-
tioner, instead of argument. As a matter
of fact, no decent excuse could be found for
a Chancellor of the Exchequer who specu-
lates in the shares of a wireless telegraph
co. when they are bound to be affected by
political dealings whereof he is “in the
know.” Nor was it “caddish” or “snobbish”
to suggest that a salary of £SOOO to a Min-
ister of State is expected to hold him aloof
from that sort of speculation even as a
Judge’s rotund stipend is expected to keep
him from buying or selling shares in a pub-
lic company whose fortunes he is about to
influence by a legal judgment. The sneer-
ing criticism of Kebty-Fletcher, M.P.,
may have been as malicious as the gibes of
a Willy Kelly in the Australian Parlia-
ment, but all criticism intended to belittle
the political character of a party by be-
smirching the personal reputation of a self-
righteous individual member of it, is mali-
cious as far as that individual is concerned.
“Snob” and “cad” are merely cant terms in
political uproar. Also, the word “dirt”
might as well be dropped out of the vocabu-
lary at political meetings. It is of no
special value, and has outlived its useful-
ness. Dirt is really soil, and soil is what
food grows upon. In fact, dirt is what we
all live upon. The interjector might as
well shout “earth” or “globe” or “planet.”
“Old Soldier” : Judge lleydon, of N.S.W.,
uttered a solemn warning the other day
about the expense of working the Arbitra-
tion law. But he omitted to mention the
explanation of the undue costliness. It is
largely the system. Every Wages’ Board
must have three members. There is the
Chairman, generally a more or less briefless
barrister with no objection in the world to
stringing out the hearing of the dispute for
weeks, or, if necessary, months. Then there
is the Employers’ Representative who often
has a similar object in view, for one reason
or other. One N. S. Wales Board, after
doddering along since last September, looked
up amazedly and found, the other dav, that
the dastardly employees whose case it was
brooding over had gone on strike. Strikes
aDd cheque-drawings—these are among the
chief products of Wade’s much-boomed con-
traption.
N.S.W. Works Minister Arthur Griffith
told the recent railway strikers the truth
about themselves when he styled them “in-
dustrial outlaws.” It seems the porters
made no attempt to impress upon the Gov-
ernment —their own Government, by the
way—that they would strike if something
was not done suddenly. They could, with a
little agitation, have got the Royal Commis-
sion without resorting to a strike at all, and
thereby have saved themselves some money
and the country a vast deal of inconveni-
ence. They didn’t even warn their own
executive. They never gave the Labor Coun-
cil a chance to act on their behalf; they
never gave anybody a chance to do any-
thing. They just struck. They wouldn’t have
Ugh! It’s Alive!

“I reah-lly cannot understand,” said Bruce,
“Why these infernal people won’t keep
quiet.
They hold their billets safe; then why the
deuce
Raise such a riot?
“We had a nice, dull Fusion long ago,
When Deakin first towards our crowd
seceded ;
Why can’t they fall asleep, and take it slow
As Georgie Reid did?
“They don’t know what majorities are for;
They think they’ve got to hurry up and
do things !
And so we see them wave their fists, and
roar
For horrid new things.
“Although I say a law is base, and vile,
And reah-lly don’t know inhere I ought
to class it,
They look upon me with a scornful smile,
And, Ged! they pass it!
“If I became Prime Minister by chance
(A chance that’s too remote to need dis-
cussing),
1 wouldn’t lead the country such a dance
With all this fussing.
“No. sir ! the party which inspires my love
Would never share in such a vulgar
tussle;
It doesn’t strain and haul and heave and
shove
And make things hustle.
“Should we come into power, you’d see a
change!
Three years of gentle somnolence would
serve us ;
You bet your life we’d give you nothing
strange
To make you nervous.”
And I believe that Bruce and all his crew
Who unto us with eloquent demands turn,
If asked to shove us on a bit, would do
Not a d —d hand’s turn.

The impudent Sydney Ferries, Ltd., is
gradually making the recent strike of its
employees a means to further cut the purses
of the public. Last week another move was
made in that direction, when the concession
age for school-children’s tickets was lowered
from 18 to 16. Amongst others, this has
exasperated Minister for Public Instruction
Carmichael, as it is having a depreciating
effect upon the attendance at his Continua-
tion Schools. > therefore, a State ferry-boat
for students is the to-be or not-to-be of the
Minister just at present. A whisper of this
has led to the suggestion that there should
be a State ferry for domestic servants along
the Parramatta River, whose “concessions”
were also bitten into to feed the starving
dividend-mongers. Likewise, apprentices
living on the North side of the Harbor which
is the monopoly of the co., and working on
the city side, desire a State boat, they hav-
ing been forced to shell out another slice of
their wages. In fact, practically all hands
on the North Shore want a State ferry, but,
as that would be Socialism, and the North
Shore looks upon Socialism as a dastardly
interference with the sacred rights of Private
Enternrise, the position calls for some further
consideration- —particularly on the part of
the North Shore.
In S. Aus. Supreme Court. Doctor (under
cross-examination, excitably) : “No, I am
not excitable. I never lost my temper with
a patient. I will pay £SO to the hospital if
you can prove such a thing.” Opposing
counsel (with the air of one who has infor-
mation straight from the stable) : “May I
take it that you mean that offer literally?”
Judge Murray (interposing): “Gentlemen, I
am afraid I can’t allow wagering in this
Court.” Disappointment of the hospital,
which saw itself on “a bit to nothing.”
Jeames, his theory :
Holman’s job of explaining to King George, last
week, why his Government ousted Lord Denman from
Federal Government House, Sydney, was one calcu-
lated to afflict the expounder with brain-fag. To
begin with, he had a hostile audience to address.
The King is known to have taken a personal interest
in this matter. He considered, seemingly, that he
was being robbed of one of his palaces, and he wrote
to Lord Denman, urging him to leave no boulder
unturned or cliff unshifted to frustrate the attempted
infamy. Whatever merits the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment’s case possessed rested broad-based on the
State Frights doctrine that the Commonwealth is an
avaricious and callously inequitable institution, bent
on grinding the long-suffering, old Mother State into
the mud of insolvency and ruin and all-round misery.
It is all Sussex-street to a ruined mangelwurzel that
George Y. knows little or nothing of the State
Frights creed. Living in a country which possesses
unification, the Geebung point of view would appeal
to him as wildly unreasonable and perverse. We know
him for an unusually free-spoken monarch at all times.
Under the steady drippage of Holman’s fluent casuis-
tries —as they certainly would appear to his bluff nauti-
cal or topmast mind —rage would probably super-
vene, also harsh words of the sort that are unfit for
publication. In fact, that is doubtless the explanation
of the silence of the cable man in regard to exactly
what George said to William on the Government
House question.
A painful episode occurred in Adelaide
last week, and Right-thinking Persons are
still quivering with scandalised grief over it.
Paris Nesbitt, K.C., wanted to appear in the
Criminal Court against the Crown. How-
ever, he had omitted to get a formal license
from the Government to do so. Accordingly
he was ordered to desist. Whereupon Nes-
bitt cheerfully proposed to abandon iiis
SILK AND DAMN THE CONSEQUENCES.
(Moans from the Bench and cries of
“Shame !” “Disloyalist!” “Fire !” “Police !”
etc., from the body of the Court.) Ulti-
mately the matter was fixed up and Paris
remains a K.C.; but it wouldn’t have hurt
him if he had carried out his threat. The
whole K.C. business is plain rot ; standing
on much the same plane as the office of
Hereditary Bearer of the Sovereign’s Ash-
tray and so forth. According to legal fiction
the K.C. counsels or advises his Sovereign.
Of course in nractice he does nothing of the
sort. Many Iv.C.’s would be dealt with very
strenuously if they essayed to furnish George
V. with advice, on the grounds that they
were not only incompetent to advise on any-
thing more involved than a dog-license dis-
pute, but were furthermore guilty of pollut-
ing the Presence with the odor of dead Beer.
Any practising barrister of 10 years’ stand-
ing can become a K.C., provided the Bar
doesn’t object—and. of course, it practically
never does. From then onward he, can never
appear in Court without a “junior.” The
junior’s fee is two-thirds of whatever the
K.C.’s fee is. The junior is of no use to the
litigant—his province being merely to do the
tiresome chamber-work for which the K.C.
has already been paid. And the litigant is get-
ting to know it in vast numbers. Conse-
quently, one of the first results of “taking
silk” is often the halving of the recipient’s
annual income. It is a stiff price to nay
for a new costume, and the marvel is that
there are still plenty to be found who are
willing to pay it.

“Puss in Boots” continues to keep Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) in as hopelessly con-
gested a state as the most frantic anti-decen-
tralisationist could wish—a notable feat this
for any mere feline.
The quite superfluous real shower of rain
that forms the “curtain” of “The Fortune
Hunter” at the Criterion still sends the audi-
ence home delighted. There is something
pleasing in seeing other people get wet, while
you occupy a dry seat in the stalls. The
fuller opportunities for character-acting
afforded Fred Niblo and Miss Cohan in this
play, as compared with “Wallingford” in-
crease this paper’s respect for the two prin-
cipals.
The mysterious title of the next Adelphi
production, “The Woman Pays,” is giving
rise to all sorts of theories. The latest is
that the play deals with a new Suffragette’s
ruse to make men feel uncomfortable by pub-
licly paying their own tramfare and lunch-
money. Whatever it is, the new Marlow-
drama sounds like breaking some new ice.
But if it doesn’t, this paper won’t be much
surprised—so very few Marlowdramas do.
Any Sydneyite who hasn’t seen Madam
Garcia’s car whirling in mid-air, with the
lady sitting unmoved while it turns somer-
saults, should immediately, upon reading this,
snatch up his hat and his best girl and make
a rush for Belmore Markets. Wirths’ circus
will, after to-morrow night, fold up its Polar
bears, elephants and Other matters, and go
forth to gather gold in the parts where the
cow is pensive and the jumbuck void of
guile. Every thrill is still in its place, and
the acrobats and trapeze artists get in lots of
fine work, while the smile of La Belle Vic-
toria is itself worth the price of two drinks
and a counter-lunch.
Two new sketches by Harry Corson
Clarke and George Bryant’s cos. are
the only new items at the Tivoli
this week. Clarke’s “Strategy” is a
delightful little comedy, and he and
Margeret Dale Owen are so near their best
that the difference isn’t worth worrying
about. As for the rest of the programme,
Henri Frepeh, juggler and one-wheel cyclist,
is in his last nights ; Alexander Prince isn’t,
nor anvthing like if : and Taylor and Arnold,
Will Whitburn and Cummin and Seaham pro-
vide enough nonsense to go on with. Next
week the Jackson family of cyclists will
begin its precarious job, and Nella Webb
will give those who didn’t get tired of her
before another chance to do so.
The Panama Canal films having been a
large success in Sydney, financially and
otherwise (chiefly otherwise, if the J.D.W.
balance-sheet is any criterion), the manage-
ment has decided on a re-flicking at the
Crystal Palace. April 28th-30th have been
earmarked for the purpose. Meanwhile the
usual menu is handed round. The best this
week were the Oxford-Cambridge race and
“The White Glove Gang,” which winds up
with a realistic burning-ship scene that comes
along as a blessed relief from the conven-
tional ending of such shocks.
At Sydney Royal next Saturday, “Antony
and Cleopatra” will once more slither adown
the joyous, but highly reprehensible paths
of dalliance, along which they slithered to
such sad purpose 20 centuries agone. Oscar
Asche will revive this picturesque old scandal
on a scale of unprecedented sumptuousness.
The Hamilton-Plimmer co. closed its pay-
able and artistic Sydney season at the Palace
last night. It does not too frequently happen
that a payable season is an artistic one, nor
is it an invariable rule that an artistic
season is a payable proposition. But in this
case the desirable double came off. In a
manner of speaking, both horses won. The
two plays that the co. staged were revivals,
the first being “Dr. Wake’s Patient” and,
the second, “The Woman of Impulse.” There
was a deal of difference in the capabilities
demanded of the co. in the two pieces, but
sufficient was shown to justify the opinion
that the management’s venture is well sup-
ported behind the footlights. As this paper
has said before, no better combination of
comedy talent has been amalgamated since
the memorable days of Brough and Bouci-
eault. At an early date the co. will cast
new plays upon the waters of public opinion,
and if it doesn’t make more than bread and
cheese, the present writer will arise on all
four feet and be publicly surprised. On
Saturday the theatre will be leased to Dad
Bailey and the rest of the family in “On Our
Selection.” The Bailey-Grant-Duggan co.
intends to hold the fort at the Palace for
several months, and amongst other interest-
ing things Edw’ard Dyson’s “Golden Shanty”
will be tried on the dog that may or may
not bark financially at the pay-box.

Any bloque of Sydney who has the habit
of getting violently and incomprehensibly
drunk, boosted or blithered, should make a
point of seeing Paul Gorden, “the drunk on
the wire,” at the National Amphitheatre.
After seeing Paul, he wall know how to walk
a chalk line even if it sways like politics.
There are several other entertainers at the
National, but none like Paul. He is the
unstable limit.
The principal “show” events in Adelaide
since that leafy city was last heard from a
week ago have been the reopening of the
Tivoli and the departure of the “red”
Dandies. Providence gave these latter clever
people an abnormally hot week for their
open-air finish, and towards the end the
public was scrambling for admission. They
go to the far end of Maoriland shortly. The
Tivoli under Mclntosh’s management dis-
cards the old First Part, and puts up a
strong all-round co., though with no people
new to Australia in it. From the Royal has
gone “Faust,” with the excellent stage
crowds, the somewhat mild Brocken revels
and Robertshaw’s cynical comic Devil u-
rendering seems modelled on Henry Irving
occasional low-comedy manner; and n Vr\-
Maxon is of the school of H. B. Irvine wtl C
admittedly imitates his father, one seemfi
see two quite different Irving imitatfnne
going on side by side. Jansen has come back
to Adelaide tor a week of mysterv m o
Royal, and the Exhibition is just unpackme
something new in the Japanese way, brought
along by Carrington Walters. Van Camn
and his erudite pigs are still at the KinFs
along with Hugh Lloyd, doing marvels on
the wire, and King and Miss Virginia
Thornton in short plays—an excellent varin
tion of Variety.
April sittings of Melbourne’s Repertorv
Theatre, at the Athenaeum Hall, began last
week with two nights of Bernard Shaw’s
“Getting Married.” “Sitting” is a tern
which properly expresses the action of this
comedy of ideas, wherein Gilbertianism con-
tends with Socialism for supremacy, and the
result is a draw. The characters come on to
the stage and seat themselves mostly and
talk in their respective turns. Perpendicu-
lar exceptions to the rule are an old-
fashioned greengrocer, serving as a waiter
(who, being an alderman, is desired to g 0
and assume his robe of office in honor of the
occasion), and a parish beadle, who acts as
herald to a clairvoyant mayoress, and whose
fat-headed mace is credited with curative
properties. Incidentally, it transpires that
the beadle is in the habit of touching the
children of the parish with his mace for ring-
worm, even as monarchs of old used to touch
their loyal subjects for scrofula and things.
The waiter in his aldermanic robes, the occa-
sional beadle, the love-lorn General in full
regimentals (the Repertory exponent of the
part suggested a militiaman just out of
pawn), and one or two other people, are
Shawian adaptations of Gilbertian charac-
ters. The music of Arthur Sullivan is needed
to complete their humor. As for the young
heroine, Mrs. Reginald Bridgenorth," who
sues for a friendly divorce from her middle-
aged husband with a view to marrying a
free and breezy young snob, she is not nearly
so human as the irresponsible wife in “The
Night of the Party.” The matron in the
farcical comedy aforesaid admits to having
supposed that she loved her husband when
she married him. and thinks that she really
did love him until he grew a pair of beastly
whiskers. She is a more convincing exagger-
ation of flighty matronhood than Mrs. Reg.
Bridgenorth in “Getting Married.”
* * * «
Shaw’s entertainment in this case is neither
farce, flesh nor good red Socialism. The
characters assemble and talk together in the
“Kitchen in the Palace of the Bishop of
Chelsea.” during the time appointed for the
marriage of his, youngest daughter in the
church next door, and their talk leaves the
marriage question just where they found it.
In most of their remarks they are obviously
insincere. No air of reality blows into the
situation save when Lesbia Grantham, the
grey-haired spinster, suggests that the laws
of Respectability are cruel to the woman who
wants children of her own, but would like
somebody else’s husband to be the father of
them. And even Lesbia’s burst of emotion
raises the question whether her powerful
maternal instinct would have subordinated
itself to her fear of marriage.
Considered as an entertainment intended
to advance the literary drama movement.
“Getting Married” is a failure. Its types
of character are not realised by the McMahon
forces. Two of Shaw’s brighter comedies —
“Candida” and “Man and Superman”—have
been more interestingly played. Miss Hunger-
ford, in the comparatively strong part of
Lesbia Grantham, shines brightly in spots.
Miss Robson handles the irresponsibility of
Mrs. Reggie with a light amateurish touch.
Gregan McMahon, Helton Daniel and Gerald
Carr are the most satisfactory of the men.
The first of this week’s sittings of the Reper-
tory Theatre had not commenced when The
Bulletin went to press.
At the Bijou, five fraudulent claimants to
the property of Watts (deceased), continue
to talk and sing to one another and to the
village constable. The constable talks back
in a slowq deliberate manner, as before,
until the weird little woman with the unpre-
cedented pair of legs, turns out to be the
rightful heiress to the property. After which
all “The Grafters” repeat the glad tidings
that a Yankee exhibition will occur in 1915,
and the American Musical Burlesque Co.
knocks off work for the night. One more
week of “The Grafters” will be its last for
the present, and another three weeks will
conclude the management’s lease of the
theatre. When “The Grafters” smes to Syd-
ney and elsewhere its local habitation will
be provided by William Anderson, it is said.

Notwithstanding the weather's recent re-
lapse into summer, Branscombe has decided
to shift ‘The Jesters” from St. Ivilda on
May 5. A week later they will start a
season at the Athenmum Hall, assisted by a
new proscenium and sounding-board.
At Spencer’s Olympia this week the
assortment of films—grave, frivolous and in-
structive—is about up to the average, or
even more so. And the leading sintiment of
the programme is Irish, bedad ! Those who
have hearts to feel and eyes to weep are
revelling in the biodrama of “Kathleen
Mavourneen,” with Miss Mary Fuller as
the leading lady who never says a word.
The daughter who obeys her parents is
still repenting her foolishness at Melbourne
Royal, where the fate of the man who be-
lieved not in iron ships is united in mar-
riage with a terrible typiste in an unspeak-
able outfit. “Milestones” is now in its
fourth lap.

De Biere is putting in some last nights at
the Opera House, and the tricks, the patter
and the personality of that cheerful magi-
cian fill the bill for about 45 minutes as
before. Welch and Carbasse, the Kremkas,
Rosie Lloyd, Poluski, and the Dago who
meditates vengeance on Spaghoni the Torea-
dor, are staying a while longer. Also, the
Maxini brethren and their brainy dawg
Bobby. The Maxinis without Bobby would
be a good “turn.” In conjunction with that
educated terrier they are one of the best.
“The Vengeance of Durand,” acted by the
Vitagraph Co., and “The Executioner’s
Secret,” a picturesquely -clothed drama of
bygone days, were outstanding features of
last Monday’s programme on the Lyric
tPrahran, Victoria) screen. To-night
(Thursday) a new lot of thrill will come to
pass, the season of a Lyric photo play being
ordinarily limited to three performances.
At the Town Hall next Saturday (19th),
the Taits introduce Eva Gauthier, ithe Cana-
dian singer, to Melbourne hearing, along
with Laurence Godfrey Smith, the pianist
who claims this country for his own.
The Hamilton-Plimmer Co. come back to
the Princess’s next Saturday. Marlow’s
latest and most successful innings closes on
Friday, in a drama that deserves a less
burdensome title than “What Women Will
Do For Love.” The comedy co. is coming
in with “Dr. Wake’s Patient,” affectionately
remembered in Melbourne as one of the
happiest of Herbert Flemming’s produc-
tions.
* * >:= *
“The Count of Luxembourg.” at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne), neither improves or
otherwise on acquaintance. It has its few
moments of genuine comedy, many moments
of pretty waltzy music and most moments of
stereotyped trivialities. In the first act, W.
S._ Percy provides a humorous situation by
climbing atop of a tall stool to discuss busi-
ness with the Count. In the second act,
Phil Smith puts some grains of novelty into
his rather strenuous drollery and in each of
the acts the interuolated dance duet is an
attractive item. The eight ballerinas are a
dream of pretty girls, but the much-talked of
waltz on the stairs, by Andrews and Florence
Young, continues to strike one as an uncalled
for proceeding. A thing that cannot be
more skilfully done by its perpetrators, is
not worth doing at all—on the stage.
“Luniac” : When you come to think over it,
there is a fine field for developing the “block”
system as practised in the news agency biz
in connection with bread and other things.
As I understand it, housewives would have to
patronise the man who had the run of the
block for the time, and other bread-merchants
would be forced to abstain from supplying
customers off their beat. Every morning I
go to work through the city from my suburb
to another suburb. On the way I meet
blokes, etc., who have come from that suburb,
and are rushing the trams to get to their toil
in my suburb. On the “block” system these
fellows Aiould take my job, and I should take
theirs. It would make a big hole in the tram
receipts, but would stop “economic waste.”
Moreover, I live in Balmain, and my “tart”
resides in Darlinghurst. In my hash-tower
the lady-help has a young man who dwells in
Woolloomooloo. It would be handier for him
to call upon my girl, and it would save me
much tramfare and boot if I could cotton on
to his “piece.” This has just struck me, and,
as variety is the spice of life, I am going to
see about it.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Joseph McCabe, the popular scientist, was as far as
Albany last week, on his return to this country.
This time he will make a longer stay than before, and
one result of his further studies on the spot will be
an elaboration of his English lecture, “The Romance
of Australia,” considered from an evolutionary point
of view. The man who makes it his business to
promote public interest in the nature of the land
the public lives in, deserves large audiences, and
McCabe seems pretty sure to get them. He opens
his tour at the Melb. Atjienaeum Hall on the 26th,
with “The Story of Life on the Earth,” an entirely
new lecture. Then he repeats his fine discourse on
“The End of the World,” and gets on to the subject
of “Life In Other Worlds,” and other themes. By
the way, when the eloquent scholar was giving his
“Romance of Australia” throughout England, one
critic suggested that “Mr. McCabe is apparently
retained by the Federal Government to awaken in-
terest in the Comrponwealth.”
His Majesty’s, Brisbane, has been celebrating its
25th anniversary. In April, 1888, the theatre was
opened by the MacMahons and Leitch, with “Held
By the Enemy.” The MacMahons have covered a
lot of theatrical ground since then, and are still
going strong. Charles is en route to Europe to see
if anything there that would induce Maorilanders to
part up a bit more entertainment money has been
overlooked. James ahd Joseph are meanwhile keep-
ing the aged and honorable banner flying above the
business—the former in Sydney and the latter in
M.L.
There is talk of the Julius Knight Co. at Melb.
Royal playing “Man and Superman” anon. If this
happens it will provide an interesting comparison
with the recent Repertory Co.’s performance of Shaw’s
comedy. Barring Gregan McMahon in the part of
Tanner the Talker, the Repertory cast of “M. and S.”
was strictly amateur, and uncommonly well selected-
considering the limited resources of the club. The
performance was good enough. It remains to be
seen whether a stock professional co. can do much
better.
Mrs. Cornwallis-West, formerly Lady Randolph
Churchill, mother of Winston, has been doing two
things—suing her youthful second husband for resti-
tution of conjugal rights, and rehearsing a new comedy
called “The Bill.” The play is of a political pattern.
The measure in question is a Universal Suffrage Bill.
One scene is laid on the terrace of the House of
Commons and another in a room behind the ladies’
gallery. What a chance there might be, by the
way, in a political drama, based on the career of the
politician who brings in the Bill for giving every-
thing to everybody without, at the same time, taking
anything from anybody !
“Way Verley Penn”: Rumor went lately into
various newspaper offices saying that Lawrence Dun-
bar, formerly villain with Blandolt, Anderson and
other managements for 15 years, had died in Chowth
Africa: Rumor told a lie. Dunbar is alive and well
in the country, and early in March signed on with
the Charles Howitt-A. Phillips Dramatic Co, as stage
manager. The co. is producing “Bella Donna,” “A
Member of Tattersall’s,” “Those Terrible Twins” and
“Raffles.”
“Motley”: The biograph has just been showing in
Australia a picture of elephants ploughing, in the
winter off-time, on Sanger and Co.’s circus farm,
Surrey. A mere advertising dodge, of course. Bar-
num worked it on his farm at Bridgeport, close by
the passing railway trains. When the novelty wore
off the elephants stopped. Years afterwards, in his
“Confessions.” Barnum said: “It was only one of
my puffs. It just occurred to me in the winter to
start the elephants on this rig. I reckoned the
news would fly all over the States, and so it did.”
In the Sanger biograph two monster elephants are
hitched on to a plough which could be easily tugged
bv two horses; and that alone should put the fake
away.

ROYAL.
Behold the gentle young lady of the days of
1860 and 1883 — innocent, pure, obedient, etc.,
etc. —being led by her beloved family.
And now behold the
gentle young lady of
1912-1913.
George Bernard Shaw’s latest eruption was a leg-
pulling lecture at Ilford, on “Socialism and the
Middle Classes.” Socialism, he said, spells success.
When he and several other young fellows in the early
’Bo’s started the Fabian Society, they were told by
their relatives that they had inevitably ruined them-
selves and thrown away all their prospects in life.
As a matter of fact, he and his fellow-Fabians had
got on extremely well in the world. “Look at me!”
exclaimed Shaw. “I am one of the most celebrated
people in the world. I am not only a man of genius,
but am making money out of it. Then there is my
friend and brother-Fabian, Sydney Ollivier, who has
become a knight, a colonial Governor and a lot of
other exalted things. Ollivier was our first secre-
tary, and if anybody had told his parents 30 years
ago that he was destined to become the great and
distinguished man he is to-day, they would have
laughed that person to scorn.” Moral (according to
Shaw) : Respectable people who want their sons to
succeed in life and become knights, governors and
exalted things, should bring them up as good
Socialists.
“0.K.”: A Melbourne playbill announcing the
“Anal performances” of George Coppin in 1881 .(he
farewelled some more after that), gives a list of
his theatrical doings from the time of his arrival in
Sydney in 1843. His first appearances were at the
Victoria Theatre, Sydney, on sharing terms, and
his share frequently exceeded £5O a night. He
started management in Tasmania in 1845, and later
in the same year he took the Queen’s Theatre, Mel-
bourne. “The entire working expenses of the theatre,
including rent, were about £47 per week,” so it is
certain that the local stock actors didn’t get princely
salaries. In ’54 he brought an iron theatre and G.
V. Brooke and others to Australia, and made so
much money that next year he and Brooke bought
the lease of the original Melbourne Royal and the
freehold of Cremorne Gardens, “upon which they
expended upwards of £100,000.” On the first night
of management at the Royal the receipts were £478
15s. 6d. Then they ventured the first grand opera
season ever given in Australia, and dropped £3OOO.
The public was not yet ready for "rand opera. But
a one-night appearance of G. V. Brooke in a couple
of farces, when he was passing through Melbourne to
Sydney, brought £531 16s. When the “regular
dramatic season” started, with Brooke as star, the
expenses were £4OO a week and the receipts for the
first night £356 15s. The Cremorne Gardens spec,
was disastrous, and for some years Coppin’s fortunes
fluctuated between ruin and prosperity. After Mel-
bourne Royal was burnt out (uninsured) in 1872,
Coppin got a 99 years’ lease of the ground on which
it stood, built a new house, and was one of the
lessees until 1882, when J. C. Williamson took it
over. Reading between the lines, one perceives that
poor shows and enormous profits were George Cop-
pin’s idea of theatrical business in the bad old days.
He made money very easily and lost it very stupidly
as a rule. The opening of Cremorne Gardens in Mel-
bourne, on the assumption that the whole adult popu-
lation would visit it about once a week all the year
round, was an absurd error of judgment.
“Bloggs” : Ever notice the uncanny passionlessness
of the love-letters that figure in flicker dramas? The
beautiful wife of the black-browed Arizona sheriff
decides, let us say, to forego the delights of life in
an Indian-infested log hut, and to bolt with Bad
Pete the Gamester. She accordingly gets on her
horse, gallops about 75 miles up and down hill, over
plains, through rivers, and in and out of forest glades,
with the following, which she posts in a tree-stump:
“Dear Pete, —Have decided to accede to your oft-
repeated request, and fly to New York. Will be at
the cross-roads at 10.15 a.m. to-morrow. —Yours faith-
fully, Hannah."
Pete gallops about 84 miles, through the same varie-
gated type of country, gets the letter out of the
stump, and writes and posts this: —
“Dear Hannah. —Glad to hear you will be at the
cross-roads at 10.15 to-morrow preliminary to flying
to New York. —Yours resp’fully, Pete the Game-
ster.”
It produces a curious effect, after witnessing a love
scene of the most furiously enthusiastic description,
to read the following in the copper-plate handwriting
of the lady offender
Dear Armand, —My husband is going to the club.
to-night. Call on me then. —Yours truly, Lais.”
There is one good point about this style of corres-
pondence ; it is eminently safe. If picture show fre-
quenters modelled their love-letters on the samples
thrown on the screen, successful divorce and breach-
of-nromise suits would be reduced bv about 50 p.c.
“Saphead”: I’m not going to be so rash
ns to back a fresh champion against cither
the Bwitish cavalryman or the Horsetralian
brumby-chaser, but would like to mention
that there is a darned good rider to be seen
on any night, except Sunday, in any city or
suburban picture-show. The American cow-
boy-actor and his mortal foe (or fellow-
artist), the Redskin, negotiate mighty stiff
pieces of country at breakneck pace. Pos-
sibly the pace is the outcome of the opera-
tor’s desire to get home, early, but I’m in-
clined to think that the fellow who rides for
help, guiding his mount with one hand and
turning in the saddle with the other to fire
at his pursuers with both feet, is fit to be
classed with the world’s best riders. Am
willing to shout “Cavalryman,” “Frank Brown” and
co. to the threepenny seats to prove it.
“Star” : It is satisfactory to kriow that Melbourne
Shakspeare Society has resolved to emnloy its ener-
gies in the erection of a Shakspeare Theatre, rather
than in the foolish business of piling up more marble
effigies to William’s immortal memory. In the work
of building a playhouse the Repertory Club will co-
operate ; and that makes the repertory movement
look healthy and hopeful. An adequate theatre is a
crying need. Without it there would be small chance
of roping in more than the noble 400, whose, guineas
serve to keep the Repertory movement geeing, and
whose enthusiasm can’t atone for the absence of a
simple, but suitable establishment, in which to house
the literary play.
(Continued on page 36.)
The Sins of Man and the injustice of
“Man-made laws” and Man’s administra-
tion of them will do more to help the suffra-
gette movement in England than 50 bonfires
in letter-boxes. For instance, under the
heading, “Why Some Women are Militant,”
the organ of window-breakage reports the
case (taken from the Yorkshire Evening
Post) of a well-to-do man who decoyed a
couple of eight-year-old girls to his lodgings
and “assaulted” them. The Bench con-
sidered the offence proved in one instance,
at any rate, and the well-to-do gentleman
was fined £lO and costs. It goes without
saying that all the indignant mothers in that
district are disposed to sympathise with any
woman who gets three months in quod for
breaking a shop window in a moment of
political agitation.
A Mr. McKenna, who must be more Scawtch
than Irish, gravely announced to the House
of Commons that “forcible feeding of suffra-
gette prisoners would not be employed in
future unless the offences were dangerous
to the public.” Which means that a quite
harmless enthusiast who threatens to give
the policeman a good hard smack if he de-
clines to be otherwise goaded into arresting
her will have the option of dying of starva-
tion in prison. Even a noisy nocturnal win-
dow smasher will be allowed to refuse food
in gaol. In fact, those suffragettes who are
well worth keeping alive will get the Law’s
generous permission to die, whereas the few
who ought to die will be carefully kept alive
lest they cheat the hangman.
Commerce is a careful soul —about its own
profits. For some time past it has been
known that regulations governing the sale
of infants’ food were to be issued under the
authority of the Commerce Act, and these
were recently framed. One of them pres-
cribes that certain articles shall be branded
with the words, “This food is ordinarily un-
suitable for infants under the age of six
months, and is not to be given to such in-
fants except under medical direction.” The-
Sydney Chamber of Commerce doesn’t appar-
ently believe that such articles are good for'
little Australia’s stomach; but it suddenly
remembered that some of its members had!
stocks on which the warning label hadn’t;
been stuck, wherefore it gravely held a meet-
ing and “decided to ask the Comptroller-
General to hold the regulation over untill
January 1, 1914, so as to give merchants
and manufacturers a chance of disposing of
present stocks.” Little Australia’s stomach
may suffer in the meantime, and a few
graves be digged in consequence; but what
is that by comparison with giving manufac-'
turers and merchants a chance of disposing
of present stocks?
GENEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Adeline Genge, the dancer, has been engaged by
J. C. Williamson for a tour of Australia. — Cable,
VICTORIA.
I am sick to death of coons
And Gaby glides,
Of the old plantation croons
And dusky brides.
Of our grizzlies, turkey trots—
Terpsichorean sots—
And I wait for Motion’s Queen,
For the art of Adeline—
The art of fairy Adeline Genee.
When the world was very young
The dance was born ;
Ere mature the primal tongue
ur bright the morn.
David danced before the Lord
(Who a front seat could afford)
And the Greeks made dancing mean
All the joy of Adeline —
Light-footed joy of Adeline Genge.
The old Iloifteric dance
Told a tale ;
Aristotle foiind romance
Shall not fail;
’Tis of poetry a kind
Light as Hesperidean wind ;
Vol'tion scarcely seen
Is the art of Adeline—
The fairy art of Adeline Genge.
The Pyhrric dance sent Spartan
Forth to fight,
And the Scotch reel done in tartan
’S a mad sight.
And the dance known as St. Vitus—
(Who was a co-saint of Titus
Or of someone else) —is mean
When compared with Adeline—
The art of fairy Adeline Genee.
There are dances wild and various
—Dances mad :
The dance of St. Macarius
Silly-sad.
And the early Christian prancing
And the bayaderes dancing;
But of motion there’s one queen,
And her name is Adeline —-
Volition’s poet—Adeline Genge.
In the lights of London town
Years ago ;
Where the millions, up and down,
Ebb gnd flow.
From the Circus, Piccadilly,
Came the thousands, sane and silly,
Came broker and the Dean
For to iook at Adeline—
For to see the fairy Adeline Genge.
In the lights of Leicester Square—
(Long years away!)
Is a dancer, dainty, fair
As any fay ;
She’s a sylph and dryad, too,
And as delicate as Blue,
Sfivres china for a queen—
And a fay is Adeline—
Queen of the Empire, Adeline Genge.

For sheer nerve commend this paper to
the Sydney Telegraph’s smug effort
(10/4/T3). headed “The Right to the
Streets.” “Are the streets of Sydney in-
tended for traffic or public speaking?” asked
the Telegraph of the Minister for Justice,
in a very firm tone of voice. With particu-
lar reference to the evils of street oratory
the paper remarked :
It has been complained that the prevention or
punishment of street speaking is a blow at the liberty
of the subject. But what about the liberty of transit
to which a crowded way may put an end?
Every time there is a Federal or State elec-
tion the Telegraph addresses the populace
on the subject, in a manner of speaking,
from its front windows in King-street. The
oration is not a mere fleeting nuisance of
of an hour or so’s duration. It begins in
the afternoon of the contest, and lasts all
night and well on into the next day. Dur-
ing a good deal of that time King-street, the
most “crowded way” in Sydney, is rendered
utterly useless for purposes of traffic. A
more or less undesirable crowd assembles in
its thousands, and cheers and hoots, jostles,
picks pockets, curses, and as the evening
waxes, gets drunk —that is a goodly propor-
tion of it does. Portions of it become com-
bative. The already scanty supply of police
is depleted in places where a policeman is
an asset, in order that the crowd attracted
by the Telegraph to King-street, may be
watched, and from time to time arrested.
No public service is done by this interference
with the liberty of transit through King-
street. The facts suplied from the Tele-
graph’s front windows are essentially the
sort that will keen for an indefinite period.
And the nuisance, so far as many thousands
of citizens are concerned, is intolerable. If
there was any consistency about the Tele-
graph it would jump on itself furiously for
its past misdeeds in the way of erecting a
vast board over its front door and pasting
election returns theron, with the object of
luring a crowd and earning advertisement,
which is to say money. But will it? Not
on its life.
A pathetic specimen of the sort of work-
ers who suffer from trades unionism is
Albert Peary, who related his woes at Syd-
ney Central Police Court the other day.
Peary stated that every time he got a chance
of “putting his shoulder to the wheel” the
unions pushed him from his billet, and, as
the result of his anxiety to get work, he had
acquired insomnia. His suppressed energy
kept him awake at night, he said. The
police said that poor Peary had been con-
victed of robbery in various cities of divers
States, and the Bench sent him to nurse his
grievance against trades unionism in gaol—
for one month.
* * * *
The Sydney railway porters’ strike was
doomed from the moment the locomotive
and signalling staffs declined to join. They
were men who have never yet been beaten
in any railway strike by being replaced.
They have been defeated by bluff, or by lack
of funds, but no railway system in the world
has ever organised at short notice a new
loco, and signalling staff to take the jobs of
the men who had gone out. A goods porters’
work is not altogether unskilled —no work
is—but it is work that is easily learned and
can be done by unskilled men working under
skilled direction. The only skilled men
among the strikers were the shunters. Their
places would have been somewhat hard to
fill, for their work requires not only know-
ledge but constant watchfulness, foresight
and judgment. But, in a service like that
of Sydney, there are any number of men
who, though holding higher positions at pre-
sent, were, a few years ago, smart shunters.
Unless they could be induced to come out
also, they could keep things going in some
sort of way, and train fresh men. The loco,
and signalling men are the autocrats of the
railway service, and the Amalgamated
Society, apparently under the fatal influ-
ence of Catts, who is really only big enough
to be kitten, made the blunder of taking up
a hostile attitude towards the particular
union which really holds the fate of any
railway strike in its hand. For the good
terms on which the strikers are going back
they can thank Minister Carmichael. Left
to the tender mercies of Johnson, unassisted
by the loco, and signal men, they would have
been smashed in a week.
“Briner” barks some information: —
The Maoriland Dreadnought is sister to our own
battle cruiser Australia, so now we’ve seen what
we’re going to get. Eight 12in. guns and 16 4i-n.
quick-firers. Twelve-inch steel over the' Vital parts.
Steamed 27 knots on trial without developing en-
gines to .the full. Believed she can do 29 knots —an
awful pace for such a mass, and involving terrific
coal-eating. At full speed the coal-eating is 36 tons
an hAur. Eight thousand tons were used in coming
from Durban. Only eight tons left in Melbourne.
But the 650-ton oil reserve was intact. They were
going to try her with 24 hours’ speed crossing to
Wellington. ” The Maoriland ship is an improvement
on the previous best, the Indefatigable, especially in
range of fire, and the Australia will give the Maori-
land craft a little point or two. The two pairs of
turret guns amidships can range almost all round, and
all the big guns can be trained for either broadside,
with four big guns to bear ahead for a chase. Two
others can be trained far past the broadside position.
Four turrets, each two guns. The crack American
visitors, like the Connecticut and Louisiana, only
had two turrets, each two 12in. guns. But they are
six years old, and thus miles behind Australia’s
beauty—to come.
CRUEL MAN.

1ST B OWLER: “Why, I admitted I was wrong before she had a chance to argue with me.”
The Victorian police are as ineffective with
a revolver as with a “clue,” but some of them
can aim straight with bricks and sundries.
Thus the other Saturday afternoon a citizen,
happening to revisit his city shop, discov-
ered a man investigating the contents of his
safe. He proceeded to tackle him, but was
discouraged by a heavy hammer. With
assistance, however, he captured the intru-
der and sent for the police. A ’tec arriving,
the latter handcuffed his prisoner, collected
certain burgling tools, and started to escort
the safe-breaker to the lock-up. But the
victim wasn’t taking any lock-up, and
sprinted swiftly down the street, with the
Law lumbering in the rear. The Law was
rapidly losing ground, when it aimed a 21b.
weight at its escapee. The shot was good,
and broke his wind. He recovered, however,
so the hammer next caught him in an awk-
ward spot and steadied him. But even with
this handicap he still had the pace of the
Law, so a brace and bit were propelled with
excellent aim. But it took a lot to bring
that bird down, and the Law would proba-
bly have been reduced to heaving tramcars
if a passer-by hadn’t stuck his foot in front
of the runaway, and ended the show. Then
the pair returned on their tracks to gather
up the weapons, and found that all but the
21b. weight had disappeared. No loose pro-
perty is allowed to lie on Melbourne’s streets
very long. # ,
The Sydney papers thought they had a
fine dramatic subject in the isolation of a
party of fishermen at North Head. These
hunters of fish had landed on a ledge of rock
adjacent to a comfortable cave where they
are in the habit of camping. The sea rose
and smashed their boat. One of the party
was apparently a raw haiid, unable to appre-
ciate the subtle enjqyfnents of fish. To be
a good fisherma-p, It’ seems, is the same as
being a Covenanter in the days of
Charles the Twoth of England, son of the
Man of Sin with the long, essenced hair.
Old David Leslie’s idea of a Coven-
anter was a man who could sit out on a wet
hillside in pouring rain listening to a prayer
an hour long and a sermon two hours long,
and enjoy it. So the raw hand left, climbed
the wall of Australia, told someone he met
that there were blokes marooned down below,
and went home. Rescue parties went out,
and got into communication by lowering lit-
erature on a line. The reply turned out to
be a furious request for tobacco. Tobacco
was supplied with a note suggesting that
the parties should be hoisted in a basket.
The answer was to the effect that they didn’t
want to break their necks in a basket. Pro-
bably they were surprised that a few fisher-
men couldn’t take a week’s holiday without
having pilot steamers and police with ropes
chasing them up. And then the sensation
fizzled out.
Westralia’s boast regarding its immunity
from railway accidents is commencing to
wear a wilted, frayed-out, second-hand aD-
pearance; but as there is still no death-roll
in connection with the smashes, most of its
luck still sticks to the Department. Some
years ago the Kalgoorlie express, by far the
heaviest passenger train in the State, whilst
standing on a siding, was bashed by a goods
train with all the emphasis born of a 40
mile-an-hour gait; and later the same train,
hankering for the crowded life, careered
across country and pulled up in a railway
dam. On each occasion nothing more serious
than shock and bruises came up for com-
pensation. The latest attempt at notoriety
happened the other night, when a runaway
engine smote a crowded passenger train
which was standing at a station. The
Groper luck was again good, as only two
passengers were injured. One of them was
ETisola, who has dealings with the Devil in
the matter of stage illusions. Still, these
riotous little happenings do some good, inas-
much as they serve to dispel the impression
that W.A.’s trains don’t run fast enough
to catch up to an accident.

APRIL FOLLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ALMA. —Will not see you any more. Good-bye for
ever —JACK. —S. M. Herald, April 1, 1913.
JACK. —Advt. too late. Talk’s cheap after Sun-
day’s smoodging and fooling.—ALMA.
S. M. Herald, April 3 , 1913.
And so they part. The halcyon days of wooing
Draw to a swift and agitated close.
At first I thought that it was Alma’s doing;
It might be Jack’s; who knows?
In Tuesday’s Herald came his valediction:
“Good-bye for ever; will not see you more.
Which left me with the absolute conviction
That Jack was feeling sore.
I, too, had known the species of emotion
Which rent his honest heart before it broke.
Alma, no doubt, misprising his devotion,
Had found another Bloke.
Apparently some interloping Gerald
Had burnt his incense at the self-same shrine,
And Jack had thrown his hand in ( per the Herald,
At eighteen pence a line).
That was the situation, as I read it.
A young romance was ringbarked in the bud ;
Jack’s dignified renouncement did him credit,
And Alma’s name was Mud.
But two days later, on my biassed vision
Burst Alma’s crude, yet spirited retort....
Was Jack, despite his martyr-like decision,
Less injured than I thought?
For Jack had penned his little ad. on Monday,
His previous ardor having calmed and cooled ;
And yet the day before — i.e., on Sunday—
He Smoodged. He even Fooled.
That Sabbath eve his thirsty soul he sated
With all the sweets that dalliance holds in store,
And then sat down next day, and wrote, as stated,
“I will not see you more.”
Jack, it is up to you to go and fix it.
You cannot leave the remedy to Fate.
If there are grounds for Alma’s ipse dixit ,
“Your advert, came too late.”
Tell her your love is still alive and burning;
Ask her to overlook your passing whim ;
Describe it as a pardonable yearning
To pull her tender limb.
Say that your dearest wish has been imperilled
By what you meant for seasonable fun. .. .
Remember, your effusion in the Herald
Appeared on April One.
N.S.'W.

Montenegro, the little public mosquito
which is fighting a horse with one hand and
defying the threatened rear attack of three
elephants with the other, is the most inter-
esting place in Europe just now. It is
about one-seventh the size of Tasmania, yet
it held its own, in a sort of way, against
the Turk even when he was the terror of
Europe, and owned most of Austria and all
Southern Russia, and almost all Northern
Africa, and was sending his gay armies into
Italy with the threat that he would knock
the Pope’s head against the wall of St.
Peter’s. It is so mountainous that enemies
and vegetables fall off it and break their
necks in the plains below. It is barren, and
as poor as two beggars. For some centuries
there wasn’t a single road leading into it:
roads were handy for invaders, and Monte-
negro couldn’t afford to take chances. The
first piano which ever reached this indomi-
table speck of rock was hauled up precipices
by ropes, and carefully investigated at the
top of the first precipice to make sure that
no Turks were hidden inside it. The popu-
lation is—or was, till it was killed while
trying to take Skutari with its teeth—about
250,000, or 64 to the square mile, and
the craving of the people for a fight is
about 640 to the square inch, which is
equal to 60 knots an hour. A quaint, in-
furiated, honorable survival of the Middle
Ages is Montenegro. It may be getting
near its end now, for even the most lone-
some and embittered rock can’t hold out
against modern science, but it has made a
great splash in history for its size.
“Leo Lear” at a seat of learning:—
Geelong Grammar School has re-hatched its founda-
tion stone; G.-G. Denman was master of ceremonies
this time. The blue building at the top of Moora-
bool-street was built in 1857, and on that occasion
Governor Barkly was the show-piece at the “laying.”
Geelong, of late years, has so thrown off its "slum-
ber that the school, once standing in lonelv isola-
tion, is now crowded by the city. Likewise, it is
crowded inwardly by the rush of young Australians
to absorb the wisdom of the ancients and moderns
It was, therefore, forced to shift camp; and after
one false start in the direction of Belmont, it finally
chose a site on the north shore of Corio Bay. Here
a kind of independent community will be established ;
the school will have its own dairy farms, sewerage
system, vegetables and other accoutrements. The
town council has purchased the old building for a
town hall and offices at a cost of £13,500. After a
.prolonged search, the original foundation-stone was
unearthed, and was relaid by the G.-G. The new
buildings and the 200 acres surrounding them are
to cost about £60,000 ; and towards this, £45,000
is in sight, mostly contributed by “old boys” and
friends, with, here and there, a contribution from
an enemy.

The Methody parson can still be a humor-
ist sometimes. Sydney Telegraph gravely
prints that, at a meeting held the other day
to farewell and hearten various of the
brethren who had been passed on to other
circuits, one of the farewellers and hearten-

There is no case on
record of a bedridden
person leading a double
life. —Scrap of medical
philosophy.
In the pious days of
Charles I. a law was
passed in England to
prohibit the murder of
the ox and the mutton
on the blessed Sabbath.
Nobody in Westralia was
much concerned about
the ancient curiosity un-
til the other day when a
butcher had a row with
one of his stipendiary
cow-assassins, and the
latter declined to turn-
to on Sunday. The angry
boss promptly sued him
for breach of contract,
and the stipendiary
assassin as promntly
consulted a lawyer. The
lawyer remembered the
pious statute of Charles
I. and reverently placed
it before the Court with
the remark that, when Westralia
made a start, it took over the whole
boiling of J. Bull’s law, and had for-
gotten to repeal this one. Gibbons,
R.M., was duly impressed, and de-
clared that the ancient law was a
good enough raft for the cow-assas-
sin to escape upon. So the West-
ralian butcher now kills the mutton
on Sundays in fear and trembling, and
the butcher’s customer sniffs his Mon-
day’s meat with suspicion and an
alarmed nose.
Gaud Mayor Hennessey, of Melbourne, the
new broom who looks likely to sweep harder
than his predecessors, talks of importing the
very latest thing in mechanical scavengers.
This is a suction street-cleaner, which has
been working with great success in some of
the dirtiest English cities. It operates, on
the same principle as the suction carpet-
sweeper of domestic life, and should prove as
entertaining to street loafers in Melbourne
as a Druids’ procession. They will be able
to follow the thing around town and watch
it eat up the dust by the way. Presumably
it will also pull the vagrant bits of paper
into its huge belly, in which case the Gaud
Mayor’s new wire rubbish baskets will be
thrown out of action.
The disorganised condition of Victoria’s
Health Department is well illustrated by a
desolate building which stands just outside
the grounds of the Infectious Diseases Hos-
pital at Fairfield. This building was erected
last year for the Health Department as a
sort of Measles Hospital, but it has not yet
been opened or used, patients being still
accommodated at Coode Island Sanitorium,
where the solitary present inmate costs the
State about £6 per week. When the new
building was finished, wire and flock mat-
tresses, Windsor chairs, bedsteads and bun-
dles of bedding were taken out and dumped
on the floor—and there they stay! There is
no fence,_ and stray cows wander along and
poke their horns through the windows, and
dwell on the verandah and lean up against
the walls ; also birds, cats, lizards and other
insects sample the bedding. The Health
chiefs say they erected the hospital in the
expectation that the Infectious Diseases Com-
mittee would take it over and run it; but
the Committe says it couldn’t dream of doing
so until a fence is put up. better ventilation
provided, and various other things done.
Meanwhile John Weary, who has nominal
administrative charge of the Fairfield Hospi-
tal, is deliberately dreaming over the recent
disclosures, and wishing the committee or
the department or somebody would do some-
thing. A most restful person is Weary, but
dreadfully expensive on acocunt of the things
he doesn’t do. As regards the things he
does, he is about as cheap as a vagrant.

“0.K.” : H. H. Champion has, I see, been
telling The Bulletin that its recent re-
sume of the tiresome Peter Andersen case
contained so many misstatements that he
hasn’t time to answer them. Presumably
he is ignorant of the truths he calls “mis-
statements.” In any case, a man who shrieks
“misstatements!” without attempting any
statements of his own in contradiction of
them, is on a par with the oracle who dis-
misses an argument with a contemptuous'
“Pshaw!” The one and only fact which
H.H.C. advances in excuse for the Peter
Andersen agitation is practically a full
endorsement of The Bulletin paragraph
whereat he sneers. The authorities, who
have twice (or oftener) committed poor
Peter to a lunatic asylum, are prepared to
let him out on the understanding that he
will go to a distant country and lose him-
self. Mr. Champion, affecting to treat this
concession as a humorous absurdity, says
“Andersen is a lunatic while in Australia,
but could be certified to be sane if he will
only go away.” The proposal, that seems
screamingly funny to Champion, who hasn’t
got the hang of the case, appears seriously
humane to those who have got the hang
of it. Andersen is a monomaniac. Mad on
the subject of his wife and everything relat-
ing thereto. Deported from the land of his
missis and his married life, he might be sane
enough. As long as he stays within walking
distance of his wife’s abode, he is bound to
be a nuisance.
“ Jeames” on a Point of Morals: —
I see, by the cable, that during her trial at the
Central Criminal Court, London, last week, Mrs.
Pankhurst, the Suffragette, “complained that she
was not allowed to tell the jury of the notdrious
circumstances in which a certain Judge was found
dead some years ago.” The circumstances were
similar to those in which the late Pres.dent Felix
Faure, of France, was found dead some time back,
and every gossip in London knows the Judge’s name
and enough stories about him, true and false, to fill
a directory. The marvel was that the lady should
have thought that a public recital of the long-
deceased gentleman’s moral laches would have had
any effect advantageous to the woman’s suffrage
movement. Her idea, presumably, was to show what
naughty men there were in high positions, and the
advisability of replacing them with virtuous women.
But Britain, for all the sanctimoniousness of its
middle classes, has seldom worried much about the
morals of its prominent men, so long as their brains
were all right. It has had spasms of Wowser frenzy,
but only spasms. The founder of the Marlborough
dukedom sold his charms for money as often and as
openly as many an outcast woman on the streets.
Nelson lived for years, and ultimately died, in
unashamed adultery. Wellington’s morals were de-
scribed as “horrible” by one of his most intimate
friends, and his character was horrible in many
other ways. Practically to the end of his life he
followed the sordid pleasures of the raffish-minded
type of youth of fashion, who comes upon the town,
fresh from school or a University, with money to
burn. The_ nobleman who was selected to guide
Queen Victoria’s political footsteps at the beginning
of her reign, had a reputation of the bluest variety
in the circle in which he moved. And it is the same
to-day, human nature being the one factor that re-
mains unchangeable. Of course, a big section of the

people of the United Kingdom are not in a position
to learn about the failings of their rulers. This ap-
plies to most of those who neither go into Society
nor frequent the great clubs. But even if they did
know it would not seriously trouble them. British
statesmen, administrators, ambassadors, soldiers,
judges and sailors may easily be identified in every
age who did their duty by the State valiantly and
well, while steadily defying a social code which they
found unsuitable to their temperaments. The notion

genius, valor, probity and patriotism of a high and

the minds of elderly females and fools'.
A TOAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A long life, a strong life, and for me a merry one ;
A stake in the right place when Fortune the wheel
has spun ;
A drink when the throat’s dry and one more when
that is done;
A cool head at midnight, a clear eye at morning
sun ;
A hand for your friend’s hand, a hand he need never
shun
In glad days or sad days when man’s errors over-
run ;
A fair time, a rare time, where true happiness is
won ;
A girl’s kiss, a friend’s tear, when they come to
bury one.
N.S.W.
BILLY B.
YOW LEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Chinese company, known as the “Yow Lee,’
regularly consigns Chinese stowaways. labelled
“Goods” to Australia. —Recent press item.
They are sending Goods, are the Great Yow Lee;
They have packed them tight for a trip by sea;
They have cheek-bones high
And a quaint, slant eye—-
These Goods we get from the Big Yow Lee.
In the China boats of the Big Yow Lee
In the bunkers stowed. is a companee ;
In the night it groans,
And it shakes its bones, 1
These Goods we get from the Big Yow Lee.
In the China boats of the Big Yow Ler
No pigtails bear, as of yore, you see;
They seek our land
With a smile so bland,
And a kinsman’s kick from the Big Yow Lee.
The Goods are gathered most carefullee,
And stowed in a hole that a dog would flee.
And the day they land
There’s a welcome grand
For the corpse-like Goods of the Great Yow Lee.
It’s a dreadful weird, that the Goods must dree ;
In a rough, tough skin they must sheltered be ;
For the berth they know
Is a hell below—
These Goods that come from the Big Yow Lee.
From the slimy slums by the old Yang-tze
The Goods are drawn with a greedy glee ;
From the by-ways foul
Where the leprous prowl,
They rake them in, do the Big Yow Lee.
And just as a joke or a sprightly spree,
They dump them down with a license free—-
To twist and broil
In an endless coil,
With a label: “Goods from the Great Yow Lee.”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

“Kodak”: Jack was a real bushboy, and.
when he came down from his native wilds to stay
with us, he was chiefly interested by the tradesmen’s
horses, the cows that grazed on the vacant lots and
the bird-life of the Coast. A strange chirp or bird-
call was sufficient at any time to draw him out .of
the house at a run to recognise the newcomer. His
happiest morning was when one kookaburra told
his friend a joke on the old gum in the yard. One
of the numerous unseen neighbors was old Macpher-
son, who lived in the big house at the back. It
was his habit, in the cool of the evening, to strut
up and down for half an hour playing the bagpipes.
We were so used to it that none of us ever thought
to explain the distressing uproar to young Jack.
We afterwards remembered that the moment the
hidden performance started he would become moodily
attentive and walk restlessly about. But we never
dropped until the night when, unable to bear it any
longer, he blurted out: “By eripes! If I had a bird
that kicked up a rotten row like that one does, I’d
put a charge of shot into it!”
“Polstan”: Someone wants to know why
bees leave home when nothing seems to warrant the
flitting (B. 13/3/’l3). When things are slow and
business slack, bees very often change locality, gen-
erally for the worse, as a bad season invariably
means annihilation for a swarming hive. But when
there are plenty of stores and the hive is in a healthy
condition they don’t evacuate. The unguarded store
of honey that the inquirer found isn’t hard to explain.
People will insist in spraying, with a poisonous
mixture, fruit trees when in full bloom (a useless
proceeding anyhow at that period) and the working
or field bees die in millions as a consequence. In a
couple of days the hive will be empty save for
drones, queen and young bees. These hang on for a
while, but the empty hive grows cold. The unfor-
tunates bunch together in a futile attempt to keep
warm, but daily the ball of bees grows smaller, till
in a week or so they are all dead. Bees require
much warmth, and when the blanket is lost that is
the finish. Or a hive may possess a young queen,
which gets destroyed or lost before she is laying.
The superseding or young queen kills the old one
if the hive hasn’t already attended to this matter,
so if the former dies before she is mated there are
no eggs available from which to raise a fresh queen,
and in the flying season the hive rapidly dies out. Some-
times the old queen is permitted to live on for a while
when a young queen is occupying the hive, but such
instances are not common. In the honey season the
working bees wear out and fall in a few weeks, and
if there is not a good queen on hand to provide
somewhere about 2000 eggs daily, the outlook for the
colony is poor.

“Mary McC.” : I have always suspected
the Australian cow of being a malicious and con-
science-less beast, but in my worst dreams of the
lurking possibilities of the evils which hide themselves
behind her placid front, I never regarded her as the
wholesale murderer she has lately been shown to
be. Practically every drop drawn from her
swelling udder is loaded with poison, and
wittingly or unwittingly she takes her part
in the great congestion conspiracy to kill
off the Australian population in record time.
All evidence points to the fact that the cow
is a walking destruction, a grazing pollution,
a disease-laden curse. She is nearly as in-
jurious as an Asiatic invasion, and carries as
much death in solut'on about with her as a
moderate-sized machine gun could scatter in
a crowded street. Yet nobody is taking her
very seriously. A few mild scientific curses
have been handed out to her in various re-
ports, but the milkman goes his rounds just
the same, and the hearse and flowers are
much as usual. Possibly one or two Govern-
ment Departments in some of the States may,
in a leisurely, half-hearted sort of way, in-
sist that a beast here and there shall be
tried and executed for its crimes, but that is
about the limit to which the machine of
State will go. Yet something drasti
needed, and a Holy War ought to be pro-
claimed throughout the Commonwealth
against the enemy of our peace. What on
earth is the good of giving a baby bonus if
the cow is afterwards given a free hoof to
kill the baby? Let us either do without
cow-milk, or live on the extract of the goat,
she-ass or camel, rather than tolerate the
bovine iniquity. The time is ripe for
slaughter. The hour of vengeance has struck.
Let the murderous cow die in her sins. If
it costs £100,000,000 to finish the job, let
us borrow the money and finish it. The
thing may be cheap at the price. Consump-
tion and the allied diseases are slowly
diminishing; let us take them while they
are on the run, and drive them out once and
for all. The White Plague is a venomous
beast, and the heart of the horror is a cow. We
will hit it hard in the have done with it.
“F.H.R.” : They say you can’t kill a car-
pet snake. But that is rot. You can —with industry
and patience. I was bashing suckers one morning
when I ran on to a pile of snake that put me in
mind of those huge coils of rope you see on an old
sailing ship. I made a chop at it, and sorry I was
next minute, for it was a 10ft. “carpet,” and I had
spoiled the skin. He was cut in two about a yard
from the head, which end made strenuous endeavors
to bite the boots off me, though it took no dislike to
my mate. We left the two pieces about a rod, pole
or perch apart, and didn’t call in again till dinner-
time. Then, to my surprise, the head part had suc-
cessfully swallowed the tail part, tail first, and was
painfully crawling away. I feel almost sure he is
dead now, for I burned him and can see his bones
in the ashes.

“Paspalum”: The man with hayseed in
his whiskers bought a dozen young heifers in Syd-
ney, and when shipping them by a coastal boat in-
sisted thej' should go at “calf” rates. The clerk
said “No! they are cows.” “Cows be blowed!”
says the bushy. “Why, strike me, they haven’t got
their top teeth yet! Look here, and I’ll show you.”
He did. Percy admitted the evidence. The boss of
the office came along. He, too, looked and was con-
vinced they were still calves. What’s more, they
were shipped as calves, and the cow man went away
with a grin which hasn’t worn off yet. He knew
that cows don’t grow top teeth.
* * * *
“Pick”: Professor Stirling, of Adelaide,
who wants to know truly what the wombat has for
dinner, may be recommended to cancel any doubt
he has concerning the little chap’s liking for roots.
The flats adjacent to a wombat’s villa present a
chronic aspect of disrepair from constant scratch-
ing, and any farmer with a field of potatoes, car-
rots or parsnips near a wombat burrow must pro-
tect them with palings or wire netting if he wants
vegetables for his own dinner. All the same, roots
are not the only line on the wombat menu. The
gourmand will smack his lips over young grass or
any green herbage, and when he finds a pumpkin or
melon he reckons he has struck the fleshpots of
Egypt.
* * * *
“Snell” : Business aptitude flourishes amaz-
ingly in the bush. There were five of us, and we
camped under cover of a huge boulder opposite
Nevill’s store, at Mile End. I had many opportuni-
ties of observing the ebb and flow of commerce at
the Mile End General Store. The emporium was
constructed of palings and roofed with shingle. It
had only one window, which gave it a disconcerting,
cock-eyed expression. Through the dust and cobweb
covering the panes, you discovered a bottle of rasp-
berry vinegar, two jam tins with faded labels and a
drowsing cat. There was always a baby falling down
the three steps leading to the store door, which lent
the place an air of perennial activity. When I called
for a loaf of bread, a large, sullen woman, with soap-
suds on her arms, pushed her head into the shop,
and said: “No bread Chewsdees; bread Thirsdies.”
Then she went away. Later on the same day I called
again, in hopes of pickles, and the same woman re-
sponded after I had knocked for seven minutes. She
did not stay. “Don’t I tell yeh no bread Chews-
dees? Bread Thirsdies!” she barked, and went
away. In the afternoon of Wednesday I once more
visited Nevill’s, expecting to be permitted to buy
some jam. Nobody else had visited the Mile End
General Store in the interval. The same woman with
the same soapsuds on her large arms came half-
way out of the living-room at the back, spotted me,
and wailed in profound despa r: “Lor’ blesh me soul!
Can’t you give a body a minute’s peace? Don’t I
tell you no bread Weddnesdee? Bread Thirsdee!”
I did not get the jam.

ence clerk. I am proud of this sample:—

say to you to send not the goods that I get before
last week. One dozen pair good braces. Two dozen
cashmere socks. I meet Bagsheek last week and two

Bagsheek not to belt me every time he meet me.
The money order I send you for the goods. He belt
me with the big stick in his cart he’s carrying it to

THE CLOVEN “OOF.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Australia, which has won high place in the
arena of sport, is, it seems, holding its own also in
the. humbler blit more useful sphere of dairying’.
Giving evidence before the Food Commissioner (Mr.
Bavin) yesterday, a dairyman complained of the
slowness of immigrants who were tried at milking.
“There is no comparison between them and the
Australians,’ said he. — Nevis item.
They’ve ta’en the cricket ashes —and, Lord! that
was bad enough ;
lor nothing here on earth could well be worse.
And they decked our new-fledged champions with
the Order of the Muff;
And then, as if to ramify the curse,
They came and squelched us at the courts (the
tennis courts, I mean) —
Just how twas done I disremember now;
But thank the gods of glory—all that ever yet have
been—
Our prestige is unsullied near the Cow !
We saw our sculling triumphs turn to ash and Dead
Sea fruit—
Strange fruit that was as aloes in the mouth.
Vi e saw our pride and glory lost in sorrowful pur-
suit,
And a drooping tail a-trailing in the south.
It was deep humiliation—it was terrible to bear ;
It was hard to tear the laurel from our brow ;
But a gleam of golden sunshine lights the darkness
of despair ;
For, after all, could Barry milk a cowt
They’ve plugged our pugs; our whitest hopes were
but as tallow glims
That flickered out before the storm of bash.
They fell like vicious Vandals on our stalwart Bills
and Jims
(And, in-ci-dental-ly, upon our cash).
But what do bruises matter, or a pug’s dismantled
jaw?
Before the fist of Fate we’ll meekly bow,
Since we’re the greatest milkers that the wide
world ever saw. ...
Hail, Australia, Master—Mistress of the Cow !
N.S.W

“Ginger”: The Mulga and the Spinifex
are White, but the mere Outback ought to be
ashamed of itself. Take Riverina for a sample. The
White Australia sentiment is as dead there as last
year’s pumpkin-vine. Outside motor-car circles,
Chow cooks, Chow shopkeepers and Chow gardeners
are the most opulent and haughty citizens. Right
here in Hay are miscellaneous broods of half-caste
Chows, half-caste darkies,' half-caste Hindoos. You
can find them all living under the one roof sometimes.
In Narrandera lately I saw Binghi swop Black Mary
to a white rabbiter for a sheep dog. A couple of miles
further on the rabbiter >got out of the trap to open
a gate and discovered the dog under the trap—he had
broken away from Binghi. I don’t know one
affluent Quong in Riverina who isn’4 married (more
or less) to a White Australian girl. About 12
months ago, in Cheltenham (Vic.) I hiked into a
Chow picnic, and Saw some of the best-looking women
a man would see in Melbourne. No ; don’t trust to
Mary to keep the race white. That is a job that
must be seen to by plain Bill.
* * * •
“Vergniaud”: Ten years ago, while fish-
ing in a big hole in the Namoi, above Manilla
(N.S.W.), my line snagged and I lost the sinker.
I improvised one from a snail padlock I had in my
pocket, baited with a crayfish, and threw in. As the
line whistled through the air bait and sinker got
tangled together. Before I had time to pull the
mixture out, something like a medium-sized shark
became attached. After half-an-hour’s playing I
thought I had landed a cod of 401 b. or so, when the
brute gave a 5 h.p. kick and was back in the water
again. Lately I was riding along the river bank 15
miles lower down, when I came across a group that
had just caught a fish which I reckoned went well
over half a hundredweight. They opened him up
and were surprised to find that his innards contained
a closed padlock. I bet the crowd an even quid
that a key, which I produced, would open the con-
cern. It did, and I won. You’ll probably not
believe this plain, unvarnished tale; but I can intro-
duce you to the girl we bought the subsequent
drinks from.

“Unumgar”: “D.R.Y.’s question, “What
makes a herd of bees leave its home without appar-
ent reason"? (B. 13/3/’l3) “D.R.Y.” found the
place full of honey ; yet the owners had cleared out.
Of course they had. Having done their duty to a
future generation, they had left to found a new
home and start another family going. Every swarm
of bees in the bush, if left to itself, will fill the
hollow or nest with food, and make a warm bed
for each little bee previously placed there by the
queen, 'then exit. Curtain.
