“Jimmie Pannikin” : I agree entirely with
“Cavalryman,” that the Australian cannot ride, and
is not a horseman in the best sense of the word. I
spent three out-o’-doors, observant, optimistic years
Outback and Further Out, and I studied equine edu-
cation very closely, because I have always been a
horse-lover. I found that the further Outback I
got, the more the equine educational methods de-
teriorated. I used to think that the ability to ride
the saltbush cyclones of buckjumpers, which I often
saw West-o’-West, was proof of horsemanship and
hardihood. After about two years of patient and
persistent study, I
learnt that any fool
of a monkey-like
man can stick to the
best of the worst
jumpers without
being a horseman.
The sand and salt-
bush spaces are
stuffed full with poor
horsemen, and most
of the Far Out rough
riders are really
horse - murderers.
That’s why horses
are becoming a di-
minishing Australian
asset. Thousands of
valuable mokes are
killed every year by
these well - named
horse-breakers. I n
fact, Australians are
the worst horsemen
probably in the
world. They can’t
ride for sour apples;
neither can th ey
swim or lie. The
Federal Government
should import a few
thousand riding masters to teach our lean, wiry,
bronzed and brutal bushmen of Outback how to
ride, drive, nr'nd and train horses. I suppose we
could easily get them from the London or Manchester
prairies, or the tangled forests of Birmingham.
“Billy B.” : How far can ants smell tucker ?
I know. for a fact they can get on to it from a
distance of about 40 yards, and this is how I know.
I was working at the desk in the front room of the
house the landlord allows me to occupy at a robber’s
rental. Before lunch I ate an apple and tossed the
core into the waste-paper basket by my side. At
afternoon-tea time there was a commotion in the
family on the discovery of a procession of millions
of ants, which led from a colony of the pests at the
end of the garden, along the yard, up the back
steps, through the dining-room and hall, round to
my room and into the waste-paper basket where the
core of the apple was black with them. I reckon
it would be extremely unpleasant to stick a nose as
sensitive as that into some of the Ma State’s spend-
ing departments at present.
“S.H.”: On going to our local butcher's
shop the other day, I saw a fair-sized spring cart
standing in the road. It was piled up with all
sorts of heavy-looking articles, but./.there was no
horse between the shafts. A neat middle-aged
woman stood beside the caravan waiting for her
elderly husband, who had gone inside to buy meat.
Husband and wife had travelled all the way from
Tingha, a distance of about 400 miles, pulling their
belongings with them, and seemed none the worse
for the journey. Still, it was a curious vehicle to
find on the Parramatta-road among the smart motor-
cars which infest that dusty thoroughfare.
“Gouger” : Frightful is the position of the
poor devil maimed in the bush and setting down to
die, knowing that help is impossible, the nearest
white man’s camp being, perhaps, 100 miles away.
In most cases, where a lone skeleton has been found
months afterwards, something has been found to
show that the poor wretch “died game.” On Rotham
Park station, in the Gulf country, a black boy left
behind a mute testimony of this sort lately. While
mustering his horse bolted, and the boy disappeared.
The. whole station crew turned out, but after a
fortnight’s constant search all hope of ever seeing
the boy again was given up. About two months
later a party accidentally came across what was left
of him, near the bank of a gully, barely 200 yards
from where the searchers had passed day after day.
Roth legs were broken, and the poor young devil,
with death creeping upon him as the days passed,
had taken off his shirt and stuffed it with grass, that
the end might, at least, be as comfortable as pos-
sible.
ft * * *
“Gouger” : A hardy group of men are the
sandalwood-getters. On York Peninsula the ordinary-
looking, dark-barked, light-green-leafed oily tree
grows only in the forest country, around the coast
and far inland. With a good pack team the col-
lector makes his way to his particular district,
choosing a spot where there is a tribe of blacks
camped handy. To these he distributes rations and
a tomahawk a man, and each buck returning from
hunting brings a sandalwood stick into camp. The
stack soon mounts up, especially if there happen to
be a hundred or more bucks on the job. The sticks
are cut into 3ft. 6in. lengths, barked and packed to
the coast. Thence the cargo is taken to Thursday
Island in schooners, to await final shipping, mostly
to China. At £25 a ton, the man who strikes
quickly a patch of sandalwood does mighty well.
Some gather the vegetable on contract, charging a
couple of pounds a ton less than market price on
the coast. Others have their own cutters, and go the
whole procession.
“Polstan” : When “8.J.5.” asserts that a
squadron of Australian Light-Horse could lose the
crackest cavalry regiment in Europe he isn’t far out.
I’ve spent 16 years in the Mounted Rifles and Light
Horse, and during that time have dropped on some
remarkable samples of horse-mastership by whole
squadrons at a time. I’ve seen raw horses served
out to a squadron, and straight away these animals
have been put through drill movements with a skill
and dash little short of marvellous. A well-known
staff officer of Light Horse, who saw service in In-
dia, told me that he has never seen; this sort of
thing done outside Australia l —-no one would ever
dream of serving out an untrained horse to a Bri-
tish cavalryman. There are Light Horsemen at
Narranda (Vic.) who will run down a scrub kan-
garoo in the thick bush in ten minutes, in great
style, too. If “Cavalryman” (B. 20/2,/’l3) wants
to find out just how much Australians can ride let
him take a sprint with the Light Horse at the next
encampment. He’ll want all the nerve he can mus-
ter. A L'ght Horseman rarely takes a trained horse
to camp, though he invariably brings one back with
him. He holds to the old Mounted Rifle maxim
that “camp’s th’ place to quiet th’ blanks,” and as
a consequence whole truck-loads of snorting, rolling-
eyed “young ’uns” go annually into camp with the
regiment to receive their education.
“Umfaan”: The Federal trawler fossick-
ing at a depth of 1000-odd feet in the Australian
Bight, raked up 13 finny beasts of great edibility
and tonnage. One was only 3in. short of a fathom
in length and was 4ft. round the equator. It had
needle-like teeth, which interlocked as closely as
the jaws of a ticket punch. The head was shaped
like a torpedo, and possessed large, goggle-shaped
eyes 2in. wide. Weight, over 401 b. Being deep-
water fish, they turned turtle on the surface and
went off pop. The trawler claims the species as
new r , and threatens it with a name.

“The Seedling - ’: Re strawberry clover (B.
13/3/T3, etc.). It didn't get into Victoria as packing
for merchandise. The vegetable was introduced to
C!ow Band by the late Black, of Tarwin Meadows,
South Gippsland. During a visit to Switzerland he
noticed a creeping clover. He liked the look of it,
and promptly secured the end that grows the creeps
and sent it to Gippsland. The Black boys planted
it on the flats of the Tarwin River near the South
Coast. In a few years it covered miles,, of country,
and has done a bit towards putting a comfortable
lining in a few pockets.
“Wal”: Agree with “Sunflower” (B.
27 /2 / ’ 13) about the comparatively little-known
beauty of Papua's flora. Representatives of all
climates are to be found in the Possession. At sea
level the botanist is in a paradise of tropical vegeta-
tion —vines, creepers, palms, orchids, parasitic
growths of countless variety, and gigantic timber
trees. Incidentally, many of these trees will be of
much value in future years when the settlers awaken
to the knowledge of the wealth of their forests.
Climbing steadily upwards, the observer passes
through zones of almost every temperature, each
with its own peculiar flora, until, at Mount Vic-
toria, the highest point in British Papua is reached.
Here, at a height of nearly 14,000 ft., the “Old
World” flowers, such as buttercups, daisies and cow-
slips, abound. The cataloguing of all specimens
would be a stupendous job, about equal to the
enumeration of the many-colored sins of the joyous
cities of Sheol.
“Eddyscrj”: Wellin, Wellin and Davis
were driving on a small coal seam in a gorge at
Dry Spur, Gippsland ; and after five weeks of it
Davis went abroad on a Sunday afternoon, seeking
some break in the awful monotony of Wellin and
Wellin. Henry Wellin wouldn’t speak, and Wesley
Wellin couldn’t hear. Davis longed for a human
creature with some garrulousness and a sound tym-
panum, and sought the big bark hut out on the flat
land towards Bullfoot. The hut door was open, and
when Davis appeared au it a man inside spilt a dish
of spuds, and a large, sour-looking gohanna, that
had been drowsing where the sunlight fell within
the hut, sailed out, and nearly batted Davis off his
feet, flirting between his legs, before sliding up a
gum. The hairy man inside stood among his spilt
vegetables, “garpin’ ” at the stranger within his
gates. “My name’s Davis,” said the visitor. “I’m
one of a party prospecting coal up the gorge.
Thought I’d drop in on you for a neighborly pitch.”
“Oh,” said the hut-holder, and he picked up a
spud. “Name iv Cadden,” he added after two
minutes, picking uf> another spud. Davis sat on his
heels in the doorway, and tried for half an hour to
maintain a conversation, during which time Cadden
spoke three more words, and picked up three more
potatoes. At this stage the miner was constrained to
admit that his mission was a failure. He arose to
return to Wellin and Wellin. “I’m afraid you’re
not over-fond of society,” said Davis. Cadden took
up another spud, and examined it closely. “Oh, I
dunno,” he said. “I s’pose I’m ez great a bloke
fer company as you’d meet anywhere. There’s
Peter. I’m ’most always with Peter.” He pointed
to Peter. Peter was the gohanna

POT LUCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The isle of Palamand was gay ;
Its one red shirt flew on a pole.
A mission man had come that way
To save the island’s precious soul.
He was a gentle, chubby chap
Whose knees were hidden by his lap.
The tall, proud chief of Palamand
Beheld the parson with disdain.
The former sported feathers grand;
His hair was like a charger’s main ;
The pastor, who was far from tall,
Had scarcely any liair at all.
“Our hunting has been bad this week,”
The chief explained ; “and fish are few.”
“Of heavy feasting do not speak,”
The pastor said, “I beg of you :
“I’ll take pot luck.” They raised the lid.
It’s needless p’raps to say he did.
VICTORIA.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Major P. W. Fallon, lately made a lieut.-
col. and given command of the Irish Rifles
(Sydney), belongs to the well-known family
of Albury pioneers who for half a century
have been in the wine-growing business. The
Colonel was a solicitor to start with, but
on the death of P. E. Fallon threw up Costs
and took to the management of the family
vats. His predecessors in the colonelcy of
the Irish Rifles have been Hubert Murray,
now Lieut.-Gov. of Papua; F. B. Freehill,
the solicitor who died ; and J. B. Nash, who
mixes medicine and politics with his mili-
tary enthusiasm.
DR. ANGAS JOHNSON , OF
ADELAIDE.
After reading Alfred Deakin’s great speech
at the beginning of the Federal electoral
campaign, The Bulletin becomes more and
more convinced that Alfred is the re-incar-
nation of Pompey. Pompey was the most
perfect Roman gentleman _of his time. He
was tall and good-looking and affable, and
he wore his toga at the correct angle, and
nobody who met him could help liking him.
He was brave and conscientious. His in-
tentions were of the best, and he never of
set purpose did a bad turn to anybody. And
he only missed the ’bus—the old Roman ’bus
—by half an inch. But he missed the ’bus.
The ruins of the ’bus that he didn’t catch
are scattered all over Macedonia to this day.
Mr. King O’Malley (in his capacious hat)
for the 500th time : “I am Minister for the
Crown!” A piping voice from the back:
“Who’s Minister for the Brim?”
Adelaide Racing Club has lost an ex-chair-
man, an occasional owner, and a keen sup-
porter. in J. R. Anderson, K.C., just dead of
cancer, at 48. He was a steward of Oak-
bank and the Hunt Club, too; and was as
enthusiastic over football as he was about
racing.
A mostly-imported monument, provided by
bis friends, has been put over the grave of
that good Protectionist, ex-Treasurer J. R.
Dacey, who wore himself to a premature
death in the earlier and less profligate days
of the N.S.W. Labor Government. Dace.y’s
remains lie in the Yarra cemetery, and Pre-
mier McGowen will make some remarks at
the unveiling of the stone next Sunday,
April 20.
The Australian Government has, it seems,
appointed an official chaplain; anyway,
Saturday’s S. M. Herald announced that
Prime Minister Fisher and Josiah Thomas,
Minister for Eternal Affairs, were doing
something or other. Without going outside
the present Cabinet, it is probably as good a
choice as could have been made, for Thomas
is a local preacher of great eminence among
the Methodists.
Lord Dudley’s disappearance from public
life tells its own tale. He was firstly Lieu-
tenant-General of Ireland. Then he was
Gov.-General of Australia. Now he has sub-
sided into himself, and is just Lord Dudley
again. Not even Duke of Dudley. King
Edward’s death was a bitter blow to him.
He came to Australia on the understanding
that his genial old friend was going to pay
a health visit to these Labor-blighted shores.
The King died before he could fulfil his end
of the contract, and with him died Dudley’s
hope of a “step.” He never tried, guberna-
torially speaking, again.
The late Henry Wrixon achieved his most
notable act of Toryism when he was Attor-
ney-General in the lamentable Gillies-Deakin
Ministry, 1886-1890. Bailey, of Sydney,
went to Melbourne with the loudly-pro-
claimed idea of starting a local Sunday news-
paper. Whereupon Wrixon, viewing Sunday
journals as the Devil’s organs of democracy,
rushed a Bill through the Vic. Leg. Assembly
prohibiting their publication within the
State. Needless to say, the House of Fat
did not reject that Bill. When a Labor
Government gets going in Victoria its first
care, if it is wise, will be to repeal the
W rixon law.
From The Bulletin’s Oldest inhabitant:
Died last week, William Condie Renwick, a
brother of the late Dr. Arthur Renwick, and an old
Sydney commercial identity. The father was a
master plasterer, who gave his sons trades. Arthur,
afterwards a knighted M.D., served an apprentice-
ship to carpentry, and worked at the trade until an
accident made it difficult for him to carp any
longer, and this circumstance led him to study medi-
cine. The other son was a draper, who, after he had
learned the business, started on his own account in
a small shop in Pitt-street, south of the original
premises of Farmer and Co., and long since absorbed
by that firm. Later he moved into King-street,
occupying part of the block now known as Waters’s
Corner. Here he remained until 1888, when Mrs.
McCathie, who had been managing the business of
H. W. Webb, a neighboring draper, succeeded him.
Mr. Renwick then went on the land at Richmond
River, and resided there for a quarter of a century.
In his old drapery days he was at one time Chairman
of the Great Cobar Copper Co. —the neople who sold
out to the syndicate that made all the money.
Up to the time of Governor Strickland’s
departure, Westralia had had 36 Governors
and Acting-Governors. The first was Cap-
tain James Stirling, lI.N. lie hung out as
Lieut.-Governor from the end of 1828 to
September, 1832. Captains were popular
thenabouts. Stirling was followed in turn
by Captain F. C. Irwin, Captain Richard
Daniell, Captain Picton Beete, then Daniell
again, ditto Stirling again, this-, time as
straight-out Governor, as distinct from
Lieut.-Governor. Stirling had the longest
run of any of them, holding office in two
terms for about nine years. Captain Fitz-
gerald, Mr. John Hutt and Mr. Arthur Ed-
ward Kennedy each held the job down for
about seven years. Lieutenant-Colonel
Clarke had the shortest experience of For-
restralia. He lasted from January, 1846,
to February, 1847.
J. R. Anderson, K.C., who died the other
day in Adelaide, had the unusual experience
of having been Attorney-General without a
seat in either House. Butler, in 1905, want-
ing a law Minister badly, gave the job to
Anderson; and he held it for nearly five
months —until a new Premier (Price) wined
the whole lot out. The whole lot at that
time consisted of four, for in those days a
few of the States really were trying to
economise in view of the transfer of so many
of their functions to the Commonwealth. In-
cidentally, Queensland began with a Minis-
try of two, Premier Herbert and Attorney-
General Pring; but five days later a Trea-
surer was added. It is said that the Gov-
ernment started with 7d.. so at first it
didn’t need a Treasurer. Richard Dry ran
Tasmania for nearly three years with a
Ministry of three, and as Dry dropped out
three days before his colleagues, the Minis-
try at last got down to two. Neil Lewis ran
the same State with four Ministers. At
the other end of the scale, Tommy Bent took
almost his whole partv into his Cabinet. He
had eight honorary Ministers and quite a
heap of salaried ones. To be a Victorian
Minister in Bent’s time was as common as
to be named Smith.
“Melb”: The Bulletin (10/4/’l3) men-
tions the yarn that ex-Judge Casey was a
policeman in America —or it may have been
a gaol warder. There was another story
that in such capacity he locked up the lanky
theatrical Baronet Don, for debt, and Don
snubbed him for it when they met in Ben-
digo. Casey was managing the local Ad-
vertiser at the time, with Mackay as edi-
tor. Mackay’s Rob Roy and Mclntyre’s
Bailie Nicol Jarvie, with the amateurs,
were famous; and Casey was equally good
as Paddy Murphy, in “The Happy Man,”
with the song of “Och, Brian O’Linn had no
Breeches to Wear.” But in politics the
little fat man had rather a bad style—too
much humility, somewhat Tartuffian, greasy
and apt to be rancid. Later, on the bench,
he improved wonderfully, and developed
quite a lot of dry dignity.
“Vic.’s” send-off: —
11. J. Wrixon has gone. He married the widow
Anderson, Money Miller’s daughter, 41 years ago,
and the money choked him. Still, he did one grand
thing for Australia. In the case of Ah Toy v. Mus-
grove (Victorian Collector of Customs) a majority
of the Victorian Full Bench, with Chief Justice
Higinbotham and Judge Kerferd dissenting, decided
that Chinese Immigration could not be completely
blocked. There was a successful appeal to the Privy
Council, where Wrixon pleaded the case as Attorney-
General. Wrixon ought to have been Speaker when
Bent got the billet; but the candidature of J. G.
Duffy spoiled him. At another time he seemed posi-
tively to be the leading candidate for Chief Justice-
ship, Premiership, or Speakership, whichever he cared
to go for; yet he obtained none of them. He always
failed at the critical time. When Victoria was in
its great financial crisis, the men to whom the public
turned were Deakin and Wrixon. Both shirked the
job ; and Turner, the Unknown, took it uu and made
a success of it. In every capacity he lacked bite,
edge, impetus, momentum. Yet he was never un-
stable —he was a Gentle Tory all the time. When
he dropped out of politics he wrote several awfully
stuffy books; but Money Miller’s son-in-law didn’t
care whether anybody bought them.
SCHULER , Editor of Melbourne AGE.
Samuel Way, of South Aus., the oldest
Judge in the Australian Commonwealth, was
77 on Friday last.
The widow of Chief Justice Darley, of
N. S. Wales, who died in London recently,
was a daughter of Captain Sylvester Browne,
of the mercantile marine, who settled in
Sydney in the ’3o’s. This Browne was an
enterprising man who had a business house
in Macquarie-place, and a country residence
in the bush that now forms part of the
inner suburb of Enmore. In or about 1840
he chartered a brig, and took his family and
household belongings to Port Phillip, where
he finally settled. Two of his sons are T. A.
Browne (“Rolf Boldrewood”) and Sylvester
Browne, well known as a mining and sport-
ing man. There are three daughters still living
—Mrs. Cockshott, Mrs. Massie and the
widow of Peter Scratchley, an eminent
engineer.
Lx-Judge Casey, who died in Victoria the
other day, reached the bar via Parliament.
A good many men have done the same thing
since then—including Commonwealth Attor-
ney-General Hughes and N.S.W. Attorney-
General Holman—but Casey led the way in
Australia. He was one of the earliest
colonial” barristers admitted in Victoria.
In those days nearly all the gowns came
from London and Dublin, and were ad-
mitted in Victoria because of their admis-
sion “at Home.” They had not to undergo
any examination. The “colonial” men were
few, though the examination wasn’t hard.
But the attorneys didn’t run much for
the colonial” men. Everything “colonial,”
whether jam, boots and shoes or barristers
was gently sneered at. Politics, however!
helped “King” Casey, and he got a practice
but never a big one. He seemed to take a
bigger .man —George Higinbotham—as his
model; but there was some distance between
them.
H. V. McKAY, of Sunshine Harvester repute,
the Liberal candidate for Ballarat.
Hubert Saumarez Smith, son of the late
“Hob Cab” Primate, resigns his clerical secre-
taryship. and starts for England by the War-
rimoo. The young man is scholastic in taste
and temper, and during his time in Aus-
tralia kept out of the sectarian stadium —a
virtue that can also be put to the credit of
his otherwise-undistinguished father.
A Few Words from the Bar Table: J. M.
Harvey, the latest N. S. Wales Equity
Judge, should do well —as well as Street, to
whose appointment his bears a close analogy.
He knows his job thoroughly, is young and
earnest, and possesses sound health. But
Adrian Knox would have been a better man
for the billet, and Leverrier the best pos-
sible. Why the Government passed over
Leverrier is one of the leading mysteries of
Phillip-street and the pubs adjacent thereto,
at the present time. He is the best scien-
tific and patents lawyer in Australia. Had
he been selected a vast store of invaluable
knowledge, which no other barrister pos-
sesses, would have been placed at the dis-
posal of the State. And the State badly
needs some expert in this line on its
Supreme Court Bench. It would have been
a graceful act on Knox’s part to accept the
Judgeship that is alleged to have
been offered him. Incidentally. he
would have obviated a good deal of
forensic blasphemy by so doing. Many a
hard-up advocate visioned himself the richer
for Knox’s translation to a non-competitive
sphere. Now the chances are be will go on
monopolising all the best work for ever, or
therabouts, since he is a mere youth still as
lawyers go. By the way, the Labor Ad-
ministrations, both in the Federal arena and
in N. S. Wales, have been singularly mal-
adroit in their handling of judicial appoint-
ments. No one should be given an oppor-
tunity to formally ‘refuse” a High Court
or Supreme Court Judgeship. The old, and
more seemly, system was for the Government
of the day to get one of the possible Judge’s
friends to sound him. He took the possible
appointee into a pub, and asked him softly
if he would like to drink a steady and dig-
nified job. If the report was even
doubtful no offer was forthcoming, and
the State was saved from, looking like a
bagman casting round as to where he might
place his goods.

TWO POOR MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pierpont Morgan died of acute indigestion. For a
long time he had been unable to take any kind of
nourishment. He left one hundred million dollars.
-—Horrible public information.
The millionaire was white and sad.
He wore a thousand pounds in fur.
A tattered pauper, thinly clad,
Arose beside his car. He had
A stomach like a sepulchre,
So hollow ’twas. He, too, was pale.
His cracked voice piped a touching tale.
“A copper, sir; one penny, please!
I’ve scarcely tasted for a week.
The inward cravings never ceare.
A penny, please, to give release
For one day from the rats that tweak
My entrails with unceasing stress.
Rich man, relieve my emptiness!’’
The mill’onaire was white and sad ;
He wore a thousand pounds in fur,
In richest linen he was clad.
With twenty million pounds he had
A stomach like a sepulchre ;
And to the pauper thin and pale
The rich man piped a touching tale.
“Relief, relief; oh, give me peace!
I have not supped through one long moon.
From inward torments no release
Has come to me. They never cease,
My agonies. For that great boon,
The hearty stomach of a clown,
I’ll give you twenty millions down!”
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Greater J. D. Williams Amuse-
ment Co. (Sydney) :
Towards Re-
duction of
Dec., Profit. Dividends. Deficit. Deficit.
1910 .. - £54,694
1911 ..£33,210 p.c. = £23,503 £9707 44.987
Goodwill added (net), £11,523 56,510
1912 .. 13,471 7$ p.c. = 13,538 56,577
This has been the most-talked-of co. of the year, and
its shares have had more tribulations than any others
on the market. The business has suffered from a
mixture of hot air and water. It started with hot
air. J. D. Williams, a Yankee showman, turned up
in this country, and decided that it was “easy.” He
hustled, and formed himself into a co. Of course
there wasn’t much real capital in that co. —it was
mostly hot air and water. Next, partly in order to
put in some more water, the first co. was formed
into this Greater J. D. Williams Co. Twelve months
ago, when the first accounts were issued, the capital
subscribed and paid was £148,281 in £1 shares, of
which £53,700 represented the hot-air and water
before mentioned, plus £994 preliminary exes., mak-
ing the total deficit £54,694, with which the table
starts. But they hadn’t exhausted the hot-air reser-
voir yet. The Crystal Palace was projected in Syd-
ney. and presently the co. paid £lO,OOO for the good-
will of that —for the brilliant idea, in short, of a
kind of glorified Paddy’s Market. Also £5523 was
paid by way of “initial and opening exes.” of the
Crystal Palace and the Britannia Theatre (Melb.) ;
buit as against that there is a reserve of £4OOO, repre-
senting, apparently, unrealised profit on “shares in
other cos.” These shares figure as an asset for
£4050. The co. or cos. are not mentioned. The
investment may have been in Bud Atkinson’s busted
circus for all this paper knows. Williams, or
the Williams Co., certainly had something to do with
that concern.
Here are the assets:—
Melba Theatre (Melb.) . . . . £33,215
Leaseholds . . .. .. •• 51,876
Cash . . . . .. . . 8,041
£93 132
Less liabilities .. .. .. 61,912
-£31.220
Films, merchandise, etc. .. .. .. 39,159
Plant, furniture, etc. . . . . . . 28,182
Book debts, pre-payments, etc. .. . . 17,078
Shares in other cos. . . . . .. • • 4,050
Exclusive picture rights .. .. .. 4,740
Goodwill items .. . ■ • • • • 69,223
Total assets .. .. ..£193,652
These represent—Capital. £180,506; and alleged re-
serves, £13,146.
The investor wants to know two things in particu-
lar —(1) “What is the security for my money?”
(2) “What rate of interest am I going to get?” As
to the security: on a breaking-up basis it is pretty
certain that in the Greater J. D. Williams Co. there
wouldn’t be many shillings in the £. The freeholds
and leaseholds are in at cost (less a small amortiza-
tion allowance in the case of the leaseholds). The
cost was no doubt excessive; and the premises are of
little value except for their special purpose. Investi-
gations show quite conclusively that, with exceptions
perhaps, films, merchandise, plant and furniture were
badly bought. The accounts are signed only by the
auditors, who take no responsibility for tbe valua-
tions. The assets are. however, worth more
than break-up value if the co. can use them.
And this depends on whether the co. can
make money. That it made anything like
the profits it is alleged to have made in the first year
is unlikely. It will be noted that the dividend paid
for the first half of the second year absorbed more
than the net Drofit for the whole year. No doubt
profit sufficient to cover the dividend was shown in
the first half-year, which means that there was no
profit at all for the second half, or, if there was a
profit, it was all absorbed in writing down assets
that had previously been over-valued. Again and
again it has been pointed out here that disclosed profit
depends upon the valuation of assets. Here is a
picture film ’ rich cost £lOO, and stands at that
figure in the books. Honestly it is now worth £5.
If it is valued at £lOO the profits may be (say)
£lO,OOO. If, however, it is valued at the honest
£5, the profits come down to £9905. Multiplv
that film by 100, and the important bearing which
valuation has on the profits is obvious. The 1911
valuation of films was made bv two people in the
co.’s service, who have gone. This paper declines to
take their valuation. It is not sacrilege to refuse
to accept the present valuations either. The two
published statements are therefore in all probability
utterlv valueless for the purpose of showing whether
a profit can be made by the co. All they do is this:
they show a very fine income. Everv investigation
shows that there was prodigious waste. You can
pick up stories of mismanagement and extra-
vagance anywhere, and a lot of the stories
are true. Put the two things together—
the undoubted revenue and the equally un-
questionable waste, and the worse you make out
the past mismanagement to have been the better
hope you give the co. for the future. From the
point of view of the investor who likes his sleep, the
thing is of course impossible. But the speculator
who is impressed with the new management and
thinks there is a future in the picture business will
see points in it.
The financial side of the “big” picture business
in the eastern States is suffering a complete change.
There were four big cos. at work —-Amalgamated
Pictures., Ltd., better known as Tait’s; West’s,
Spencer’s and the Williams Co. These people have
been competing against one another for films. The
Taits got tired, and, at their instance, an amalgama-
tion was effected: the General Film Co. of Aus-
tralasia, Ltd., was formed to take over the business
of the Amalgamated Pictures, Ltd., and the Spencer
and West cos., and to purchase the film businesses of
Paths Frgres, Harrington’s and Gaumont’s in Aus-
tralia. The J. D. Williams Co. stood out for a
while; but subsequently it went in on an agreement
to form two cos.—one a co. to hire out films; the
other a co. to exhibit films. In the former the
Williams Co. takes 75,000 shares and the other three
split up 225,000 among them. Their proportions
are now being fixed by the accountants. In the
exhibiting co. the J. D. Williams Co. takes 120,000
shares and the others 180,000 shares. Why two
cos. should have been formed isn’t clear; it seems
to quite unnecessarily complicate matters. These
cos. take over the whole of the goodwill of the
businesses; and the existing cos. will hand their
theatres, etc., over to the combine, the latter holding
them on lease and paying rent. For the stock-in-
trade and plant 6 p.c. debentures will be issued by
the combine. The arrangement runs almost on all-
fours with the brewery combine in Melbourne. Thus
for the future the Greater .1. D. Williams Co. (like
the others) will have onlv three sources of revenue—
(l) dividends from the combine; (2) rent of
premises leased by the combine; (3) interest from
stray investments. The theatres will be managed
by the Combine’s Board of Directors, who, it is
understood, will, as far as possible, retain the
present managers in local control. How the public
will fare has yet to be seen: the scheme certainly
looks all right from the companies’ point of view.
And by reason of the better buying that will be
possible, it would look all right even if the puhlic
got no worse treatment in the future than it has had
in the past.

The co. was registered in 1904 with a paid capital
of £40,008, to take over an old-established business.
Of this amount, £30,000 was given in shares for the
goodwill (£15,000) and assets; a-nd £lO,OOO was
subscribed for “B” shares carrying certain rights
which have now expired. All shares therefore now
rank alike. With the rapid extension of the rubber
business and the development of new lines by this
co., including the manufacture of motor tyres, fur- •
ther capital has been called for. The next list shows
the growth of that item and the movement of others:
1907. 1910. 1911. 1912.

Thus the co. needs £60,117 more to trade on
than it did 12 months ago; and of that
amount £42,000 has come from new capital, £6878
from premium capital and £11,239 from the public
(increased liabilities). Of the total amount, £34,000
has gone into stock, about £ll,OOO into
plant, £6OOO into premises and £BOOO into book
debts —-all movements which naturally result from the
manufacture of new lines, the result of the Pro-
tectionist policy which so annoys that quaint combi-
nation, Attorney-General Billy Hughes and the im-
porter. Why it should annoy the importer is plain
enough; but it is a mystery why it should annoy
.Billy Hughes, who professes to be so much con-
cerned about the defence of Australia. Apparently,
Billy Hughes has an idea that Australia can be best
defended by the workers in the rubber and other
factories of France, America and England. A par-
ticularly cheerful view of the situation, so far as the
Perdriau Rubber Co. is concerned, is that it has
financed its trade extensions so very largely on its
own money. It can’t ask for its own money back ;
but when a co. works on somebody else’s capital,
there is always a danger of the bank manager be-
coming less affable at the most inconvenient time,
which means realisation sales and the sacrifice of
something.
With £49,226 in stock and £41,627 in plant,
it is obvious that the amount of profit claimed by
the co. depends largely on the valuation of the one
and the depreciation of the other. So far as the pub-
lished accounts show, nothing is charged direct
against gross profits to cover wear and tear : £2500
is, however, transferred to machinery and plant re-
serve, which is now £7851 in credit. If this is the
only sum charged against gross profits to pay for the
depreciation of machinery, then the true profit last
year was much less than £15,397, and the true re-
serves are much less than £9526. It seems in-
credible that nothing should be charged direct; and
though the thing could be done without disclosure
(renewals, for instance, and even additions might be
charged to the unpublished working account), there
is no trace of it anywhere. The doubt wants clear-
ing up.

That “Profits” column looks very good indeed no
violent gymnastics anywhere, but a steady movement
the right way. Of course the figures very largely de-
pend on the stock-taking. Supposing a pessimist with
a bad liver came along next year and superintended
the job in place, let’s say, of the optimist who did it
last year, it is quite possible that there would be a
difference of £lO,OOO or more in the valuation; and
that would make a large hole in next year’s profits
And such things are possible, as this co. found out
some years ago when the directors discovered that
certain branches had inflated values, which is the nice
way of saying that they had invented fictitious profits.
And when you add fictitious profits to this year’s true
profits, you ve got to take them either out of reserves
later on or out of future true profits. There’s that
risk in connection with every co. whose assets con-
sist largely of stock, and the risk is greatest where
the stock isn’t under one roof, is varied, perishable,
and liable to fluctuations in value. The most the in-
vestor can do is to size up the management and take
a general view of the business over a series of years
He may still go wrong, because errors may be re-
peated year after year; but somebody is much more
likely to drop on them in the course of 10 years than
in the brief space of one year. Taking that general
view, things certainly look all right with Kemp-
thorne-Prosser’s; and the management seems to be
prudent—in proof, note that when new shares have
been issued a bit of premiums has been got in to
strengthen the reserves, and observe the steady
writing down of plant and other assets. Going into
balance-sheet details these results are dug out-
1903. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Capital . . £200,000 £200,000 £228,708 £230,000
Liabilities . . 44,354 57,810 59,599 61,851
Stock .. 145,824 154,582 165,677 173,402
Factories, etc. 80,093 104,062 111,908 115,639
Book debts . . 46,516 53,827 58,180 65,817
Cash, etc. . . 9,997 5,761 13,836 2,444
There is really nothing that seems much out of
place there. Liabilities have grown £17,497 in 10
years; but the business has evidently grown very
considerably too. There is nothing spectacular
about it; it looks iust a sober, solid, steady, profitable
business that isn’t forcing things, but is nevertheless
getting its share of the new trade that arrives with
every immigrant, home-grown or imported.

The £32,218 written off for the past year is made
up this way: Leaseholds, £750; plant, £3OOO,
rolling stock, £2000; goodwill, £15,000; freeholds,
£11,468. On the surface it looks as though several
of these items ought to be charged direct to Profit
and Loss, instead of appropriating amounts out of
declared, and thereby inflated, profits. But it all
depends. Probably a fair amount to cover the year s
depreciation has been already charged, the appro-
priations being extra amounts to cover depreciation
that ought to have been charged years ago, but
wasn’t. Anyway, £10,026 has been charged direct
for depreciation of various sorts and bad and doubt-
ful debts; and the directors say that the “special
provision made for writing down plant, machinery
and rolling stock,” has reduced those items to abso-
lute value.” There still remain freeholds, and lease-
holds and goodwill. These things are lumped with
machinery, plant and rolling stock, the whole un-
tidy heap standing at £459,269. If a co. issues
accounts for the purpose of showing what its position
.really is, it does not mix goodwill with the tangible
That item is merely make-weight—so much
froth in the pot. The goodwill froth probably still
represents a lot of the capital. The co. was formed
in 1887, and that was a time when goodwills were
valued at a very tall figure. The businesses taken over
were those of E. T. Smith and W. K. Simms; the
share capital was £250,000 (150,000 shares paid
to £1 and 50,000 paid to £2) ; and the balance of
the purchase money was represented by a mortgage
debt, subsequently converted into 5 p.c. debentures.
The debentures (£200,000) still stand; and as the
£lO,OOO interest is a first charge against profits, the
debt for present practical purposes amounts to a
large cumulative pref. share issue.
There is nothing visible to account for so large an
increase of profits during the last few years. In
1906, 1907 and 1909, S. Aus. was having very
prosperous times; and though thirsty Broken Hill
wasn’t as affluent as it is just now, it wasn’t hard-
up. There is something to account for a steady, even
fairly quick, growth of business and profits; but a
jump from £17,551 in 1908-9 to £5O 968 in 1912-
13 can only be explained by assuming that when A.
E. Nott became manager in 1905 (they have made
him general-manager since) he proceeded at once to
do some vigorous cleaning-up, and has gradually eased
off. Also, no doubt, the anti-liquor campaign was
costing more money a few years ago than it is now.
That big Cold Tea vote in S. Aus. is always likely
to cause trouble. Not that the closing up of a pub here
and a pub there is going to seriously affect the con-
sumption of beer; but it is going to affect the book-
value of the freeholds which have had their beer-
cannon spiked. The situation, then, is something
like this: (1) There is a heavy debenture debt, and
the demand for regular interest on it is dead-sure.
(2) The value of the tangible assets is unknown to
anybody except insiders. (3) Wowser and other
anti-grog influences are stronger in S. Aus. than in
any other State. (4) S. Aus. (plus Broken Hill)
has had a succession of extra-good years, and to the
real prosperity has been added the bogus prosperity
of a borrowing and spending boom. Against these
things place the facts that the S. Aus. Brewing Co.
has a solid, old-established business; that competi-
tion in Adelaide isn’t keen; and that profits could
shrink one-half and still pay 74 p.c., and leave about
£BOOO a year for writing-down goodwill and the
like purposes.

During its land boom Melbourne overworked most
things except its C.O.D. principles. Having dis-
covered that the day of the private trustee was pass-
ing (probably it had a suspicion that most of the
people it knew weren’t to be trusted), it projected
an unnecessary number of cos. to do the business.
When the slump happened, three of the cos.- —the
Union, Australian Natives and Colonial Permanent—■
did the sensible thing, and formed themselves into
this one. Unfortunately that didn’t end the trouble ;
even six bad eggs won’t make one good egg, no
matter how hard you may beat them together. The
trouble was that such a heap of their capital had
been put into mortgages, whose value consisted
largely of hot air. It took years of nursing to re-
store the finances to order, and during that long
period the shareholders got little out of the busi-
ness. Now, however, the mortgages have practically
disappeared, and, assuming that the city freehold
which has been bought and much imnroved is all
right, the assets look a solid lot. This shows how
things have changed :
1896. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Liabilities.. .. £132 £9,235 £722 £65
Public securities 32,667 33,288 33,286 32,758
Mortgages . . 32,451 8,692 2,269 2,210
Freehold.. .. 27,632 40,938 44,474
Deposits, cash, etc. 10,044 23,806 9,885 8,341
The paid capital is £73,822, or£l per share. There is
10s. per share uncalled ; and in the event of liquida-
tion shareholders are liable for another £l. This
puts the co.’s' clients in a very secure position, for,
of course, the co.’s capital and reserves are the guar-
antee given to clients that trust moneys will be there
when they are payable, and that the instructions of
testators will be carried out. Not that the ordinary
trustee co. guarantees either principal or interest of
trust estates. So far as this paper knows, there is
only one office which does that —the Public Trust
Department of Maoriland—and then, of course, it
must ba permitted to nominate the securities. But
what a professional trustee co. does that a private
trustee doesn’t, is to put up a guarantee that there
will be no unlawful mishandling of the dead man’s
assets. Obviously, the value of the guarantee de-
pends to a great extent upon the ratio of the co.’s
funds to the value of the estates in hand. A co. with
£50,000 of its own and £5,000,000 of trusts clearly
doesn’t offer as good a guarantee as a co. with
£50,000 of its own and £10,000,000 of other
people’s money. It is highly desirable, therefore,
that every trust co. should set out the amount of
trusts which its funds have to more or less protect;
and this co. is one of the few that don’t do it. It
is a stupid omission, because, on any such compari-
son, the Union would probably show up exceedingly
well. The co.’s backing is very good (Fairbairns,
Bowes Kelly, David Elder and the like are inter-
ested) ; it has a valuable connection in Queensland as
well as Victoria ; the last of the old dead-horse ap-
pears to have been shifted ; on appearances, the co.
has not credited itself with commission which it
hasn’t yet earned ; and altogether shareholders’ out-
look is very satisfactory indeed.
London Stores, Ltd., which bought a

The business was taken over in June, 1911; but
£3OOO was paid for the profits as from Feb. 1, and
£1536 was made on that deal. This amount being
included with the profits, it seems a fair thing to
regard the co. as having traded from Feb., 1911,
though the div. was paid for eight months only.
The goodwill and such-like items mentioned above
were as follows to start with:—Building alterations
(leasehold premises), £7400; preliminary exes.,
£2309; goodwill, £16,659. Total, £26,368. Orig-
inally it looked as though the building alterations
must be debited with £9400; but £2OOO of this
has been transferred to shop fixtures account. Fur-
ther “alterations” run to £1666, which amount has
been added to the paper assets. This was really an
amount paid for rent while the premises were being
altered. The practical effect of the expenditure by
the co. out of capital is, or should be, to reduce the
amount that would otherwise have to be paid as rent.
The premises are held on a long lease; and instead of
paying the landlord rent for the improvements, the
co. makes them itself and gradually writes off the
cost out of gross profits. To that extent it has or
may have something to show for part of the deficit-
something in the shape of a reduced rent bill. But
that is a very vague, misty “asset.”
The capital is £70,000 ; accumulated profits and
reserve funds of various sorts, £5695 (this was
before payment of £2BOO, the dividend for the
second haif of the year) ; liabilities. £ 5368 ; total,
£81,063. Against that the co. has —Stock, £39,952;
cash, £6184; book debts. £3095; furniture, fixtures
and plant, £6807 ; and the intangible assets afore-
mentioned, £25,025. But for the intangible assets,
which are certainly large, that position would look
very well indeed. The smallness of the book debts of
all kinds is an excellent feature, and the co. is
clearly in a position to get all the advantages of
buying for cash, while offering the same benefits to
its customers. It is too early yet to say whether too
much was paid for the goodwill. Even at the end
of the first year the directors remarked—“ Since the
purchase by the co. the wages have largely increased
by decisions of the Wages Boards.” Still, though
this probably means that the figures on which the co.
bought were too optimistic, the owners might not
have sold for £15,000 if they hadn’t smelt some-
thing. so it can’t be said yet that they got too much
for their name and stand and connection. Never-
theless, as a name alone won’t hold a customer for
long, the wise articles of association provide that 10
p.c. of the profits must go to reserve, presumably for
the writing-off of the goodwill item, and the steady
replacement of that fluff by solid coin or marketable
goods. A provision of that sort and a few other
things suggest that they were prudent men who took
this business in hand.

Adelaide has just roused up to the fact
that, though it is unusually well fed in every other
detail, its fish supply is both scarce and costly. A
co. calling itself Fish Industry, Limited, with Lance
Stirling (President of the Legislative Council) and
other solid men as directors, is, therefore, getting
its prospectus out. The move is the d:rect conse-
quence of a report by the Federal trawler Endeavor,
which finds the sea around Kangaroo Island barren,
but the bight a little further west thick with fish
70 miles from shore. “It is not a question of sup-
ply,” Commonwealth-Director Dannevig says; “it is
one merely of transit to market.”

“Kilowatt”: Re the deal made by the
Hobart Gas Co. with the Hydro-Electric Co. of
Tasmania. The Hobart Gas Co. is only buying cur-
rent wholesale and retailing light at the rate pro-
vided in the Government Bill. The Gas Co. pays
a minimum of £5OOO a year from the beginning,
and recoups the Hydro-Electric Co. £20,000
already spent on electric light installation. The
Hydro-Electric keeps all its power contracts
and takes over the gas co.’s power service. This
arrangement had to be made in order to complete
an undertaking to supply light to the municipalities
from the end of 1912 ; as the Hvdro-Electric
wasn’t ready, the gas co. kept the contracts for it.
So the deal is good bpth ways. Hydro-Electric
has an authorised debenture is«”o of a million, and
has issued a second £150,000 to complete the hydro
scheme and the zinc-smelting and carbide works.

When the Empire Trade Commission
spoke in Brisbane recently with B. Magnus, general-
manager of the Mount Morgan G.M. Co., and formerly
manager of the electrolytic refinery at Kembla
(N.S.W.), it heard something it didn’t ex-
pect. Briefly, Magnus informed the Qommis-
sion that, of the copper produced in Australia, only
one-fourth was refined here, while about three-fourths
went to foreign countries to give foreign workmen a
job and supply foreign manufactures with their raw
material; and he wanted to know if the English roll-
ing mills couldn’t keep the refining business inside
the Empire. Later on he progressed a piece, and
said he would like to see all the refining done in
Australia, and tentatively suggested an export duty.
Just there the Commission opined that it wouldn t
do to butt into Australian politics, and Magnus was
choked off that subject; which was a pity. As he
put it, the Australian Government levies import
duties, and why shouldn’t it impose export duties?
Yet Magnus only touched the fringe of the matter.
The copper that is produced here should not only be
refined, but also manufactured to the utmost limit.
So should the tin and other metals. So should the
wheat, wool, hides, etc. We would shriek with
laughter if the Australian farmer exported milk or
cream and then re-imported butter for his own re-
quirements ; yet that is what Australia is doing with
its lead, copper, tin, wool, hides, etc.
At the moment of writing about 6000 men
are idle at Broken Hill, and most of the mines are
shut down. The mining cos. have ostensibly no
quarrel with their own workmen; but the stoppage
of the Silverton tramway has cut off their means of
reaching the seaboard. It is a pitiful business alto-
gether. Nominally, the entire hold-up is over seven
employees of the Silverton Tramway Go. who exer-
cise more or less authority—such people as assistant
or relieving stationmaster, etc. The unions— they
are all closely federated at Broken Hill—insisted
that these people should join the Clerks’ Union.
When they didn’t join, all the employees came out.
The seven then joined. But the co. says that these
men are officers, and, under the rules of the co.,
officers may not join the Trades Hall. The co. there-
fore declines the proposal to remit the matter to
any arbitration court. The Broken Hill unions, or,
rather, the handful of men who control them, long
ago acquired that most troublesome disease called
swelled head, and for several years past they have
been a thorn in the flesh of the Labor party; never-
theless, there is room for arbitration in this, matter.
In principle, the Silverton Tramway Co. is quite right
■ —-no man in authority, and with the power to recom-
mend a man’s preferment or disrating or disnussai,
should be compelled to join a labor union. He is
the employer’s eyes and ears; it is to him, in case ot
a dispute between employer and employees, that the
employer must turn for expert advice. It should he
for an arbitration court of some kind to say whether
these men come within that category. If they do.
the co. should be entitled to say whether it win

arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

* * * *
Zinc Corporation’s estimated profits, after
paying for tailings and everything else except office
exes.: January, £11,667; February, £12,618;
March, £13,293. It is cheerful while it lasts. But
it won’t last for ever—or even for half of ever. The
secured tailings dumps contain about 1,800,000 tons;
and they are being eaten up at the rate of from
300,000 to 350,000 tons a year. (For the three
months mentioned the input was 76,020 tons, or at
the rate of 304,080 tons a year.) That means six
years’ life at the present rate of treatment. On top
of that there is the output from the South Blocks
mine, owned by the co. This property can produce
35,000 tons of zinc-treatment material per annum,
along with about a third of that quantity of slimes.
How much ore the mine can profitably yield depends
upon the metal markets ; at present it is fairly safe
to say there are a million tons showing, or enough
to keep the lead plant going seven years—simul-
taneously, of course, with the zinc plant. The pro-
bable life is longer ; so the Zinc Corporation has seven
or eight years, or thereabouts, fairly well secured. For
an existence beyond that it will have to depend on
(1) further purchases, at prices that are not likely
to yield the present profit to the treatment co., and
(2) to developments in the South Blocks. As to the
profits to be made in that time, all depends on the
metal markets; and The Bulletin can’t say what
they are going to be, as its prophet is nursing a
cold caught through neglecting to take an umbrella
with him one recent morning that turned out wet.
• * * *
Mount Boppy, the Ma State’s most pro-
lific gold mine, which has put £419,582 into J.
Bull’s pocket since 1902, had to face so big a capi-
tal expenditure last year that, for a change, share-
holders got nothing. Reserves were 230,000 tons
when the last estimate was made a few months ago.
That is about four years’ supply for the plant. But
the character of the stone has changed considerably,
and with the new processes future profits are not
such dead-sure things as those in the past have been.
“Looker-On”: The Eleanora has had a
crushing of 52 tons for 108 oz., with tons of
concentrates yet to treat. This, it is reported, is
from No. 1 winze east, No. 7 level; and I see it
printed that the same result should be averaged from
all development work. Australian investors were
badly enough bitten over Hillgroye Prop., and they
don’t want more of the same thing, I suppose. If
so, they will refuse to get excited about that crush-
ing. There’s stone to the extent of 2000 tons broken
in the course of development work, and stacked at
the surface. Let that be put through before the
Australian public accepts 20z.-odd as the fair aver-
age of the mine.
* * * *
“H.G.” : This from the Adelaide Register
is a fair sample of the “news” with which the
Chow-loving business men of Port Darwin have
kept most southern papers supplied for years past:
“The Government has the idea that the deplorable
state of mining is due to bad legislation and
Chinese. The sad fact is that all work to date has
failed to prove one really big payable proposition
in the country. Had this been found, neither legis-
lation nor Chinese would have checked the prosperity
which follows profitable mining.” Admittedly, no
big dividend-paying mining proposition has yet
been developed in the Territory. But that doesn’t
prove that the Territory is not rich. When the Pine
Creek railway was completed the Chinese coolies
who were brought out for the work remained in the
country; and until recently a couple of thousand of
these, with their descendants and those others who
have been smuggled in, remained there. They have
lived, and most of them have lived well, on the gold
and tin they have won. Many of them have taken
away small fortunes also. The mining they have
done has in the main been surface scratching on
mineral areas discovered by whites, for the Chinese
are not prospectors; in nearly every case they have
paid tribute to the white merchants, agents, or Gov-
ernment officials of Darwin. In the gold-bearing
area near Brock’s Creek are to be found acres and
acres honeycombed, pig-rooted by Chinese, and to
a great extent spoiled for future legitimate pros-
pecting. The Zapopan mine in that district was
held by Darwin people, but the initial costs depleted
their funds. Instead of making good use of the tail-
ings they had, they got rid of these for a song, and
the man who bought them made a handsome profit.
The mine they let on tribute to the Chinese. And
this is what happened. The gold was found along
the footwall. The Chinese simply gouged out suffi-
cient stone to give them room to work, and when
they were eventually beaten by the water, through
their primitive methods of working, they left the
reef hanging, supported only by a few props here
and there. After the following wet season the mine
was no longer safe. To work it again would require
a new and expensive shaft. Yet when the Chinese
left the mine they were on good stone. This is but
one instance of many. Wherever allowed to go into
a mine, the Chinese made narrow workings till the
water beat them, and then they left behind a death-
trap. Prospecting in the Territory is not an easy
matter. The white population has always been
small, and for the white laborer there has been
little chance of regular employment at wages. The
prospector seldom owns much money, and conse-
quently, no matter how promising his show, he has
had to go to the Darwin storekeepers and agents
for assistance. Thus, the office-stool has acquired a
share, and eventually got a controlling interest, and
in many cases the lot. The next step has been the
putting of Chinese to work the claim on tribute;
then the eyes have been picked out, and presently
another one has been added to the tale of aban-
doned mines. A few attempts certainly have been
made to test mines in a big way. but they have
failed in most cases because of grosslv extravagant
administration. The co. which held the Union and
Yam Creek mines was a bright example. Miles of
telephone lines were erected, and fine residences,
with billiard-rooms and tennis courts, provided.
Thousands of pounds’ worth of machinery was pur-
chased which was never taken out of the cases. Yet
the Union reef is undoubtedly a big thing. The failure
of these big ventures frightened speculators, and the
action of Darwin business people in persistently
dummying for Chows kept white miners out of the
country; bad legislation made it possible for these
office-chair whites to hold claims on miners rights,
and fulfil the working conditions by letting on
tribute to Chinese ; and the Chinese, by their methods
of mining snoiled for the nrosoector manv shows
which held much promise. Moreover, the future will be
the same failure, in spite of the Territory’s wealth,
unless if is insisted that labor conditions on all
mineral claims are fulfilled by white labor.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) is not
bothering about its somewhat lean bottom levels at
present. Practically all its spare time is being taken
up in searching for additional ore between the Nos.
2 and 11 levels, where a lot of odds and ends have
been unearthed during the past year. Monthly profits
now average between £2OOO and £3OOO, but as it
takes £38,500 to pay a 6d. div. on the 1,400,000
issued shares, there isn’t much to look forward to, in
view of the somewhat risky outlook and the absence
of any bullion or substantial cash reserve.
As expected, Bind Bind (W.A.), which
this paper described as boom froth, is now deserted.
A mining man of varied experiences went into rap-
tures over the show, and the goldfields papers
backed up his opinion with puffs. These were re-
published in the Adelaide dailies, and in due course
a syndicate was formed to give it a trial. Then the
Government was abused for not providing water
facilities. When it did so, and there was no further
excuse for doing nothing, the show was thrown up
and the field just about petered out.
* * * *
Golden Butterfly (W.A.) has resumed
crushing after a month’s spell. Since August last,
when the mill first started, 7428 tons have been
treated for a return of about that number of ounces
over the plates. Costs are said to have approxi-
mated 165., leaving a gross working profit of 4s. per
ton. This, of course, does not allow for proper develop-
ment, depreciation, etc. Accumulated residues amount
to about 3700 tons, valued at 11s. 4d. per ton, of which
a shade under 9s. is alleged to be clear profit. On
a monthly output of 1500 tons a gross surplus of
£lOOO is expected; but in mining it is much safer
to rely on results than expectations.

Chaffers (W.A.) claims to have driven
236 ft. on sft. of 28s. ore at the No. 16 level, and to
have 31s. stuff show'ng in the face. This sounds en-
couraging, but somehow the market doesn’t respond.
By following geologist McLaren’s advice
Great Fingall has now good lengths of payable ore
showing in the levels driven from the internal shaft
below No. 13 workings; and in the bottom, at No.
18 level, about 480 ft. of driving has been done in
fairly good stuff. At this point the shoot also shows
6igns of lengthening a bit.
* * * *
Mountain Queen (Westralia) bitterly
complains of ill-treatment by the State Government.
After spending £36,000 on plant, development, etc.,
and contracting with the Marvel Loch for water at
Is. 6d. per 1000 gals., it was told that the Mines
Department proposed to prohibit the co. getting a
supply from this source while the Government scheme
was available. As the Government charge is 7s. per
thousand, the increase in the monthly water bill
would be £219. The co. is deserving of sympathy,
inasmuch as pipe lines were laid and plant adapted
for using the Marvel Loch water before the Govern-
ment decided to extend its pipe line to the district.
The directors have brought the matter under the
notice of Premier Scaddan, in London, and he has
promised to look into it on his return to Perth.
Union and Dorritt, a little go at Grant,
North Gippsland, has the undertaker on the door-
step. It has been trying to pull through on penny
calls, eked out by Government grants; but share-
holders have got tired, and the directors have no-
thing but a hard bar and vague hopes to offer as a
pick-me-up. With another Government grant the
shareholders may be induced to part with a few more
pence; but the grant hasn’t been made yet, and if it
isn’t forthcoming the shutters will most likely go
up. The co. m’ght cut something in another 70ft. or
so. If it is disappointed there it will be quite a
fair thing to turn its face to the wall.
Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), has been pros-
pecting out west at its bottom level, where a bit of
help from the Little Ajax, or some other shoot of
ore, was needed to liven up a dog-bitten market.
The first news about the expected stranger told of
promising-looking veins with that little flow of water
which Daylesford always likes. The next word was
that the pug was in view. Speckers retreated until
they could see how this would unwind, but so far
Little Ajax’s dirt is a mere 6in., with dashed little
color in it.
* * * •
Northern Hope, Beaufort (Vic.), got
flushed out a while since, apparently through poking
an inquisitive nose into some of the old Beaufort
workings. Now the co. is cutting down the shaft
and living in the expectation that it will find 800 ft.
of a level in good order after the inrush of water.
The ground was nice, and the gamble is whether the
gutter lately opened is a main lead with a length
of a mile of wash in the co.’s lease, or a tributary.
Before that can be proved the water has to be
drained and a general clean-up carried through. This
isn’t the cheap job the ordinary man hankers after,
and it may be well in the year before Northern Hope
can testify whether it is a 2Joz. proposition or a
mere watercourse.

Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.), is handed
over to a new co. for £7500, and starts afresh with
a register of 54,000 shares of 10s. each. Under-
writers get 6000 paid-ups for flicking through a not
too popular float of 24,000 shares. These are paid up
to 4s. and subscribed for by Walhalla punters and
others who don’t usually touch mining. The old
Walhalla crowd, which wouldn’t risk its money,
although it spent £15,000 of accumulated profits
poking around the bottom levels, is to become a
memory of the past. The record of Cohen’s reef—
1,476,0000z. gold, valued at £5,110,000, and divs.
£2,376,000, of which Long Tunnel gathered in
£1,280,000 —ought to have livened up the old
stagers, but it did not. The co. will have as directors
Wesley Adamson, T. J. Greenwav. Harry Hartrick
and P. H. Miles. Government Geologist Herman will
have a big say in the policy, because the State
Mines Department puts up £lO,OOO on a £ for £
basis to help things along. There is already talk of
punching into the levels at deoth for a start, prob-
ably because the evidence isn’t convincing that the
old co. really worked Cohen’s reef there. Next will
be some prospecting near the surface and a try in
the shallow ground at the South Long Tunnel. E.
G. Dickenson has the secretaryship, vice Dawson,
who goes farming, presumably having collected his
liquidation fee prior to leaving for his fallow at
Terang.
A Sydney concern. Phosphates, Ltd., is
out for the Commonwealth bonus of 7s. a ton. The
co. has secured the Rockhampton (Q.) deposits of
bat guano, and the Customs Department has arranged
to pay the bonus, as on weathered deposits which
have become phosphatic rock. The phosnhates in
this guano are soluble up to 96 p.c., and the lowest-
grade stuff as yet developed is identical with fertil-
isers selling in Melbourne at 755. a ton. Quantities
are as yet not defined. The railway is already handy,
and is to be extended to within half a mile of the
deposits.
w * * *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, held
its half-yearly meeting lately. Chairman Millican
confessed that the stopes above No. 4 and No. 5
levels had not come up to expectations; 4173 tons
were crushed for 1122,979. When No. 5 is driven
further in towards the Brilliant Blocks boundary, it
should, however, cut better stone coming down from
No. 4 level. It is intended to sink the shaft and
the winze below No. 5 level as soon as additional
boilers are installed. No. 4 level is also to be ex-
tended into the Brilliant Blocks lease, in accordance
with an agreement, which also provides for the sink-
ing of a shaft some 700 ft. deep. This shaft has been
started from the end of the crosscut at the 2000 ft.
level, and it will take the Blocks a few months to
complete the job. The Deeps will also have to
spend a good deal of money to connect the workings
of the two mines, and if the crushings do it all,
shareholders may consider themselves lucky.
* * * *
The New Queen Cross, Charters Towers,
is completing its haulage shaft, which will be
equipped with a double truck-road. Shares were 6d.
12 months since, so some speculators have made
money— -or, anyway, have the chance to make it.
Of course, the Queen Cross cannot be expected to
sustain an average of £l2 per ton, but prospects are
good near the Papuan’s boundary, and there is,
roughly, 1000 ft. of country to explore over towards
the opposite side of the lease. Its success has made
a cluster of neighbors busy. The Brilliant and St.
George expects to strike the Moonstone reef in the
block claim at less than 500 ft., and Clark’s Brilliant
and Day Dawn will also prospect several reefs, in-
cluding the Hidden Treasure, that dip into the Cross
lease. Most important to the Cross is the work
being done by its neighbor, No. 2 South-East Day
Dawn, which is driving towards it at 120 ft. vertical
on a diorite formation, with a vein of mineralised
stone showing.
For the first quarter of this year Mount
Morgan produced copper and gold valued at
;L252,844 (Jan., £77,329; Eeb., a 83,849 ; March,
£91,666). A quarter million in three months
shows that the mine is holding its end up; but it
doesn’t say a word about costs.
Avowedly with the object of getting some
Australian interest in the Mammoth Copper Co. —a
Glasgow concern with a mine out beyond Mungana
(N.Q.) —a block of 5000 shares has been reserved
for issue in these parts. As far as can be made
out, 70,000 £1 shares are authorised, and 50,000
have been issued, the last lot of 5000 at £1 pre-
mium—the price Australia will have to pay for its
5000, if it wants them. It will probably like to
know something more about the mine before it
wants them badly. The co. holds a big area, run-
ning along what is supposed to be the lode for nearly
two miles. The Chieftain is at one end; the Clans-
man at the other. Between these two points,
where most work has been done, there are numerous
shafts and a tunnel or two, and oxidised ore has
been got in some places; but no details are avail-
able. The Chieftain has been probed to 160 ft., and
J. C. Vivian, the imported manager—probably a
good man, though whether he is safe in his esti-
mates or not this paper doesn’t know—reckons the
reserves at 30,000 tons. The plans, however, show
very little development work, and any figure that
could be arrived at by survey now couldn’t give
much of an idea of the resources of the mine.
There is on the Chieftain property a water-jacket
furnace which had a trial run a while ago and pro-
duced 254 tons matte —40 p.e. copper, 6idwt. gold
and 21oz. silver. The ore seems to be worth from
10 to 12 p.c. copper, ldwt. gold and 7oz. silver.
The stuff is good enough, if the quantity is there;
and the thing is certainly worth looking into. If
the lode runs for anything like two miles, and there
is 10 p.c. ore in shoots all that way. the fact that
the matte has to be carted 20 or 30 miles won’t
knock the bottom out of the profits.
# * * *
Gippsland Copper Co. has just completed
an experimental smelting run under the management
of Santowski, formerly of Mount Molloy (N.Q.).
Matte produced was 75 tons, beating all former
efforts. But the same old trouble has yet to be
faced—sufficient cash capital to put the mine in
order and find ironstone. The revenue derived from
twopenny calls is eaten up before the money is paid,
and shareholders have got into the luibit of forfeit-
ing. Reconstruction seems the sensible way. There
are many copper propositions with le-'s pretensions
to success that have had more attention from the
public. The ore averages 3 p.c. copper and carries
platinum and palladium, an addition to the matte
which metal buyers seem to appreciate.
* * * *
Manager Laby, taking his Victorian ex-
perience up north, is threatening to make the Oaks
Amalgamated pay on sdwt. dirt. In fact, with stuff
torn out from a 10ft. face, and going as low as
3dwt., he has lately more than covered working costs.
But North Queensland hasn’t got into the habit of
providing itself with plant that means cheap work,
and Laby is almost in a state of rebellion. The
directors naturally want to give him a chance, but
that means calls, and shareholders are not falling
over one another to pay them. Laby’s idea is to get
to the sulphides, meantime attacking the big low-
grade bodies with more economical appliances. He
deserves his fling.

Great Fitzroy (Q.), taking copper at
£67 Is. 6d. per ton, estimates last month’s revenue
at £15,300. Working expenses take £14,776, so
profits are still not in sight, when depreciation, office
exes., etc., are allowed for.
The outlook for Vulcan (N.Q.) doesn’t
improve. Last crushing was 823 tons for £1706=
£2 Is. 6d. per ton. For last half-year (to Feb. 10)
3926 tons gave £9936 =£2 10s. 9d. per ton, and
even on that grade of stone the Profit and Loss bal-
ance was reduced from £2195 to £1387.
For its last 12 months Tasmania Gold
Mine (Tas.) crushed 51,889 tons for 21,4090 z., which
didn’t pay expenses. Ore reserves above the 1500 ft.
level are reckoned at 137,000 tons. The reef gives
a good average stoping width, but intrusions of
barren rock lessen its value. The flow of water is
also tremendously heavy (3,500,000 gallons per day).
The eo. owns ground on the bank of the Tamar
River, and to raise the wind it is proposed to sell
some of it to orchardists.
* * * *
When the Austral-Malay Tin Trust was
formed a few months ago, H. E. Pratten, who had
negotiated the options, was sent to the Malay Penin-
sula to give an eye to the borings and see to the
business side of the deals, if any were made. The
most promising area was at Taiping, between Penang
and Ipoh, where a large area of suitable dredging
ground had been tentatively secured. Subsequent
negotiations led to the representative of a strong
London crowd being let in, and word has now been
received that, the option on 457 acres having been
exercised, a London co. is to be floated, the Austral-
Malay Trust taking a half-share in the promotion,
and, of course, in the “first-robber’s” cut. The ground
doesn’t appear to be extra-rich —probably about Is.
3d. a yard—but, what is of much importance, there
appear to be no two opinions about its suitability
for bucket dredging. The property is known as the
Kamunting. The Trust will now give some attention
to its other options.
The Australian-owned Tongkah Compound,
working its rich Siamese patch, dredged up 33,500
yards last month for 7U tons black tin; and the
area is so suitable for the bucket machine that £l'2
per ton of black tin covered all costs, leaving over
£lOO profit. It is a glittering result. There is a
probability that a big Anglo-Australian co. will be
formed to take over this property along with some
other Malay dredging areas.
* * * *
The Queensland Full Court lately did a
good thing in deciding that the Commissioner of
Taxation was wrong in charging income tax on an
amount received by a co. as premiums on a new share
issue. The Commissioner said it was “income from
personal exertion” —the co. had done so well that it
got an extra price for its shares. The matter was
discussed here some time ago in connection with a
similar claim in S. Aus., and the opinion was ex-
pressed that, since reserve funds are such an ex
cellent thing, any action which might deter a co.
from establishing such a fund by charging pre-
miums for new shares or otherwise, was imprudent.
The Full Court in Queensland, dissenting from Judge
Rutledge, came to much the same conclusion. Pre-
miums are really amounts paid towards the reserved
capital of a co. by way of purchasing an interest in
the reserved capital already accumulated ; and, if the
S. Aus. law allows such premiums to be taxed as
income—which it isn’t —the time is ripe to alter
that law so as to conform to the sensible Queensland
Full Court judgment. Meantime, if the claim is in the
discretion of the S.A. Commissioner, he might do the
fair thing and drop it without pushing any citizens
to the expense of litigation.
