A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 14, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The Bush Book Club met at State Gov-
ernment House by Lady Edeline Strickland’s
invitation, the other afternoon. Her Lady-
ship, with' little Mrs. James McGowen at
her elbow, sat at a large round table, and
industriously assimilated a few hundred facts
which floated round the ballroom. Colonel
Campbell and A.D.C. Forbes, firmly but-
tressed behind uniforms lashed with large
gold ropes, gently led the early Bookworm
to the vice-regal presence. The strenuous
Margaret Windeyer was in front of the
fighting line as Hon. Appraiser of Literature
that May be Read. Anything left over goes
to the Seamen’s Mission or some other
Cause. The smart young Club secretary,
Beulah Bolton, well tailored in elephant
grey, nearly inked her brand-new white kid
gloves when the facts wanted something
.sudden done to them with a pen. Mrs. hon.
treasurer Rutter waved a generous helping
■of ostrich plumes in the forefront of the
assembly. Private Sec. Victor Hood made
■a thoughtful pilgrimage to the reporters’
tables, to add his informative mite to the
mountain of white paper, spread around to
:sop up the annual report. There seemed to
ibe enough writing material lying about to
•contain the full opinion of the N.S.W. Oppo-
sition on the political doings of Mr. Speaker
Willis, with footnotes and addenda by Mr.
William McLinen.
Financially, the Bush Book Club is hang-
ing on to the sum of £9l 2s. 6d. till more
supplies come in. Mr. Raymond Jones,
who audited the assets to the uttermost
farthing, was present on the day of reckon-
ing at State Government House. With
him was Mrs. Raymond, looking very blithe
behind a large white tulle bow. Governor
Strickland made a wary entrance to the
Bush corroboree, when the flood of female
oratory had been stemmed with tea-cake. The
Eldest Daughter, who laughs on the sunny
side of 17, was looking for work all the
afternoon. She rendered efficient first-aid to
dames-in-council, in a very young white
frock, topped by a flat black hat that flung a
velvet ribbon under a round white chin.
Hon. treasurer Dorothy Stephen, of the
Girls’ Realm Guild, went off to Eurone a
week or two ago, to cultivate her taste for
water-colors in France and other Conti-
nental parts. Her hon. salary for counting
up the Realm cash, and chasing lost farthings
to their long home in the bank, is exactly
Nothing, and even that is in arrear. The
Guild intends to reduce it to half that
amount, if treasurer Dorothy doesn’t bustle
home at the end of her 12 months’ leave of
absence. In the meantime, organising sec.
Grace Burrows is trying to kidnap a locum
tenens in place of the wanderer. Some day
soon, she’ll pounce round a corner suddenly
and seize the capable Miss Minnie Tange by
the hair of her head, and appoint her to the
glittering billet.
The Girls’ Realm is praying hard for a
fine day for its Zoo fete, when a large and
healthy menagerie of young persons will add
to the attractions of the Moore Park show.
I fancy they’re going to process, in fancy
costume, the Zoo authorities taking half the
gate-money, or something of the kind. Ouh-
side the gates of Tigerland, the Realm
damsels will sell some of the lighter deli-
cacies of the season, having taken their
solemn affidavit not to obstruct the traffic.
The Fine Arts Society in Bligh-street,
sells pictures more or less by stealth, and
blushes to find the cash. Its left hand
doesn’t know how much its right hand col-
lects, and it loves to be a sort of hidden
benefactor, like King Wenceslaus or old Pa
Christmas. Still, its exclusive and oyster-
like silence is broken from time to time by
enterprising travellers like Baldwin Spen-
cer. Last time Explorer Spencer was here
he carried off some of Norman Lindsay’s
work, a yard or two of Bondi beach torn off
in oils by Griiner. and a portrait-study by
Harley Griffiths. But the Fine Arts Society
is so retiring that only the determined wav-
farer can find out where it is and what it
sells. As an artists’ medium for artists’
work, its excessive modesty compels it to sit
back and say nothing and bury itself in
thought. Thus it awaits the fierce rush of
several thousand buyers banging on its sign-
board for a Blamire Young hill-top or a
Haley Lever fishing smack.
Frances Hodgkins is now showing her
wares in water-colors in a charming little
gallery, arranged for the occasion, at
Anthony Hordern’s vast emporium. Take
the lift and anything else you can get, pro-
vided nobody is looking. The Hodgkins style
is so versatile that you can gaze at half a
dozen of her pictures without recognising
the same touch. She is a Modernist, but
repudiates the ultra —before that expression
of the present revolution in Franch art. Yet
she is not always a Modernist. She paints
the Dutch peasant or Netherlands
Hodge with the same sure, swift, clean
strokes that Hogarth used when he
startled comatose British art with the
strong portrait of his rather beefy-look-
ing sister. This picture, which hangs in the
National Gallery (London), is conspicuous
for its detail.
The Modernist wipes detail off his mental
palette and gives a smudge for an infant’s
nose, a few lines for a landscape and a splash
of color for a city. When Frances Hodg-
kins puts her delightful Flemish women,
French fishing boats, and brilliant sketches
of Continental European squares, streets and
market places aside, she boldly follows the
Impressionist cult. The leisured French
class, sitting on the sands to watch the tide
come in, the immaculate French infant, the
Parisian nursemaid (member of an almost
extinct class), are dismissed with a few
vivid dabs of color. The drawing is so good
that the situation is saved for the awe-
stricken amateur who likes human beef to
look like beef, and not like an entree of
yesterday’s cold mutton.
“April,” a water-color severely marked 70
guineas, is the finest example of Frances
Hodgkins’ work in her mid-Modernist mood.
The breezy hilltop, with Youth, Childhood
and the Dog, welcoming Spring (on the
other side of the Equator) by dancing round
in a giddy circle- —the Dog as adoring audi-
ence —give this picture the brisk movement
of Life.
The veteran Du Faur —81 not out —wan-
dered round the Hodgkins show the other
morning. He still has a voice ip the pur-
chase of Art for our National Gallery, though
his own taste in that debatable stuff seems
to linger somewhere in the early Guelph
period. He bore up without any visible sign
of lacerated feelings when he eyed the Mod-
ernist notion of an infant chewing an apple.
Dora Ohlfsen’s studio was crowded with
sunbeams and packing-cases one morning
last week. The cases contained many pro-
files on plaster medallions. They were
booked for Paris, where they will be fitted
with bronze overcoats, before returning to
our Australian walls. Leaning up against
a shelf was a fine study of Octavius Caesar
Holman. The profile, an admirable like-
ness, shows the strength and weakness of
the N.S.W. Attorney-General’s striking per-
sonality. The noble head only wants a few
laurel leaves to make it a fitting addition
to a collection of Roman tombs. The
deep-set fanatic’s eyes, the fine nose, also
imperial, autocratic and Caesarian, and the
disappointing, inconsequent chin make an
artistic triumph as detailed by Dora Ohlf-
sen in plaster. Ex-Governor Chelmsford,
served up in the same style, is a good like-
ness of the original, but the face is milk-
and-water compared to the imperious Hol-
man. Edmund Barton is a successful effort.
This medallion is destined for the Athenaeum
Club, the scene of Barton’s most festive
moments before he wore a High Court wig.
A life-size head of Chief Justice Cullen is
to go to the new Law Courts. A joyous
study of childhood, the Cullen’s chestnut-
haired little Ailsa. goes home to mamma.
Adrian Knox, K.C., shows a strong legal
jaw against the dead white placque on which
it is featured. But the Ohlfsen Victory is
the head of Holman.
Educational Insnector Fraser (better
known to Bulletin readers as “Jimmie
Pannikin”) gave a brisk address to State-
school teachers the other night, in the Girls’
High School. He called his curtain lecture
“Ideals,” but it boiled down to the Import-
ance of Being Joyful. Donald Dinnie
Fraser is an optimist of the 47th degree,
and he whisked away the cobwebs with a
breezy feather-duster homily. Before he
reached his final anecdote, in a light, bari-
tone voice, every iron-bark countenance
present was illumined with a smile like the
gates of Paradise.
Raymond Robins, a recent visitor
from U.S., was prepared to give sin-
ners a leg-up to the steep and stony
track that leads to a Better World.
He came here with the blessing of the
Y.M.C.A.. which is good luggage to carry
about with you. Incidentally, he is a brother
of Elizabeth Robins, who wrote “The Mag-
netic North” and “Where are you going to?”
Mr. Robins will clothe with many (!owc"' ;
of oratory the address You xire Going To, if
you don’t take his advice, and will tell you
all about the temperature you may expect
when you get There.
A Berlin correspondent treads on the
corns of the Modernists in Art. He stamps
his Australian heels in the following rush
of heavy-weight matter. “The Secession
building, which stands in Berlin’s most
fashionable street, was entirely full this
week (March 6) of the life work of one
man, ‘Louis Corinth.’ Have you heard of
him? He was President of the Secession
last year. He is ultra-modern, in choice of
subject, for his canvas sketches range from
sides of beef and chops to delicate flower
studies and sensitive ‘Pietas.’ Such a
strong hand, brutal almost, manifests itself
in every picture, whether it is a study in
expression like his ‘St. Paul,’ with staring
fanatic’s eyes, skinny, drawn face, lips apart,
showing only two discolored teeth ; or blood
dripping from the terrible figure of Samson
after his eyes have been put out.
“Startling people these modernists,
who handle the interior of a but-
cher’s shop or the head of a saint
with the same primitve love of ghastly
detail.... I left the collection quite limp.
The force that jumped out of every canvas,
the work of one man (not yet, I think, 50
years of age), who had filled eight rooms
with his prolific output, made ordinary flesh
feel spineless and ineffectual.... Winifred
Purnell gives her first concert this season, to-
night. and an Australian violinist. Elsie
Playfair, plays also with the Bluthner
orchestra.”
Hugh Ward is lending his massive aid to
the forthcoming ball on behalf of the Hos-
pice for the Dying (Darlinghurst). The
gallery is, therefore, assured of a dazzling
evening’s entertainment.
Mrs. Drummond, wdfe of the naval skip-
per of that name, has achieved quite a good
portrait of the Admirable King-Hall. It is
a clever three-quarter study in oils, mostly
flung off the lady’s palette during a recent
trip to Hobart.
The widow of Justice William Montagu
Manning, who died the other day, aged 86,
was a daughter of Archdeacon Sowerby,. of
Goulburn. She was the Judge’s second wife.
His first wife was a Miss Wise, a sister of
B. It. Wise’s father —and he was a Judge,
too. In fact, Judges were a habit in that
family, for there was another Manning—a
nephew this time —who was on the Supreme
Court Bench when the deceased lady’s hus-
band graced it in his gentle way.
The Kensingtons (father and daughter)
are coming to Sydney for a prolonged holi-
day. He used to be Under-Secretary for
Lands in Maoriland. As an old civil ser-
vant of great tonnage and poundage, he
wears an embroidered tail, called the Im-
perial Service Order.
Dahn Fisher, of M.L. politics, having
closed his reciprocity chat with Prime Min-
ister Fisher, is hurrying back to his native
geysers, via this town.
A horrid man suggests that, as Father
Bull is unable to deal out any form of im-
prisonment to Mrs. Pankhurst in proportion
to her crime in aiding and abetting incen-
diarism, bomb throwing, battle, murder and
sudden death, the Law should change its
methods. If it ordered Mrs. Pankhurst to
be spanked till the rising of the court after
each outbreak of hooliganism, law-abiding
citizens who have had their property _ de-
stroyed and their lives endangered, might
get some satisfaction out of the business.
The lady’s name might be changed to Spank-
worst, on the ground that she got to a worse
spank than any of her followers.
A son of the late Dr. Arthur Renwick,
who also follows the Pill and Plaster trade,
was married, at Summer Hill the other day,
to Miss Eileen Holland. The ’groom, Dr.
Charles Renwick, was supported by Best
Man Selwyn Holland, with another medico,
Dr. Hilton Smith, as assistant Help. The
bride is a daughter of Mr. and Mrs. G. H.
Holland. Papa is Lieut.-Colonel of a militia
regiment in his spare moments. The regi-
mental band, in consequence, poured forth
its sweetest strains as the Nice Girl and her
new husband received the congratulations of
a few score of friends in the home nest. The
bridal plumage was veiled in Limerick lace.
No wedding of any social frill has occurred
without the presence of Limerick lace for
quite 12 months. You might as well leave
out the license or forget the ’groom or the
old slipper as arrange for the legal ceremony
to pull through without this filmy accessory.
Some day, perhaps, Honiton, or mosquito
net, or Venetian Point will get a look-in. The
bridesmaidens at the Renwick-Holland
orange-blossoming were Estelle Boyd and
Naomi and Thelma Holland.
A Goulburn girl, Miss Olive James, daugh-
ter of the District Superintendent of the
Short of Trucks and Bogged Wheat Depart-
ment in these breezy parts, swore to love,
honor and obey Dr. John Snodgrass a week
or so ago. He is attached to the Medical
bureau in Manila, where cockfights break the
Sabbath calm with the exultant crow of the
victor. The vanquished is eaten on Monday.
So is the victor if he got badly damaged in
the hour of triumph. The Snodgrass turtle
doves depart for their coffee-colored home in
the Philippines via the Empire.
Mrs. Ronald Forbes, whose husband is
the tali A.D.C. w'ho wears his Highland legs
swathed in plaid trousers, is a dashing cross-
country rider. She is a member of a well-
known Lincolnshire family named Torr, and
chased the elusive fox on horseback from
the time she discarded her nursery bib.
Sydney by the way. has only had a mild
bln rd of Mrs. Forbes’s wardrobe. The
hurried slaying of George of Greece plunged
official dames into half-mourning at Easter.
Hence, the black and purple episodes at
Randwick, which are said to give no hint of
what the Forbes lady can do in frills when
she gets into her sartorial stride.
The only son of Admiral and Lady Poore
is engaged to Nellie Campbell, a daughter of
the late Mr. Willie Campbell, at one time
M.L.C. here. The Campbells have camped
in London of late years. Young Poore is,
or was, on a Ceylon tea plantation.
The Smith family is on the move. Two
birds of passage of that well-known nomen-
clature, Sir George and Lady Smith, arrived
here in the Orontes. He is a Knight, a
J.P., C.A. and D.L. In his office hours he
is boss of a big manufacturing firm called
Bickford, Smith and Co., England, with
feelers in Australasia. The Smiths have
seven sons and four daughters to perpetuate
the name. So the fear that the world might
run short of Smiths is apparently a ground-
less apprehension.
When does the Centennial Park tea room
serve tea? An indignant motorist asks this
question in italics. “One day last week,”
says he: “I trundled some ladies in my car
round the £300,000 worth of public money
represented in Centennial Park. The day
was fine, and the traffic, which frequently
consists of a returning hearse and a few per-
ambulators, was agreeably diversified by the
fumes of sundry motors. After whizzi™
through an infant avenue of palms, and ad
miring the gorgeous scarlet blooms of
autumn we sighted a small stone kiosk
flanked by a large garbage tin and some
empty bottles. A few babies in go-caH«
waited on the verandah with that solemn
round-eyed expression of being prepared for
the worst, which is part of the pessimism
of extreme youth. We asked for tea There
were various indications that this ornamen
tal sarcophagus had light refreshments
buried somewhere in its inside. The answer
was brief and inconclusive. “We’re not
serving tea to-day.” Only that and nothine
more, as the raven said to Edgar Allan Poe
when it stood on the pallid bust of Pallas
There was no postscript to suggest that they
might boil the kettle next week, or next year
or after the next coronation if we happened
to be passing about that time. So 1 merely
search for information. ‘When does a Cen-
tennial Park Tea House serve tea?’”
Oswald Anderson, who ha- " tuneful trick
of writing popular snacks of song, left Syd-
ney by the Ventura. He goes to try his
luck in the U.S.A. and the great city of
Bullopolis, as a person who can hit the
public ear with “g--d sellers.” Anderson’s
“Your Dear Eyes,” a slight and tuneful
ditty, with sentimental words bv Wilson
Bingham, had a surprising vogue in N.S.W
though local composers are still viewed with
suspicion.
Since the lamented de Beaupuis flickered
out, some of his many stealthy acts of benevo-
lence have crept into the light of day. Like
that veteran child of nature, Kretschmann,
the great Italian teacher often gave freely
of the best that was in him to the pupil
who was unable to pay in anything but the
coin of gratitude, which isn’t legal tender,
and rings with a dull, leaden thud on the
counter of the ham and beef shop. Part of
the alleged large income he made by work-
ing interminable hours instructing the Young
Idea must, therefore, have been merely the
lOU of Lazarus. He had sent to Italy for
a friend, who followed the same school, to
act as his colleague, and take over some of
the white melodist’s burden, which had
become too heavy for his failing strength.
But the Light went out suddenly.
Baritone Charles Rolfe goes to America
for 12 months to gather up ideas in the dizzy
round of U.S. concert platforms. His fare-
well song will shake the rafters of the Con-
cordia Ilall on April 29. After that, the
welkin will be repaired by an eminent local
firm of contractors.
The Eva Gauthier-Laurence Godfrey Smith
concert met the wettest end of a deluge last
week. In spite of the uproarious behavior
of usually respectable gutters and the splash
from city water-spouts, the Concordia Hall
was full for the occasion. The French-
Canadian singer trilled in English, French
and Italian, adding a Malay song with the
fearsome name of “Anak Udang,” as a
savory. The Gauthier voice has the full-
throated, velvet tones of an able-bodied
young thrush, and her vocal method is the
best the Paris Conservatoire can sell.
Pianist Laurence Godfrey, fresh and
sparkling after his holiday abroad, dallied
with Scarlatti and Brahms early in the
programme, and settled down to business
later, in the distracting hide-and-seek varia-
tions of Paderewski’s “Theme VariA”
A confirmed Tory remarks, in a belated
whisper that she is now convinced the
Lower Orders and Satan are forming a com-
bine —they work shorter hours, and he (as
usual) works overtime. Her chief reason
for this gibe is the fact that the Easter bun
was minus its cross this year. Where the
cross w T ent to I don’t know. In fact. I
didn’t miss the emblem, and am depending
entirely on my informant for its absence.
The true nationality of the cosmopolitan
oyster was emphasised by the murder of the
Greek King. Every oyster shop within a
wide area of the G.P.O. wore heavy black
and purple flounces during the solemn lying-
in-tent of George, in the distant land which
has the City of the Violet Crown as its
capital. (This is no relation to Australia’s
capital—the City of the Perished Grass-
hopper.)
On the word of a staid citizen, the follow-
ing episode occurred at Circular Quay last
Wednesday. Three larrikins who had looked
into the bottle when it was green, pink, yel-
low and fringed with fantods, were indus-
triously baiting two or three Japs. With
many hiccups, the most sober of the hooli-
gans at last said : “An’ when er yer goin’ to
take this blessed counttry.” In perfect Eng-
lish, one of the almond-eyed replied: “Ex-
actly any blessed day we choose.” Then the
blessed subject dropped.
“You can have wrinkles wiped off your
countenance for £lO in a London beauty
parlor,” says a recently returned wanderer;
“and for one guinea you can buy half a
dozen drops of eau-de-something which is
alleged to wash crows’-feet off your face as
long as it lasts. It lasts about a week, so
you can take your choice of having an
ordinary soap-and-water ablution, or grow-
ing young, beautiful and bankrupt. Pro-
bably the moral of the London beauty shark
is simple. If you are young and beautiful
you are not likely to remain bankrupt.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 14, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
More disloyalty! On Saturday night last
Lady Denman was flung out of the Mel-
bourne Repertory Theatre by Socialists. In
the natural heat of the moment I may, all
unconsciously, be overstating the case, so to
details. The unfortunate lady’s chair was in
a gangway of the d.c. in contravention of the
regulations of the Water and Sewerage
Board, or some such absurd and anarchistic
body. The president of the Board happened
to be there, shifting scenery or selling pro-
grammes or something; and, of course, he
hurried up and made trouble. Thank Heaven,
Napoleon Boffard, a Loyalist, albeit a mere
Frenchman, rose nobly to the occasion.
Boldly facing the official, Fe offered to go to
gaol. The official intimated coldly that he
did not wish to gaol Nap. ; all he wanted was
to have the gangway cleared. Napoleon then
offered to do something else to avert the
threatened horror —possibly to drown himself.
In vain. Amidst the anguish of every true
Loyalist present, Lady Denman’s chair was
moved a full yard from where it had been.
There was a widespread feeling in the house
that Melbourne had failed to rise to a great
occasion. What would have happened had
Sydney’s Tummas ’Enley or its even more
effulgent William McLinen been there? What
indeed! The scoundrelly Socialistic official
would, by now, in all likelihood, be carrion.
Admiral Cresswell and Coroner Cole per-
petrated a blunder between them, in their
attempt to make a mvstery of poor Miss
Margaret Cresswell’s death. It appears the
mother was at the ’Varsity Commencement,
and a flapper sister at some college sports.
Miss Margaret was overcome by gas-fumes
when taking a bath, and was dead long be-
fore her returning relatives found her.
Mount Lyell manager Sticht has lodged
himself and his family at Coonac, Toorak,
for a lengthy stay. The very best paste-
boards are pouring steadily in.
Mrs. Dick Casey, after boarding for a term
with the. Carmichaels at Bombay, went to
London in time for the Brodribb wedding.
Miss Noel’s sacrificial garment was a most
expensive affair of lace and ermine. The
Mars Buckley’s mansion was used for the
celebration.
A good old female soul —in the person of
Miss Clara Murton—passed out, aged 89, at
one of the Old Colonists’ Homes, Melbourne,
the other week. She had paid for the build-
ing of the Home she died in; also, two other
cottages on the estate were endowed by her.
Miss . Murton’s humanitarianism was both
practical and otherwise, for whilst she gave
some of her income to the China Inland Mis-
sion under the delusion that it was
a blessed thing for the Chows, she
maintained a Melbourne suburban mis-
sion hall for the benefit of local
heathen. How much more sweetly pathetic
was the end of this ancient spinster, who
toddled at last into one of her own alms
houses to find sympathetic companionship,
than that of a wheezy decrepit maiden lady
who. hangs tenaciously to her personal pos-
sessions. and dies in an odor of Toorak gen-
tility disrespected as a dreary old nuisance
by all who knew her!
Ethel Hart, late “sister” in the Melbourne
Children’s Hospital, has been appointed
matron of the Foundling Hospital at Rich-
mond. She is young, 6ft. high, and with a
physique in keeping, and popular with chil-
dren ; so the odds are, she should do well.
The recorded failures of matrons with her
qualifications are infinitesimally small in
number.
Gladys Taylor, one of Melbourne’s rather
numerous slie-lawyers, had the glory of being
the first woman in the State to personally
conduct a case. (In this matter Maoriland
is ahead, for Miss Ethel Benjamin, barris-
teress, made her debut long ago). Other Vic-
torian law-ladies have appeared in court in-
structing a barrister, but Gladys is the first
to ha.ve.got right at the business of cross-
questioning witnesses and addressing the
court. The case, a money dispute, was ad-
journed, so I can’t tell you this week if
Gladys won. or not. However, she showed
plenty of spirit. The party who was being
proceeded against began by treating her to
some impudent back-talk. When he was
enlightened as to the status of the lady, he
commented brokenly, “I didn’t know ’er
from Adam. I never knew no ladies to be
lawyers.”
Mrs. Maud Downie, sister of manager
Johnny. Smith, of. the Royal, made quite a
flutter in the family circle last week by de-
liberately marrying a Scotsman named Bryce,
and departing aboard the Macedonia for
Scotland. It is some years since she called
upon the law to break her first shackles.
Parson Cranford, of Brighton, married his
own daughter last week to Mr. Frank Wilson,
of Broken Hill. Everyone wore white, except
the officiating parent, the beadle and a few
others. The bridegroom and the best man, as
well as the bridegroom’s brother Victor (who
did the giving away) were all clad in suits of
snowy linen. One advantage of this depart-
ure from hoary custom lay in the fact that
the wearing of top or plug hats was obvi-
ated. The proper conduct of a top hat is an
art that can be achieved only by men of
complete leisure. The average strenuous
young Australian or American looks as ill
at ease in this garment as he would if he
were forced to haste to a wedding clad in a
halberd and battle-axe. After a jaunt to
Healesville, the newly-weds will face the
heat and Mary-Anne-lessness of the Great
Silver Minery.
The youngest daughter of F. W. Heath
(the profound scientist who calculates how
many minutes, seconds and fractions of
seconds the Melbourne Gup winner takes to
hasten round Flemington Racecourse)—the
daughter of this eminent man, I say, is to
be married next week to Harold Darling,
grandson of John Darling, the big wheat
merchant of Adelaide. He is in the ancestral
business. She is chiefly famed for her peer-
less complexion.
Bartley Dinsmore’s bride, who was Ettie
Ellis, one of seaside St. Kilda’s smartest
girls, struck a new note in wedding dresses.
It was completely woven in rose-point lace
and edged with ermine. In the days before
the big St. Kilda places started to transform
themselves into hasheries, the Dinsmore man-
sion was of some importance. It stood right
on the Esplanade. First, the Royal Yacht
Club annexed it, and now it has fallen a peg
or two lower. It has been invaded by caf6s
below and flats above, while the “Dandies”
chortle on the back lawn.
Speaking of the decadence of the Big
House reminds one of the complete fall of
Fitzroy-street, St. Kilda, once a stronghold
of our very richest. Mrs. Exclusive Board-
ing-Housekeeper has long been in possession
of nearly all the residential bricks-and-mor-
tarages in the street. Two of the absolutely
last have lately capitulated. Godfrey, M.L.C.,
is digging out from his stone pile and the
next-door Beauchamps are putting their own
private lares and penates under the hammer
of the Firm, and letting their home go to its
inevitable fate.
Mrs. Reed, a widowed sister of Lady
Doughty, whp was once this paper’s Mel-
bourne Chatter, is to be married to one
Alexander Stewart, of Edinburgh. He is
wealthy, much-travelled, and of such high
lineage that he couldn’t get down without
breaking his neck. A relative who was
known to the police and others as Charles
Stuart had his body amputated, and the
operation was a complete success. Another
(also Charles) hid himself up a telegraph
post, or something, after an exciting argu-
ment at Worcester. A third Charles (the
Bonnie one) wandered through the Scotch
Highlands in petticoats, and seriously compro-
mised the good name of Miss F. Macdonald,
and afterwards took to drink, which he
bought on credit. The family has been
dogged back to one Alan, who was a French
landowner over 800 years ago. Presumably
(from the spelling) the present Stewart
comes of a crowd which branched off in dis-
gust, or for other reasons, before Mary’s
time. Until then the Scottish royalties were
Stewarts of Renfrew, but by Mary’s marriage
with a tottering human weed named Henry
Stuart, they became Stuarts of Lennox.
Neither spelling brought them much luck.
It is said that that favorite boarding-house
dish called stew was named after them, but
this is quite uncertain.
Another sister, Miss “Peggy” Stone, is to
bestow herself shortly on Ernest Whiting.
He is the only mariner in a strongly clerical
family. His grandfather was Archbishop of
the Mauritius, and countless relatives have
been Anglican curates, deans, etc., while
some have actually achieved the distinction
of being eaten by tropical savages. The
’groom proposes to abandon the sea after
marriage, buy a macintosh, umbrella and
goloshes,. and start a shorq life in England.
Thus will the Australian family of Stone
be utterly dispersed. There will be two
sisters in England, one in Scotland, and a
married brother in Canada.
There were points of unconscious humor
about the Chaucer Pageant at Melbourne
Town Hall —some of them supplied by Lady
Denman’s Guard of Honor. Headmaster
Sugden, of Queen’s (the college which is
anxious to add a Woman’s Hall to its back
premises) in cap and gown, was on the
Town Hall steps. Near him stood his wife,
in a wimple (which, in case you haven’t
struck one, I may describe as a kind of
facial winding-sheet or shroud), and daugh-
ter Ruth, in a celery-green robe. Also, ad-
jacent were Mrs. President Avery, with
yards of mosquito curtain flowing from an
inverted funnel, which shot starkly up from
her highly-intelligent brow, and secretary
Jean Brown, whose dark thatch made a pre-
carious resting-place for a bilious-hued ar-
rangement like a pill-box. All these helped
earnestly with the receiving. Her Ex., in a
trained black and white garment, grasped a
posey of pink roses. Secretary Vernon,
looking over a tall white fence, was in at-
tendance. In the hall Mrs. Administrator
Gilruth sat at the Denman elbow, wearing a
circlet of feathers round her head, both
above and below. John Madden’s dame,
Warden Mclnerney and a few purple-decked
professors looked out from a pack of faces on
a tottering Norman castle keep, sadly in
need of a coat of paint. After the speeches
a procession of Canterbury Tale characters
marched round. Motor veils were prevalent
with girls young and old. The men mostly
ignored the footwear popular in rare old
Geoff’s time, and affected boots of flamingl.v
American origin. Abernethy, as Richard
11., walked with Queen Vera Deakin at the
head. Over each stall fluttered a twisted-
looking sign. The Denman crest, “a bird
hanging on to a ring,” was next-door neigh-
bor to a loose-jointed Angel. A well (where
a golden wish was supplied for sixpence),
and a towering cross, held the centre of a
modern market place. When the official
part of the job was through a horde of she-
brigands settled like a cloud upon the helpless
lieges, and extorted divers moneys from them,
practically by force majeure. This was about
the most genuinely Early English feature of
the entire show. The pilgrims made a fam-
ished rush for the Tabard Inn when their
journey was over. There was also a strong
flavor of realism about this, methought.
The Warriwee Club opened its new pre-
mises to a packed house of members. Presi-
dent Staughton (in an aggressive velvet
toque, with two sportive plumes sprouting
from its rear) made a few confidential re-
marks, whilst the feathers were tickling her
neck. Mrs. Ted Bage, wearing her usual
well-brushed appearance, exchanged opinions
with Bob Chirnside’s wife, slim Mrs. Lang
making an attentive third. Mrs. Medico
Lynch smiled gaily under a broad lid, and
Mrs. Whitelaw, in black attire, contributed
to the prevailing enthusiasm,' so to speak. In
the musical programme, Madame Betty
Brook succeeded in raising a smile with a
whimsical trifle, and Gertrude Healey really
fiddled her audience into silence, a fine
achievement under the circumstances. George
Cowley, the solitary male present, was in
strong demand, hence the suspicion that the
resolution to make men eligible for members
will presently be carried by a big majority.
Douglas Richardson and Margaret Basker-
ville have next call upon the art gallery at
the Athenaeum Hall —not for sculptors, but
for paintings. Their joint picture-show opens
on Friday (18th), and will keep the walls
furnished until May 1.
Soul-specialists Smith and Robins, to-
gether with the financial and working props
of the Young Men’s Christian Association,
were garden-partyed on Saturday afternoon
by the Harpers, at their Toorak cot. Mrs.
Robert, temporarily disabled from active
service, was unable to do the honors, so
Mrs. Crosbie stepped into her shoes and sup-
ported the host in pump-handling the
solemn-jawed, stove-piped males and their
expensive-looking women folk, whilst a band
on the lawn played rag-time melodies. An
adjournment for speeches was made to the
crimson ballroom. Brother Smith, decor-
ated with a chaste white flower, and distil-
ling Yankee evangelism from every pore,
proved the tallest talker.' Though only a
24-hours-old resident, he has discovered at
least one of our plague spots in an anti-
quated Y.M.C.A. building, which, consid-
ered as a means for luring local youths from
mere worldly pleasures, isn’t worth a cent.
To make virtue a family habit, parents were
exhorted to pour in contributions. Smith is
a sleek, well-oiled showman, handicapped
slightly by a top-note which cracks in mo-
ments of intense emotion. Partner Robins
is an apologetic-looking individual, with
nervous hands, which he wears folded. The
male quartet, who travel in the wake of the
soul-rousers, rendered vocal items about “the
high being brought low.” Robert Harper
listened wearily, holding his head in his
hands, whilst his opulent commercial
brethren sat round wearing stolid faces.
Being a fairly high lot themselves, they
contemplated their approaching lowness with
no enthusiasm to speak of. Tea and
buns, mingled with roses and delphinium,
were served in the dining-room. Secretary
Clack, Chairman Greenwood, and a son of
the departed W. T. Stead assisted to clear
the board. Meanwhile the band on the
lawn persisted in giddy melodies. “For You
are all Married Men” played the sedate
company off the premises.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Our Governor went to Ballarat (Vic.) on Monday,
to stay with Admiral Bridges. With him are his
daughter Alicia and Captain Fletcher. Lady Bosan-
quet and the remaining twin are still rusticating at
Port Lincoln. When they all re-assemble it will be
at North Terrace, and if there is not shortly after-
wards a perfect orgie of foundation-stone laying and
the like, I, for one, my dear, will be surprised.
The Louis Wilsons have gone for a six months
j'aunt to England, taking Vancouver and Amer.ca
generally on the way.
The Koonunga Estate was passed in on Monday
at £lO 15s. an acre. In 1907 the late Mr. Toms
bought the property for £7 2s. 6d. an acre, and put
in a lot of time and cash improving it. His total
wealth is sworn not to exceed £25,000.
Mrs. Acres, who has been spending the summer at
Mount Barker with her parents, Mr. and Mrs. Barr
Smith, is at the Grand Central with her daughter,
Miss Hawker. They both return to England next
week. Miss Hawker and Miss Joan Giles (who is also
off) are being affectionately larewelled on all sides.
Mrs. Tom Barr Smith had a dance for them on
Wednesday night at Birdsgate, and Mrs. Fisher gave
a similar entertainment on Friday.
Miss Kathleen O’Dea, who left by the Mongolia,
was feted through the medium of an Exhibition Build-
ing concert on Saturday night. The guest, who was in
a frock of mingled white and pink, sang well herself.
The same boat robbed us of Miss Acraman and her
sister, Miss Hogarth and Miss Von Doussa.
The Autumn Flower Show occurred at the Exhibi-
tion on Thursday. There were only four decorated
dinner tables, and Miss Babbage’s cool arrangement
of blue and maiden-hair fern caught the .judge’s eye.
Miss Isabel Hague was a close second. The roses
were glorious, and some of the cactus dahlias would
have been winners anywhere. From the late Mr.
Caley Smith’s garden came some gorgeous peony
dahlias, and gladioli which had achieved a purple
almost black in its intensity and richness. Mr.
Smith was a keen and persevering experimentalist
and horticulturists will miss him and his work.
Mrs. Herbert, of Papua, is on a visit to her sister,
Mrs. Dr. Evans, at Hindmarsh.
Artist H. S. Power, who has just returned from
seven years work'in England and Europe, is enthusi-
astic on the’ subject of Australian artists abroad.
John Longstaff’s work he considers is at its best
this year, and Fred Leist and Harold Parker are also
doing well, while from America come good reports of
Hayley Lever.
Dr. and Mrs. “Mark” Symons, who have been in
England for four years, returned to Adelaide on
Saturday.

POLITICAL
POINTS
About the most interesting contribution
to the Hop Poon Gooey discussion was the
letter of one Arthur Litchfield, of Cooina
(N.S.W.), who wrote that he felt inclined
to “sink into the earth with shame because
Hop P. G. was at last to be made to observe
the law. Well, why shouldn’t he sink? It
would at least be a cheap form of burial,
and if he sank “with shame,” that would be
about the most inexpensive thing he could
take with him. A more reckless man might
sink with tuppennce, and in the present
strained condition of the money market, the
coin would be missed. So let Arthur sink
with shame —or old boots or. applause, or
anything else that won’t be missed.
THE CAUSE OF POLITICAL UNREST.
The Prime Minister contends that the Referendum
is a national question, and, therefore, ought to be
exempt from the ordinary party tests. How can this
be so when it is introduced solely for the purpose of
furthering the aims of one party in opposition to
those of the other.... What is of more consequence
from the national standpoint than whether the Labor
party or the Liberal party shall be in the ascendant ?
Sydney TELEGRAPH.
FOREMAN FISHER: " But what's your griev-
ance? You agree with me that if we don’t
get the proper machinery we can't turn out
the work.”
JOE: “Yes, but that’s only a secondary con-
sideration. The real point is whether I’m
to draw pay as foreman instead of you!”
The opening of the Chow Parliament must
have been indeed a dreadful sight. There
were present nearly 700 members of the
new Legislature of the childlike and bland
republic, and nearly all wore frock coats and
plug hats. It is a dreadful thing to see even
one belltopper on top of one politician out
in the middle of a desert, but 700 of them
together were calculated to scare seven
devils out of the land of Han.
When they leave off talking in a large,
vague way and come down to details, there
isn’t one of them that doesn’t yell for Uni-
fication and Reconstruction. For instance,
in Westralia certain new Food and Drug
Regulations are to come in force in May,
whereupon the Western Mail :
Drastic action by a single State would seriously
disturb trade interests, without effecting any public
benefit, and, apart from the question whether the
regulations referred to are necessary or not, the need,
for uniformity throughout the Commonwealth ts
obvious.
Of course the need for uniformity in a hun-
dred things throughout the Commonwealth
is obvious; but when anyone mentions the
one way by which the obvious uniformity
can be produced—the way by. which the
United Kingdom produces uniformity in
Cornwall and Cork and Caithness stupid
paints like the Western Mail shriek
“Socialism!” and reach for their umbrellas
to drive the ravenous beast off the doorstep,
their purpose being to shove the gamp into
the animal, and then open it, so. that the
whalebone ribs will fill the interior of the
creature and utterly destroy it. . And that
ravenous creature isn’t within miles of the
doorstep.
“Yoob Et” writes :
If the awful new President that Uncle Sam has
elected carries out his purpose of granting “inde-
pendence” to the Philippines, there will almost cer-
tainly be big trouble close enough to Australia s
door to give this country a feeling as if cold feet
were walking down its back. The talky, fussy Fill-
pino will be recognised as master of the country.
He already has a Parliament, and a plug hat with a
feather in it, and thinks himself innumerable pump-
kins. The ignorant, but infuriated Mohammedan
Morro will proceed to cut the throat of this educated
but superficial person. The handful of white people
will refuse to be ruled by the brown folk, and will
take the situation in both hands, and start a re-
public, and then put the country up to auction
among those European Powers that seem best able to
supply a capable garrison. The Jap will interfere in
the interests of peace and in his general capacity as
boss of the northern Pacific. And then there will be
breakages—so many breakages that the crash of fall-
ing crockery will be heard from China to Peru.
A beautiful yarn is going round the Fed-
eral offices apropos the latest scheme of a
humor-loving official for making life in the
Northern Territory “bearable.” He pro-
poses, in short, the establishment of maisons
de joie for the exiles, and is alleged to be
so wildly enthusiastic about the importa-
tion of Rahab from Jericho and Delilah
from Philistia that he has even written an
official minute on the sub-
ject. If this is so, ’Siah
Thomas must have been too
startled even to Wowse dur-
ing the last few weeks. And
when ’Siah Thomas ceases to
groan heavily in the taber-
nacle things are indeed being
shaken up.
Joe Cook’s recent speech,
in which he once again an-
nounced his intention of
sinking the fiscal question
that won’t stay sunk, has
put his Victorian supporters
in an awkward hole. Some
of them, like Palmer and
Hans Irvine, don’t worry, as
they represent purely farm-
ing constituencies. But to
men like Fairbairn, who is
trying to win a city seat,
and Manifold, who is stand-
ing for Corangamite (which
has manufacturing towns in
it), Joe’s -attitude is the
devil. The Age is boosting
High Protection for all it is
worth, and has made it a
very live issue in Victoria;
and now that Fisher has
definitely pronounced in
favor of this policy, the Labor
men are going strong on it.
Joe Cook’s speech will thus
probably cost Fairbairn and
Iceberg Irvine their seats,
and should win Geelong again
for Labor. For Geelong,
with its woollen mills, is intensely Austra-
lian, and has in the Advertiser the oldest
Protectionist paper in Victoria.
Some last-possibility-in-the-way-of-a-fool
proposes that the Freetrade United Kingdom
should be fixed up with a law making the
minimum wage 30s. per week. Considering
that the average wages on the Irish rail-
ways are less than £1 a week, and that the
minimum is under 10s., and that the Irish
railways pay none too well even under these
conditions, and that this isn’t, by any means,
the worst case of sweating in the British
islands, the 30s. minimum is a large propo-
sition. It is reckoned that it would cost the
country £200,000,000 per annum. A Free-
trade country lives by free competition and
by being cheaper than any of its rivals, and
that isn’t a good place for a legal minimum
wage.
The Sydney Telegraph, in a pathetic
and only somewhat grammatical note on tne
disappearing Chinaman, states that the num-
ber of Chows in N.S.W. has fallen in about
12 years from 11,200 to 7600, and wants to
know why white men don’t turn vegetable
Johns. The paper emphasises the tact that
“The industry of being a Chinese gardener
is about the best protected industry m exist-
ence.” Now, why doesn’t every white man
with a craving for the soil become a Chinese
gardener?
AN UNDEFINED PLANK FOR A
DEFINITE PURPOSE.
He was glad to say that the two great Liberal
parties in the Federal and State Parliaments were
welded together by a common platform. He hoped
that these relations would be applied to some pur-
pose, to defeat the machinations of the Labor party
in the coming campaigns.— C. G. Wade, at Chats-
wood.
Though nominations in connection with
the Federal elections do not close until May
2, both the Labor and “Liberal” parties
have practically decided upon their candi-
dates in New South Wales. Labor will con-
test every seat except Hume (where Oom
Bill Lvne is to be unmolested) and North
Sydney (which is admitted to be Tory-satur-
ated). “Liberalism” opines to fight shy of
the Barrier and Newcastle, anyhow. One
reasons for dodging forlorn-hope constitu-
encies is that without a poll there will
be less enthusiasm among the electors,
and consequently less votes recorded for
the Referenda—an obvious party advantage
in a hostile electorate. And party advan-
tages are extremely important in Australia
where the nation is more or less a side-issue.
MR. WADE'S FIXTURES.
Both feet , as far as political progress is
concerned.
Japan is threatened with an idea. In its
brown mind it has devised the proposition
that every employer should, at the end of the
year, divide a certain proportion of his profits
among those of his workmen who have been
in his employment for the whole of the pre-
ceding 12 months and have done nothing to
cause a stoppage of work. This scheme,
which was devised by Pooh Bah. the Premier,
with the earnest assistance of Treasurer Pish
Tush, is now before the alleged Parliament.
It is meant to obviate strikes, and it cer-
tainly should obviate something. Thus if an
agile agitator persuades the staff of a big
factory to strike all except one man, that man
will, presumably, get the whole bonus. And
when it is discovered that he is the agitator s
little brother, and that the two are dividing
the profits, the strikers will probably obviate
both of them with a lethal weapon some dark
night.
“Packer’s” unholy exposure :
Just to show you the superiority in the methods
of Liberalism as compared with Labor in New
South Wales, as far as electioneering is concerned,
let me mention the doings at the Glebe (Sydney).
In 1910, James Alexander Hogue, ex-Minister under
Reid and Carruthers and Wade, was dashed into
outer darkness by Laborite Keegan. He was only
dashed by 136 votes in a poll of 8366, but it
finished him. This was to have been expected, but
James Tremendous Hogue and some of his friends
(or enemies) professed to be mightily surprised.
The surprise on that occasion, however, was out-
done recently when they discovered that local Lib-
erals were endeavoring to jettison the ex-Minister
James Innumerable Hogue, and run another candi-
date in the interests of the party at the forth-
coming elections. Of course, the local league must
select the candidate, and a little time ago that body
was galvanised into life by persons who steadily paid
in a shilling and demanded membership. This was
the callous mob getting ready for the unsuspecting
James Universal Hogue; but the procession of bobs
became so noticeable that even James observed it.
And then there was hurrying to and fro. When the
secretary had time to breathe he found 1780 new
members on his list and breathless candidates still
hurling others through the door. The “Liberal”
press sees in this “gratifying evidence of awakened
enthusiasm irt the 'Cause” ; but we know better. If
the Other Man gets 901 and we get only 900 the
Cause can go to the bowels of the ash-barrel — we’re
gone a million. Still, as I said before, our methods
are not to be compared with the unspeakable de-
vices of low wharf lumpers and such. If you have
any doubts ask James Interminable Hogue.
The “Liberal” selections in New South
Wails for the coming Federal fight indicate
that the party had no embarrassment of
capability to draw on. Three (Fegan
Fleming and Conroy) are faded rejects, and
two (Scott and Orchard) are successful re-
tail tradesmen on trial. The rest are local
men with alleged “popular followings.” Nor
does it look as if any of the new men nomin-
ated by Labor may unload a Minister’s port-
folio.

bluffed out by the suf-
fragette women who
refuse food in gaol.
Says he : “If one were
allowed to die, scores
would follow them.
These fanatics fear

The N.S.W. Cabinet is going to be called
the Scabinet in future by reason of the way
it has turned dog on Labor principles.
Joe Cook is not to have another walk-over
in Parramatta. The chosen Labor candidate
is O.T). Zieman, a studious young man who
is what Cook can’t ever be expected to be,
in spite of his large capacity for somer-
saults —a young and almost passionate Aus-
tralian patriot, well read, and a straight
Protectionist. Barrister Shannon, nowa-
days fruit-growing in the Parramatta dis-
trict, who had been mentioned as Joe’s most
likely ouster, has accepted nomination for
the North Sydney seat —the one in which
tne nowadays unneard-of Dugaid Thomson
sleeps soundly.
The Liberal ladies who have been looking
for a right-thinking person to drive the
“impossible” Dr. Billy Maloney out of his
Melbourne seat have had wretched luck.
The other day they thought they had found
It, and they invited It to speak to them
nicely at their club. It went, and after a
solid hour’s talk about Protection and other
disappointing things It asked whether any
lady would care to ask any questions. After
a painful interval one very naice Liberal
lady ventured on this : “Are you in favor of
the —ah —baby bonus grant V” Then the
bomb arrived. “Yes, absolutely, whether
the child is born in wedlock or not!” Too-
rak and South Yarra had such hysterics
that next day that candidate withdrew his
name from the Liberal ticket.
“A Man Who Voted Labor” writes:—
If I didn’t know that Attorney-General Hughes
was an abstainer, I would have suspected him of beer,
lush, dog’s nose, blue ruin and mixed breweries when
he made that amazing statement that the Labor
party was not bound by the Prime Minister’s declara-
tion in favor of Protection. This idea that the
Labor party is bound to nothing but the published
platform, as decided upon by the Federal Conference,
is blither. It is imbecility and ash-barrel and old
boots. The Prime Minister of Australia is too big a
man to play a game of promises which do not bind
his party. The people do not elect the Labor people
on the platform as decided by the Conference, but on
the policy put forward by the Prime Minister as
chief representative of the crowd in power. The
great bulk of the people who vote Labor know no-
thing of the platform of the party except as it is
expounded by the Prime Minister and reported in the
press. If that huge mass of “sympathetic voters"
once understands that the party is not bound by the
Prime Minister’s policy speech, the said party will
be of little use to them. It should be understood,
once and for all, that the Labor Conference and the
Political I.abor League and the Caucus are irrespon-
sible institutions. They are amateur affairs and ex-
crescences and dead cats. The country recognises the
Cabinet as represented by the Prime Minister, and the
Legislature, and if anything outside these authorities
makes too much fuss it will be obliterated.

OUR MENTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A remarkable feature of modern public life is the
intense dislike of the wisdom born of experience that
is displayed by extreme factions like the Labor
party. The people need disinterested guidance, poli-
tical leaders of the great parties in the past used to
make a point of consulting specialists—the success-
ful banker, manufacturer, merchant and landowner.
—Argus, 22/3/’l3.
Alone we are not qualified
(By “we” I mean the mob
Or multitude) to come to a
Decision, in a proper way,
On anything. That job,
Requiring strength and breadth of mind,
Must to our betters be resigned.
I ask you, why should numbers rule?
’Tis most absurd to count
All stomachs, disregarding size ;
The large of girth are always wise!
Those who are paramount
In commerce, and who own the land,
Own, too, the wit to understand.
Large questions —these must baffle us.
If we should be bereft
Of squatters, bankers and the crew
Of wise importers, who, oh! who
To guide us would be left ?
In politics and things like that
We have to put our trust in Fat!
VICTORIA.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Though only a very junior cricketer,
Ramsay Davidson’s feat, when playing recently for
a Williainstown .(Vic.) team against one from Fitz-
roy, is worth noting. The Fitzroy lads were two
short, and Davidson polished off the whole side
(eight wickets, clean bowled) in nine balls for 0
runs. As his mate bowled two maidens, the whole
side went out in 21 balls for 0. Davidson bowls
right arm, fairly fast and with an accurate length,
and last season collared 52 wickets for an average
of 5.19 runs. Williamstown Senior Club has offered
him a place, but he prefers the pleasant irresponsi-
bility of recreative games to the strenuous endeavor
of pennant cricket.
* * * *
The end of the 13th round of Sydney First
Grade Cricket happened on Saturday afternoon in
perfect weather. The public interest was concen-
trated on the match between University and Red-
fern, which were dead-level in the second place in
the competition—three points behind North Sydney.
Of course, had there been any chance of North Syd-
ney biting the dust in its flare-up with Paddington,
there would have been some excitement; but Pad-
dington had started badly, hitting up only 163 ; and
North felt in no way alarmed. Its confidence was
justified, for with Moore’s 73, Hordern’s 42 and
Poidevin’s 27, 211 were hoisted for the loss of eight
wickets, leaving North on top with two wickets and
48 runs on the first innings. When stumps were
drawn on the previous Saturday University had biffed
431 for the loss of nine wickets. It let things stay
at that, and started on the job of running Redfern
through two innings if possible. But the way Rix,
one of the opening pair, treated the bowling, made
the prospect somewhat blue. If the rest of Red-
fern had played the same sort of game, University
would have wanted all its huge score, for though
batsmen came and went, Rix went on vigorously till
105 appeared opposite his name. University got
desperate, and threw in 16 sundries to show it was
getting excited. But the rest of the team was not
up to Rix, and the side vanished for a miserable
180. Massie had cut the tail to pieces, raking in
five wickets for 24. The follow-on started at 4.15,
and Uni. looked anxiously at the light. Was there
time? Single and Minnett, who had more or less
failed with the ball in the first innings, were taken
off, and the job left to Stack and Massie. Redfern
could not face the combination. Also, the Redfern
tail was obviously and painfully down. Only two
men —White and Hayes —reached double figures,
making 15 and 11 respectively. Six men went with-
out notching a run, and the group included Rix, the
lion of the first innings—but then that was to be
expected after his great effort. The whole side was
outed for 40, Massie taking six wickets for 19 and
Stack four for 17. It left University with_ a three-
point win by an innings and 211 runs. It’s a long
while since a serious competitor for the premiership
got its ear rubbed in the gutter in this fashion. This,
of course, elevates University to the position of
runner-up, and lessens the distance between it and
North Sydney to two points’. For the rest, Sydney
beat Petersham on the first innings by 18 runs, the
figures being 213 and 195.' Petersham’s best scorer,
Norton, had the dead luck to be only 94 when his
companion, the ninth man (the 10th being absent),
was bowled. Burwood inflicted a somewhat severe
castigation on Waverlev at the latter’s park. Bur-
wood had left 306 on the board, and AVaverley could
find only 112 towards repayment. Following on, it
rushed up another 101 for the loss of four wickets,
so Burwood had to be content with a first innings
win by 194. Still, it was enough. Glebe just man-
aged to crawl out on top of Central Cumberland,
making 277 to the 268 of the Parramatta team.
Gordon’s victory over Middle Harbor was a compara-
tively tame affair of 47 runs, the place Mr. Wade
represents making 246 to the 199 of the place that
sends Dr. Arthur to Parliament. The missing ninth
round—it went astray through untoward and seasonal
events —will be started next Saturday.
Jim Sullivan, the English middleweight,
made a holy show of the tough, plucky Tasmanian
fighter, Reg. Midwood, on Saturday night. Midwood
will be long remembered in Sydney as the man who,
after being grossly battered for a number of rounds,
and finally knocked down four times in the last round
bv Ercole de Balzac, got up and knocked his
smasher out. Midwood that night created a reputa-
tion for gameness that will take some demolishing.
But on Saturday night, he was pitted against a man
far above him in class—a fighter as cool as an ice-
berg, abnormally strong, armed with a straight left
and a quick inside cross that was as deadly as sin.
Midwood lasted 10 rounds, and the towel was thrown
t > the four winds just in time to save a knock-out.
Exactly how many rounds Midwood would have
lasted‘had not Sullivan damaged his left wrist and
thumb-joint in the first round is hard to say. This
writer’s opinion is that he would not have got past
the third. Sullivan is a peculiar fighter in every
respect, and a peculiarly effective one. His strength
is terrific. Again and again Midwood clinched for
safetv, and Sullivan swung him off and sent home
the inside right cross as he shook him off. He had
only one hand to fight with, for with his left
he could only bluff, bilt he bluffed till the cows
came home. Half the time Midwood was in the ring
he was taking counts. Not a round passed that he
didn’t go down several times, and of the number of
his failings the scribes lost count. It was a para-
lysing* sort of battle, and the onlookers grew weary
of hearing the monotonous “1, 2,3, 4,” etc. He
was game as they make ’em, but just as in real
warfare, courage counts for nothing if there is no
skill and force to back it up, and in spite of his
virtues, Reg. Midwood was a crude amateur com-
pared to this polished artist from Britain by way of
the States. Still, Sullivan has his drawbacks. When
he scores and his man is dizzy Sullivan does not
follow up, and, by raining a shower of blows on his
opponent, end him. He deliberately waits for him
to recover. It is ideal glove work, but it isn t
boxing as understood in these latter days. Sullivan
is a better sportsman than fighter, but a cold, cruel
world will knock that chivalry out of him. Then
Sullivan will be a great fighter. Of course, it may
be that he didn’t follow up on Saturday night owing
to his damaged fist. Anyhow, he’s a fine, clean,
manly artist of scrupulous fairness, who is certain
to become exceedingly popular in the near future.
Consider the Sydney University athlete R.
J. Massie, and the parts thereof. As a cricketer he
gathered 33 wickets at an average of 10.33 in
Svdnev First Grade competition this season, lie
gathered the ’Varsity heavy-weight boxing cham-
pionship by the simple process of showing himself,
whereupon the opposition just faded away. ne
turns the beam at over 15st., and as he is among
the entrants for the coming Australian Amateur
Championship tourney, there’s a bad time in store
apparently, for someone. # #
Petty Officer Curran, who is shortly to
meet William Lang, of Melbourne, arrived in Sydney
last week, and, with Sid Burns, was received into
the pugilistic fold with the usual gastronomic cere-
monies. Curran commenced his boxing career while
serving on the Australian Souadron some years ago.
Among his victims are lan Hague and Gunner Moir,
and his last fight with Lang he won on a foul. Poor
Lang' lost his block entirely on that fatal occasion,
saw red, and while Curran was down-on one knee he
smote somewhere about five times, and most of the
smites arrived. Curran was one of the men who
arrived at Ladysmith by that last train that brought
the 4.7 guns to the besieged township. He was with
the gun ; so he has shed more than nose gore in his
country’s cause.
* * * *
Adelaide has been smiling over a state-
ment that some of the horses taking part in Oakbanlc
events were blessed by priests before they ran. Arch-
bishop O’Reily, however, has told the local Mail
(5/4/’l3) that, whether it is true or not, there is
nothing to smile at. His Church has forms of prayer
officially sanctioned for use over horses, stables, bees,
birds, butter and beer. Also, when the Mail inter-
viewer feebly inquired, ’’Can a man back horses and
still remain a Christian?” the broad-minded prelate
sat on him with this: “Christianity hap nothing
whatever to do with the business. There is nothing
more unchristian in backing a horse than there is in
eating your breakfast in the morning, or taking off
your boots at night.” Then he added what he knew,
that Corrigan, at the top of his fame as a steeple-
chase jockey, always made his confession just before
mounting, and always wore a scapular in the race;
and added (what he probably didn’t know)—that in
a short time every jockey had bought a scapular, and
every jockey wore one, even Protestants and Jews.
BIFF.
At the Sydney Stadium Reg Midwood, after
going to the boards a dozen times and coming
tip for more, is beaten by Jim Sullivan.
The Australian horse-record for high-
jumping has suffered further damage. In November,
1912, at Horsham (Vic.), Landlock cleared 7ft. Ojin..
beating all previous figures. Last Thursday the
same horse, kept and circus-trained for the business,
left 7ft. lin. of atmosphere under him at the Quirindi
(N.S.W.) Show. Tiger, a Manilla (N.S.W.) prad
that could be easily mistaken for a cart-horse, and
up to that time unknown to the show ring, was
runner-up with 6ft. lOin. The figures of that vener-
able nag Pegasus are somehow not included with the
records. It’s probably because of the vaulting habits
of Peg. that the poets who ride him are so addicted
to asking anybody they meet to spring a tray-bit.
Punters and bookies are at a dead end
-concerning a gallant steed named Audacity, which
was entered for the Newmarket (Vic.) Handicap. After
the V.R.C. had accepted nomination and allotted the
nag a weight, it was discovered (but not before the
brute had been well backed) that it (the brute) was
not properly registered. The upshot was that it
didn’t start. The Victorian Club (the recognised
authority on bets and betting) decided that Audacity
had been a bona fide vehicle for betting, and that
all the gambles stood good. An aggrieved punter
then appealed to the V.R.C., and the committee
decided that as the beast, or brute, was never in
Newmarket Handicap, all bets were off. This airy
disregard of the facts that Audacity was on the
V R.C.’s official entry list, that the entrance fee was
paid and the official handicap awarded, has simul-
taneously annoyed the Victorian Club, Patrick Moses
and the suffering punter. If Patriqk Galilee takes
the money that the club insists must 'be paid he will
be barred bv the V.R.C. Also, if backers don’t pay
up as per the V.R.C. decision, they will be defaulters
in the eyes of the club. So there you are! And
where are you?

Here’s more pluck. Walter Burn, who
rode Master Koran in the Onkaparinga Cup (S.A.),
had his leg broken during the race throU T f * , a it h b °
bumping him. Yet he kept on and finished. Had it been
the case a bishop who broke his leg over a difficult
passage in the middle of a sermon, and ye
on to the finish, this would have been regarded as
real heroism. As it was, the matter was simply
alluded to as “a nasty accident.’’ ,
* *
Saturday was a red-letter day among the
bowlers —the men who trundle unbalanced spheres
of timber along a green sward, at a game which
Drake and the rest played regardless of the fact that
it was legally prohibited. The Pennant of the Syd-
ney bowlers was the prize. Ashfield went up against
City, and after a desperate struggle managed to
crawl out on top by just one point, the figures being
82 to 81. It was a magnificent struggle, which re-
mained in doubt till the very last globe was rolled.
In the first rink the opposing sides scored 24 each.
In the second, City scored 19 to Ashfield s 17, but
reversed it in the third by only making 19 to Ash-
field’s 22. Then, most remarkable of all, the fourth
rink saw them both make 19, so the match was won
bv the difference between second and third rinks.
There was a fine crowd to watch the finish, which
was close and exciting enough to satisfy the most
exacting performer. #
Young George Gray, the billiardist, has
found trouble in Melbourne. The local solicitors for
John Roberts have issued a Supreme Court writ
against Gray for the amount of the judgment and
costs (plus 4 per cent, interest), which the claimant
obtained in the English courts, and whereof the de-
fendant has not yet paid a brown penny. A trifle of
£2307 ss. lid/is the outstanding liability.
* * * *
The rowing carnival which is to decide
the ownership of the N.S.W. State Sculling Cham-
pionship was commenced on Saturday last on the
Parramatta River, that lazy, drifting Shatt-el-Arab
of the Bagdad of Australia. The great event itself
will not blow into the realm of accomplished things
till the afternoon of Saturday next. All Saturday
saw was the Heavy Boat Championship of one mile,
of which two heats were worked off. The first fell to
Bailey, who beat S. Pearce and Starr, and the second
went to A. Pearce, after an exciting scrap with
Mantova, who at one time had a lead of three
lengths. But A. Pearce overhauled him, passed him,
and beat him with a length to spare. It was a
magnificent sprint. In the one-mile double sculls
H, and G. Beach beat Black and Bernie by three
lengths in Bmin. 35jsec. For a quarter of a mile
it was a fine neck-and-neck affair, but after that
the brothers started to creep away, and kept on
doing it to the finish. Next Saturday will see the
finals rowed off, and should provide some excitement.
* * * *
■ The silken bonds of empire are to be fur-
ther strengthened by an invitation to the Thames
Rowing Club (London), to send an eight-oar crew to
Australia. The N.S.W. and Victorian Rowing Asso-
ciations are co-operating in the matter, and if the
visit comes off the crew will take part in contests on
both the Paramatta and the Yarra. A joint com-
mittee has been fixed up to attend to the business
and do the thing properly.
N.S.W. Policeman W. O’Reilly is a youth-
ful veteran athlete who appears to grow more agile
as the winters whirl by. During the past 12 years
O'Reilly has been cock of the walk in hammer and
shot events. At Sydney last week he heaved the
161 b. hammer 133 ft. 3iin. —which is Ift. l£in. beyond
the State record of F. J. Morgan, put up in 1911.
Altogether, O’Reilly won half a dozen events, caber-
tossing. hammer-throwing, shot-putting, etc., and if
he can" only discover a clue he appears to be alto-
gether the sort of cove necessary for the proper
filling of the job of policeman.
* * * *
R. J. McNamara, F. Corry and R. Spears,
who whacked the recent Six Days’ Races at Sydney
and Melbourne, left for Yankeeland last week. They
are probably the finest trio of riders that ever left
the Commonwealth, and should be heard from soon.
Before leaving they swore by the Holy Jumping
Kangaroo that the 1914 Six Days Races would find
them back again—but the lure of the dollar is
mighty, and the chances are that Australia has seen
the last of them. Cyclists can make sovereigns in
Europe and America where they can but scratch up
humble bobs in Australia.
* * * *
After all, there is apparently something
in a name. At the Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) Show last
week, E. J. Clout won the wood-chopping contest.
Has anyone found a promising youth named Biff ?
If so, there might be White Hope possibilities about
him.
* ♦ * *
That particular section of the men who
chase the muddied sphere, known as the Australian
Rulers, made a sudden and desperate plunge' into
the strenuous life on Saturday. While the summer
heat was withering the land a party of reps, met
in some cool hostelry, where the sound of the liquid
flowing from the bottle was made still more soothing
by the splash of a fountain, and arranged for a series
of matches between teams representing the railway
departments of Victoria, N.S.W. and South Australia.
South Oss. met N.S.W. on Saturday last at Adelaide,
and wiped the floor with the Ma Staters by 124 to
74 points. South Australia had not only better
combination, but also showed better individual play.
The Ma State team was just hopelessly inferior, and
that was all there was to be said about it.
Adelaide football, which is carelessly lib-
eral to its players in anything that can possibly be
ranked as expenses, but reckons to steer clear of
direct professionalism, has received a shock from the
plain speaking of Renfrey, captain of Sturt. The
local juniors, he said, were not a good enough reserve
to draw on. “It had cost them a few pounds to get
better footballers to come to the State, and to their
own club, but their efforts would benefit not only
Sturt but the whole of S.A. The committee was not
going to let Sturt go down when it could obtain
men to prevent it from dome so. They would secure
their players, whether they had to go to the North-
ern Territory or to America for them.” (“Oh,
oh,” from members.) Since the meeting the “Oh,
ohs” have come from the other clubs.
* ♦ * *
Great discussion rages among fight-followers
in Sydney as to whom fortune will favor in the Jerry
Jerome-Dave Smith battle next Saturday night. The
man who believes implicitly in the “natural-born
fighter,” wagers his reputation on the aboriginal,
while the chappie who has faith in the trained and
scientific scrapper declares that Smith will eat the
colored brother. Personally, this writer thinks if
Smith can catch the elusive native, colored brother
is in for a licking. Certainly colored brother hll
great agility, and his methods are so unorthodox aa
to be bewildering; but just to the same extent hi*
methods are often wrong.

“Mimosa” : After a long experience of the
coon race, 1 am of opinion that the white man's
skull was r.evei' intended to stand the same rone-h
usage as the black man’s, hence the trouble to
a ‘ White Hope.” What better evidence can von
get than in the old Kimberley and Jo’burg (S’Africa 1
days, when one of the most interesting items on ani
sports’ programme was “the cheese-smashing com
petition.” The competitors, who were generally nisr
gers of the Jack Johnson type, were allowed to make
their run at the cheese from any distance they nre
ferred (generally about a 10 yards’ run). Th f
cheeses were hung against a wall, and the first coon
to break his cheese with his cranium was declared
the winner. The game might have remained a
favorite pastime, but when wooden cheeses were
rung in it wenc kind of flat.

The Prudent King colt, Pilbarra, bred by
Mr. S. Reynolds (N.S.W.), is at present the most
prominent two-year-old racing in Westralia. On
Easter Saturday he annexed the Aidful Stakes with-
out unduly exerting himself, and on the following
Monday romped home in the Sires’ Produce Stakes'
worth £BOO, £5OO of which went to the first horse
and £5O to the breeder. After winning on Satur
day his owner refused 800 guineas for the colt, and
before the start of Monday’s event an offer of 1200
guineas was declined with a crash. This is pro-
bably a record bid for a two-year-old in the West
Pilbarra was purchased as a yearling by his present
owner for 120 guineas.
* * * «
There are various ways of earning the
breezy title of “sportsman,” and the assassination of
sitting wildfowl with shotguns at a range of five
yards is one of them. The duck season opened the
other day. As the first blood-red smear of dawn
appeared in the sky, hand artillery, wielded by gun-
men who had lain concealed for hours, boomed and
blazed round every sheet of water that the Wildfowl
frequent. Feathers flew, and occasionally jnnks and
fragments of birds flew with them, for the unhappy
winged things, in many instances, were dreaming of
nest and mother only a few feet from the polished
gun barrels. It was about as fair a game as Maxim-
ing an unsuspecting holiday crowd from the roof of
an impregnable fortress. Down at Jerilderie
(N.S.W.) the artillerists got so much winged food
that the local appetite for duck was not equal to
the occasion, and heaps of dead birds just had to go
bad and protest to Heaven that way. It is a plain
tale of dull and ruthless murder which lacks the
pomp and picturesque of King George’s Indian circus.
George was on the roof of an elephant like a
suburban church, and his tigers were, oh! so drunk
with bhang! But at least they weren’t such frail
game that they flew to pieces when he hit them, and
there was always a remote chance of one of the in-
ebriated beasts'making a record spring and clawing
off a pound or so of raw elephant. When the smoke
has blown away and the echoes have ceased, a sports-
man should have something more to show for his
spent cartridge than two feathers and a clot.

After an exciting scrimmage, the draughts
championship of Australasia was won by H. Egan, a
Victorian of 25 vears, who was left in the finals
with three N. S. Welshmen. The runner-up, Smith,
of Sydney, is merely 22 years old. Egan’s reward
was £2O.

Yankee land is the Mecca for the Aus-
tralian bikist. The last to be lured thither is
Aubrey Taylor, a lad of 19 and amateur half-
mile champion of Australia. Taylor goes to Salt
Lake Citv, and will there whirl his shins for that
money, the love whereof is alleged to be the root
or basement of all evil.
THE RUBAIYAT OF A GOOGLY BOWLER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ball asks not of him who bowls or throws,
But “cut” or “pulled” as strikes the Batsman goes ;
And he who lifted you clean out for Six,
He knows about it AH, he knows; he knows.
The Moving Fingers round the wide seam fit,
And “off” or “leg” turn to the Ball transmit;
But should you overpitch upon the leg,
The Batsman scores, and that's the end of it.
And that pet ball which, pitched a little high,
Hangs for a space and so deceives the Eye,
What use is it if Mid-on drops the Chance?
’Tis useless then, “Oh, get a bag,” to cry.
I tell you this: When striving foi a Win,
Sometimes I used to ring a "Wrong ’un in,
And beat the Batsman all the way to find
The Wicket-keep bamboozled by the Spin.
So I have learned, who almost was a Star,
It does not matter much how good you are,
Unless your Team supports you on the field,
You might as well be lounging in the Bar.
And so, a simple Child of Circumstance,
Thus, with the Poets, do I break a Lance,
And say to each young Man who takes the field,
Don’t miss a Chance, my Boy; don’t miss a Chance.
