The Inky Way.
The Bulletin lately received copies of two senti-
mental ballads set to music, “Mother’s Answer
Through the ’Phone,” and “Come Back, Dear
Mother.” With each ballad is included a poem, ap-
parently as a sort of literary bonus. “Mother’s An-
swer” is accompanied by “The Watchman’s Story.”
It goes this way:—
I was sitting by my camp fire —
I work on the night shift
My place of work is Coogee
Near a nasty rift.
Whilst gazing at the ocean
A woman passed me by.
I caught hold of her by the arm.
She said: “Oh, let me die.”
“What do you mean?” I asked her,
“Say have you had a tiff ?
By what right do you venture
So near this dangerous cliff'?’”
Then ths distracted female replies in EdtairaMy
plain Etagiish to the effect that the frailor fever she
secretly married, and for whose sake she Iras bean
turned (Bit by her family, has gone to sxa, taking
with him Iter treasured marriage lines! At this
moment, three lights—the signal of the sailor hus-
band's; natiarm—are observed off the coast. The sym>-
pathetic camp-firer telephones for a cab, and the
lady hurries away on the bent knees of a cab horse
towards the vessel’s anchorage. The poem continues:
Eight years have passed and gone, sir,
Since that incident.
To renovate a main, sir,
Again a gang was sent, etc., etc.
Of course, the grateful lady comes along to thank
him, her first words being:—
My husband even went so far
As to advertise for you.
Would that all poets were as explicit! “Come Back
to Mother” has a. flying angel (not one of the South
Melbourne push variety) on the cover; also, a large-
eyed, curly-haired child who wears her hands clasped.
The accompanying poem in this instance is entitled,
“Sunny Joe.” It contains a drunken father —one of
the old-fashioned brand, without which no angel
child is complete. There are also jolly sailors of the
yo-ho-ho sort, and a kind old watchman with snow-
white hair who leaves all his money to the angel
child. Father drinks himself to death, but does so
entirely at his own expense, the angel child not
having yet received his legacy. Both songs refer to
dead mothers, have a strong religious interest, and
are thoroughly depressing. Tosti’s “Good-bye” and
the well-known “Daddy” are also depressing, but not
in quite the same way. The responsible parties are
R. M. Dunbar (words), and A. B. Saunders (music).
The publishing also is credited to “R. M. Dunbar,
Darlington, Sydney.” The Religious Editor, to whom
all songs about Mother are reverently submitted, has
never been to Darlington; but the directory informs
him that the suburb also includes the district of Gol-
den Grove. No poet could desire a happier location
than that. He hopes that Darlington knows and
values its Dunbar. He does not appear to be a mem-
ber of the local council. Evidently he cares not
who makes Darlington’s by-laws so long as he makes
its songs.
The Wowser pushes his gimlet in high these
days. The Tasmanian University Council has
just fallen foul of “Ivanhoe” on the ground of its
immorality, and Scott’s removal from the pale of
respectable literature is threatened. Archdeacon
Whitington is worried about the pained feeling of
a teacher compelled to instruct his class in some
passages of the book, and Father Ivelsh had heard of
a class standing in a Corner and gloating over parts
of it. All of which is very dreadful, and makes it
quite clear that the author of the wicked tale which
has done so much to add to the gaiety of Sunday-
school libraries in the past, must be relegated to
his place among immoral writers like Shakspeare,
Milton, Homer, Dante and the scribes who were re-
sponsible for the Holy Scriptures.
* * * *
“Quarter Single” : There really seems to be justice
in the constant complaints of country deputations
that the local roads are “somethink awful.” Any-
way, the Age, in describing (17/3/’l3) the wrecking
of a furniture van at Kilmore, ended the paragraph
with the remark: “The roadway was strewn with
furniture, blocking the traffic, and later on had to
be stored in the railway sheds.” Presumably it was
unfit for further use.
An extraordinary mistake was made by a North-
ern Rivers (N.S.W.) sub-editor recently. A Sydney
solicitor had got himself into some trouble, and the
Law Institute had applied for a rule nisi calling
upon him to show cause why his name should not be
struck off the roll. The skeleton press-telegram of
the case despatched from Sydney to the northern
paper referfed to “city attorney,” but gave no name.
The fertile mind of the sub, however, fastened on the
idea that “city attorney” meant So and So, the
official City Solicitor, and he printed it so. Addi-
tional interest was imparted by means of a few
details to the effect that the matter arose out of a
divorce court case. It was something joyous in the
way of deciphering “skeletons” —except, of course,
for the solicitor, the sub and the newspaper. The
solicitor deserved most sympathy; but, like a good
sport, he declined to go for damages, contenting
himself with apologies and a trifling donation to two
local hospitals.
G. S. Porter, of Maoriland, claims to be the old-
est printer in Australasia, and, if the year-stone he
passed the other day was the 100th, as he alleged,
no doubt his claim is good. He was born on March
18, 1813, at Norwich (Eng.), according to the
family traditions, migrated to Canada, and played
a double hand at storekeeping and running a small
newspaper near Toronto. About ’54, he moved on
to Australia ; but he did not settle until he reached
Auckland, and he has been there or thereabouts ever
since. # #
“Quarter Single”: If I knew who “Nightowl (B.
13/2/’l3) was, I would buy him seven drinks and a
cocktail, and then shout for him. I, too, very greatly
wish that “young reporters and a few of the snuny
old ones” could be convinced that big words and
roundabout ways of arranging them make bad jour-
nalism. One can pardon a space-grabber, who has
lust sufficient of the English language at his mercy
to fill five sides with drivel about a presentation
(“handsomely illuminated” and “suitably inscribed )
to a J.P. who would be better dead. But stall
men should know better. Yet “coromal inquiry tor
“inquest” is as plentiful as hairs on a horse, and
such stencils as “cast a gloom,” “deserves a word of
praise,” “devouring element,” ‘ gruesome spectacle
and “indefatigable secretary” are as the worm that
dieth not. And why in the name of Horace Greeley
cannot a pressman say “John Smith’ instead ot a
man named John Smith”? If it were a girl named
John Smith, or a deceased wife s sister named John
Smith, the prologue would be worth mentioning.
William Cullen Bryant, the American poet and edi-
tor, driven to desperation by these things, once pro-
duced an index expur gqtorius in which he barred
such abominations as “initiated” for an
“transpired” for “happened —but still the reporter
transpired at every pore. On hot summer days he
transpired so much that he required three collars m
the 24 hours. The ineradicability (oh, blessed space-
grabbing word!) of the habit was turned to advan-
tage by O. Henry in his newspaper story, Callo-
way’s Code,” in which a war correspondent got a
message past the censor by using words which in-
evitably connoted other words, and these other words
formed an intelligible message. Thus foregone
preconcerted—rash— witching” meant, conclusion-
arrangement—act —hour of midnight. Prom this it
was deduced that the attacking army had concluded
arrangements to act at midnight. That story was
the only enjoyable outcome I ever experienced of
the stencil craze, or the shibboleth habit, or what
ever you like to call it. Apropos of rash act for
“suicide,” the only time it was ever acceptably em-
ployed was when a Sydney evening paper put up the
heading, “Barber’s Rash Act.” ‘Barbers Rash
had the first line all to itself and it h ke
ing a joke on a dead man’s tombstone. Probably the
sub-editor who wrote it was the last man to see the
beautiful appropriateness of his heading.
There are a few bright items that look original
even in the little book of “Maxims and Musings, by
the Marchioness Townshend. For instance. Do not
onlv make a fuss with vour husband when you want
him to give you something, and \ ery few good
looking young men see the disagreeable side
women; only good-looking young women see that.
An esteemed Siamese contemporary puts the matter
thus- “The news of English we tell the latest. Writ
in perfect style and most earliest. Do a murder
get commit, we hear and tell of it. Do a mighty
chief die, we bublish it and in borders of sombre
Staff has each one been college, and writ like the
Kipling and tbe Dickens. We circulate every town
and extortionate not for advertisements.
Victorian Police Offences Act, recently consoli-
dated, embodies the old provision prohibiting Publi-
cation or sale of newspapers on Sunday. Registered
newspapers may issue a Sunday edition containing
news of great national or public importance, but not
more than three times in any calendar year So it
anv speculator plans a Sunday newspaper for Mel-
bourne, he must get the Act amended before he fires
awav ; meantime, events of great national consequence
are "urgently requested not to happen oftener than
once every four months.
“Orient”: Again let me claim cable communica-
tion straight with Japan and China, with daily news
telegrams. Keep your globe with Australia on top,
and see who are your neighbors. London telegrams
treat us as if we lived in Putney or Wapping. Here
is a crisis in Japan. What have our papers made
of it? A mere jumble! If Australians don’t want
news from the East, or rather North, let them be
educated up to a healthy craving for it. Apparently,
democrats have won in Tokio ; yet, along comes word
that 30 millions sterling is to be spent on battle-
ships. Explain this to me! The Jap navy is far
more vital to us than to the Cockney ; yet we must
take the Cockney view or nothing. Next proceed to
China. Morrison's Pekin successor drops a peculiarly
interesting letter on “journalism” in the last Times
here. Seventy newspapers are now clattering in
Pekin, with corresponding activity in Shanghai,
Hankow, Canton —everywhere. The corresnondent.
of course, writes for the English market. What we
want is something written for us. He merely hints
that the Jap navy and army are to whin the globe.
There is no censorship, so the press boils as in
the French Revolution. Something must come out of
the seething pot. What?
Michael Cormac Keane (the “M. Cormae” of much
Bulletin and other verse), returns to his native
M.L. cathedral city, to become associate editor of
the Christchurch Press. W. H. Triggs continues
to edit that Tory tribune. His associate, W. H.
Graham —who is big enough for an All Black ’bailer
—has broken down in health, so he falls into the
padded chair of the Press Co.’s evening journal,
which Ernest IJoben had been editing. Huie, whom
Hoben succeeded, is beating up funds to start a third
evening paper in the City of the Plains. “Mick”
Keane wields just about the wittiest pen in M.L.
journalism to-day : his “Casual Chronicles” have been
a feature of the Dominion since that Cabinet-making
paper started. He is, by the way, an M.A. of Can-
terbury College, and took honors on mathematics;
his professor declared him the most brilliant scholar
in that line he had met. And vet he went into
journalism !
Ernest Short, of Parorangi, 100 miles
north from Wellington (M.L.), is a young
man who is liable to do things. A while
back he ventured from inland M.L. across
the ocean to the Argentine Royal Show with
a pen of Romney Marsh sheep to meet the
world’s best breeders. And he got what lie
went after —the world’s championship with
a Parorangi ram. But the venture cost him
£5OOO.
Camperdown (Vic.) Herald has dug up
some verses, written in 1875, by John Far-
rell (of “How He Died”) to celebrate the
laying of the foundation-stone of a
mechanics’ institute. John, who was then
brewing beer for a man named Jackson,
scattered in those times a good deal of ink
over the local Hampden Guardian. But
most of the stuff is dead now. So, for that
matter, is the Guardian ; so is the brewery;
so is Jackson; so is John Farrell. In or
about Farrell’s day. Camperdown had two
napers, the Guardian and the ( iironicle.
E. W. O’Sullivan was on the Chronicle.
Years afterwards both O’Sullivan and
Farrell passed on to the Sydney Telegraph,
one as overseer, the other for a while as
editor, and then and for many years as leader-
writer. Premier Willie Watt’s brother Row-
land came in afterwards when the Guardian
died and the Herald took its place. The
Western squattocrats hadn’t up to then
struck anything like Rowland V att, and
after he had written something simply blas-
phemous about the potentates sacred game
of polo it was reckoned it was time he went.
And he went —to Westralia, where he died.
THE FOUNDLING ON THE DOORSTEP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a discussion by the W.P.A. (not the
W.P.B. but the W.P.A., which means Womens 1 op-
tical Association) in Melbourne recently, concerning
the necessity for securing better representation ot
the Association’s views in the columns of the daily
press. Vida Goldstein and other speakers hit the
Argus severely to leg, Miss Kilkelly going so far as
to wonder “whether that paper ever had a mother
or a sister.”
Oh, Papa !
Have you ever seen the Argus’s mamma?
Is she Sairey Gamp, d’ you think?
Does that paper’s parent drink,
Or gibber with a lunatic “Ha-ha”?
And do you think the passive old resister
Has anything so sprightly as a sister ?
I wonder if the Argus’s mamma
Merely whispered through a keyhole, “Well, ta-ta ! ’
Laid her infant on a door-step, did a two-step or
a four-step,
And hit the trail for distant Panama?
I wonder why the poor abandoned child
So bitterly her mother’s sex reviled?
Did she muse so long and sadly on the ma that
used her badly
That the very name of woman makes her wild?
Does she ever tell the story of her birth?
No ; she doesn’t seem to think the matter’s worth
Interjecting in a column that is stately, staid and
solemn,
As it gently ushers women off the earth.
Oh, Papa!
I’d love to meet the Argus’s mamma!
She might tell me if and why she just left her
child to die,
And how it found its Tory avatar ;
And though she might attempt to draw the cur-
tain,
y e t—her child was on the doorstep, that is cer-
tain !
VICTORIA.
MATERNITY JANE.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. A o
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Bandicoot: You propose to surtax a land-
owner on the difference between the value of his
improvements and the “fully improved” value. How
are you going to decide what is the fully-improved
value (by which you presumably mean the value
that might be created if every possible improvement
was made) ? What, for instance, will be the value
of an acre block at Girilambone when another Lon-
don grows on the top of that place—which may
happen before the improvement possibilities of
Girilambone are quite used up? Waterspout:
When Nature manufactures a wate rspout it <*oesn
form a vacuum, but the gyrations of the al P
a more or less intense rarefaction of the atmosphere
within the column. The whirl agitates the wate
surface, spirals of vapor ascend, and the rarefied ail
condenses this vapor into “spout, which thus creeps
closer and closer to the sea, until occasionally cloud
and sea are connected. ... G.B .8.. Of cou-
read what you’ve got to say. .Whether we 11 print

....Soli: King O’Malley’s shriek about how Captain
(really Lieutenant) Cook “stood on the deck of the
little wooden barque, rocked on the billows of an
uncharted sea, straining his eager, anxious e
finally came the reward—a glimpse of an unknown
world,” was simply muddle and ignorance. Australia
was known two or three centuries before Cook was
born, and it was named New Holland, and to a great
extent charted, and formally annexed long before
Cook saw it. Even the Australian kangaroo was
described by Dutch explorers when Cook s grandiather
was hardly heard 0f... .G.E.C.: Too slight. You
don’t get : the fun you could out of the yarn
Flatfoot: Oh, you’re a despondent cove, you are;
but the chances are the North Shore bridge will be
built before you leam to turn a decent verse
Micmac: Distressing handwriting and vile verse. .. .

we have seen for two days. .. .D.J.8.: Little jig-
gettv-jig rhyme about “Life’s Charity brought

business. Scarcely a line will rhyme or s ean... .
P. H. Hickey: This paper is gratified to be told, m
reference to its views (6/3/’l3), that “the full force
of organised labor” is now guaranteed under the
basis of union proposed in March last. But it is far
from being convinced. When news of the reconcilia-
tion was suddenly fired into the clouds, it was
centred in the garish illumination that “the working
class and the employing class have nothing in com-
mon .... Between these two classes a struggle must go
on until the workers of the world organise as a class
take possession of the earth and the machinery of
production and abolish the wage system. Some of
The Bulletin’s doubts will be removed when the
fragmentary sections of the party decide merely, to
construct a simple, possible and effective political
platform on common ground, and get to work. The
job of taking possession of the earth is vague and in-
definite. and likelv to take a long time, even if no-
body else objected. You further complain: “As to
militarism and conscription, we here (M.L.) know
the infamies perpetrated in the name of defence, and
are not going to sit deaf and dumb thereon even to
win The Bulletin’s approval.” First of all you
have neither militarism nor conscription ; but if it
be the infamies of compulsory citizen defence that
you refer to, you may easily ascertain the mildness
thereof compared with the infamies that recently
befel Korea where citizen defence was not part of
the programme.... Luke Warm: Fairly funny, also
f a j r ]v libellous, for which reasons it achieved the holv
chuck. You say it is necessary for your real nam°
to remain secret. Well, it might cost us a good deal
if our name didn’t remain secret also.

Public Opinion, and
Beale High-grade Pianos.
The great majority of our Pianos are sold on the personal recommendation of users, and we are constantly receiving letters from our
customers praising the Beale High-Grade Pianos. Our instruments are in use in every part of Australia; in New Zealand, Fiji, Samoa, New
Caledonia, Mauritius, South Africa, India, and on the Continent of Europe; and everywhere they give the same unbounded satisfaction.
Below we give a few (greatly condensed) extracts from unsolicited testimonials from buyers of Beale High-Grade Pianos.

From the very first we have given our

derful factory organisation, and we
pay the best brains and the best skill,

realise that an Australian-made piano ,
is subjected to much closer scrutiny
in Australia than any imported piano,
and therefore we make—we are
compelled to make —our pianos much
better than those of our competitors.
At the same time, by making pianos
in Australia, we are in the position to
make them much better suited to Aus-
tralian conditions. Any competent
cabinetmaker will tell you that timber
seasoned in the cold, damp climate of
Europe and America cannot be ex-
pected to withstand Australian ex-
tremes of dry heat as well as timber
which is seasoned in this climate. This
is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why
so many foreign pianos develop cracked
sounding-boards, split tuning-systems
and faulty casework soon after arrival
in this country. But no man can say
he ever saw a Beale piano with a
cracked sounding-board. We abso-
lutely guarantee that the sounding-
boards of Beale Pianos will never
crack in any climate, because our spe-
cial system of construction renders
cracking an impossibility, at the same
time that it greatly enhances the tonal
qualities of the instrument. Beale
Pianos contain the only practical steel
tuning-system in the world —so there
can be no question of splitting of the
wrest-plank. And then our patented
system of cross-plied woodwork enr
sures permanence for the outside case
of the pianos.

23 and 25 Hunter Street.
“Ami”: W. J. Holloway, just dead in London,
first came into notice on the Sydney side. Charles,
who came on the stage later, was a brother ; he mar-
ried Alice Deorwyn, and Beatrice is their daughter.
W.J. married Mrs. Jenyns, widow of a doctor, and
known on the stage as' Kate Arden. She was the
mother of Essie Jenyns. W.J. acted Dazzle in “Lon-
don Assurance,” at the opening of the Melbourne
Opera House, 1872. When Essie had got sufficient
experience, W.J. headed a dramatic co., of which
she was the star. Atholwood, Greenaway, Inman,
Rolfe, Stewart, jun., and others were in the casts.
This long-sustained venture brought forward an un-
precedented number of young Australian actors in
Shakspearian and other first-rate plays. Essie so
utterly quenched the ladies of the company that one
hardly remembers any of them. When she married
the wealthy Newcastle Wood, of the local brewery,
W.J.’s occupation was largely gone. Essie’s mother
jealously estopped. the young chaps out of the part
of Romeo, for which all those mentioned above were
ambitious. W.J. himself had to be. Romeo, and at
last it became rather absurd. In London he acted
with Irving in “Lear” and “Becket.” His son is a
theatrical manager in South Africa and elsewhere.
“Haresfoot”: Gen6e and the Moscow Ballet are
coming—the biggest thing since the arrival of Sally
Heartburn, and that’s a jump of 22 years. Ballet
rapidly followed opera in London. Addison and
other sensible men thought the ballet better than
the opera. The Spectator of that day declared that
the earliest Italian singers flung heaps of insults, in
their own language, at the fashionable audiences,
which would exclaim “Bravo!” or “Divine!” after
the soprano and tenor had been warbling something
like “You English are a parcel of fools for enriching
mountebanks like us.” During the long, warlike
tension with France, with but litle peace between
1795 and 1815, the ballet in London dropped dead.
It resurrected with a rush when Bonaparte had been
finally overthrown. Then came the era of Tagli-
oni,. Cerito (or Cherrytoe), the Elsslers, Carlotta
Grisi, Grahn, and so forth. The immortal Pas de
Quatre was danced bv Taglioni, Cerito, C. Grisi and
Grahn. Short skirt was the rule; the long sk'rt
came only with Kate Vaughan, Sylvia Grey, Alice
Lethbride and the like.
Recently the Melbourne Board of Public Health
announced its intention of forcing managers of all
theatres and other stuffy places of licensed enter-
tainment to provide an efficient system of artificial
ventilation for their auditoriums. There is only
one objection to the proposed reform: under present
conditions the air at most of the show places is so
heavy with impurities that a bored spectator can
fall asleep quite easily.
In the midst of bruiser Johnson’s recent argu-
ment with the laW on the female question, a cute
Yankee manager adapted “Bella Donna” to the oc-
casion by bodily “lifting” the piece (dialogue, situa-
tions and all), merely changing the characters of
Mrs. Armine and the Egyptian Boroudi to those of
Lucille Cameron and Johnson. The enterprising
pirate was doing “overflowing biz.” when Charles
Frohman suddenly biffed him per injunction.
“J.F.H.”: The revival of negro minstrelsv at
London Palladium has proved a success beyond all
expectations. are only given in the
afternoons, as the Palladium wanted for its usual
variety entertainment in the evening; yet, although
the afternoon is not the best time for amusement
ournoses, the Palladium, which seats 5000, is daily
besieged bv crowds of twice and trefcle that num-
ber, and extra police have had to be requisitioned to
marshal the unprecedented queues, and keep them
in order. A new generation has grown up since
burnt-cork entertainment underwent an eclipse, and
to these people it is a revelation and a novelty.
Besides, even in the palmiest days of minstrelsy ‘ a
troupe of 130 strong was never seen in Londori or
anywhere else.
“Stargazer”: The pathetic craving for happy end-
ings is responsible for the fact that “Milestones” has
a decided tail. After being consistent to his char-
acter and temperament from 1860 to 1912, John
Rhead, slumps- suddenly at the finish. Other char-
acters, too, go back on themselves with a jolt that
lands them right out of the picture, with the result
that the finish gives you an impression of a hurried
and incongruous cleaning-up. To the playgoer afflicted
with a trace of artistry the close is most unhappv.
It might have been cruel to have employed the third
generation to knock out Grandpa Rhead,' but at least
it would have been consistent.
The Pollard co. of operatic youngsters, who have
been making Canada their hunting ground for a year
or so, begin to look homewards, and their next big
bill will be for steamship fares to Australia.
From. London: The speculators who took the Lon-
don Opera House off the hands of its disillusioned
promoter, the N’York Jew Hammerstein, have put
up the shutters. So it may be taken for granted
that variety, plus picture-palace business, has failed
there as well as grand opera, and Hammerstein’s
chances of getting back any of his quarter-million
are bluer than ever. The gorgeous place is built in
the new Kingsway, carved by the London County
Council through a former slum area. Londoners
have not yet discovered this new street, and they
have no use for it at night. In 10 years the groovy,
conservative Londoners may have learned how to
get into Kingsway. At present their ideas of amuse-
ment are centred' in the Strand and Leicester Square.
“J.F.H.”: Stanley Houghton, who scored a success
with “Hindle Wakes,” has had another plav pro-
duced at London Garrick under the title of “Trust
the People.” The name suggests politics, and the
name doesn’t lie. The question is: Suppose a pro-
minent politician gets entangled with a siren and
figures as co-re., should. his constituents give him
the “chuck,” and should he be hounded out of public
life? It sounds interesting, and the piece is well-
constructed. Yet it is not likely to arouse half so
much interest as “Hindle Wakes.” Somehow the
average public shies off politics on the stage.
“Rosna”: Percy Grainger, conducting his Irish jig
for strings, “Molly on the Shore,” at the Balfour-
Gardiner concert, Queen’s Hall, London, was a start-
ling apparition—long yellow hair waving wHdly,
and arms and body waving with it. The maker of
gorgeous noises looked like a little marionette, and
his conducting made you giddy; but they were reallv
heavenly noises if you shut your eyes.
INDUSTRY OR ART?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The leading theatrical managements object to the
registration of an actors’ union, on the ground that
its members are not engaged in an industry.
— Neii'S item.
I watched the ill-stared heroine,
And thought it most unfair
That any lady, such as she,
Who suffered from obesity
Should be compelled to bear
The burden of a frenzied play,
To make a Melbourne holiday.
She had to mount a restless horse,
And from a house-top spring,
And drag the hero, fat and large,
To safety from a burning barge;
The villain, too, would fling
The poor soul from a canvas cliff,
Though she was middle-aged and stiff!
The malefactor also had
A pretty rotten time.
The hero when he came around
Ne’er failed to dash him to the ground ;
The man was past his prime,
But took his share of boot and bash !
Thought I: “The fellow earns his cash!”
The low comedian suffered much ;
His toil he never shirked.
The “gods” roared when he struck the earth
With force and hurt his seat of mirth!
I mused, what time he worked:
“If this is not an industry,
In Hpavpti’s namp whof non if Kn9”
VICTORIA.

IN
BANANA LAND
As a rule, rainfall is measured in Banana-
land by the inch, but in wet seasons Innisl-
- and the Johnstone estimate the down-
pour by the foot. Cairns goes one better
this year, and gravely reckons by the yard.
If the political atmosphere were clearer and
sugar-mills either more frequent or a little
less rapacious, the outlook for the sugar-
grower (who likes his moisture in yards)
w r ould be better this season than ever before
within the memory of the Deadest Inhabi-
tant.
Up North you never know what the rum
may do to you. Hence, probably, this pre-
cautionary advt. in a Rockhampton paper:
WARNING.
ANY PERSON found DAMAGING my Char-
acter during the Easter holidays, will be pro-
ceeded against.
John So-and-So.
It ends vaguely, like a novel with the pages
torn out of the penultimate chapter. What
did, John So-and-So expect to happen to his
character during the Easter holidays? Was
it more fragile then than at other times, or
had he less time than usual to look after it?
A CONTRADICTION.
TEMPERANCE C RANK: “And is year father a teetotaler, my boy?'”
THE B OY: “No, sir, he’s a commercial traveller.”
Distinguished people have a knack of
showing up in unexpected places in Bris-
bane. One Sherlock Holmes appeared before
the Bench last week, and a certain William
Makepeace Thackeray stood before the mag-
istracy a short time ago.
“X.P.D.”: Talk about the irreligious
State of Australia doesn’t apply to the
ancient city of Ipswich, anyhow. Instead
of using corners for their legitimate purpose
of accommodating pubs, Ipswich puts its
churches there —there are two in the main
street. Yet things are so crowded in the
sanctuaries on Sundays that ' there is an
overflow, and complaints are made in print
of the street preachers interfering with the
“inside” worshippers by their loud bellow-
ing about Babylon and Benjamin and Beer-
sheba, Thei;e was a big poll lately on the
question of extending the town boundaries,
but I don’t know that it w T as altogether to
relieve the overlapping of religions.
“Lex” from' Outback :
The moral of this story is that we are a practical
people out here' in . the Bush. The case was heard at
Longreach. Accused was one Winterßottom, : locally
known as “Summertop,” of the ancient Order of
Master Drovers. He was charged with the larceny
of a dog. This charge “Summertop” indignantly
denied, and hired Gentleman One to help him in his
denial. As the case could not be finished the day it
commenced, he had to spend a night in the cells.
Next morning, after hearing the evidence, speeches
of counsel, and charge Of the Judge (Arthur Rut-
ledge), the jury* 1 found the prisoner not guilty.
“Summertop” no sooned heard the words of release
than he slipped from the dock through a window, and
I caught just a glimpse of his coat tails disappearing
in the doorway of, an adjacent pub. The Judge, who
is rather short-sighted, didn’t notice that' the prisoner
had gone; and, (assuming that he was still in the
dock, apologised to him during 15 minutes or so for
the mistake that had been' made ’in his tem-
porary imprisoninent. The dog, the subject of
all the trouble, was still in the hands of the
police. As no less than three persons had claimed
it, the sergeant to know whose property it
was. The Judge did his best to get an agreement,
and at length suggested a conference. A conference
was duly held, and an agreement arrived at whereby
one of the claimants was to get the dog, and the
other two were each to get a pup out of the first
litter coming to this world after the next sitting of
tne Qourt; and the Judge made an order accordingly.
Thereafter all was peace and much beer.
The Victress.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How fair that City was, how dreaming fair!
As I drew near,
My feet no longer faltered.... that was past.
I held the desert vigil, and the fast
Were purposed for this fruitage. “Lo, at
last
The One I seek awaits me, waiting there!”
1 spoke so of the City, drawing near.
I spoke so, knowing well that She w’ould
hear,
Who waited there,
Flashing a spirit summons from the goal.
And rich flame-pinions fluttered at my soul
As would no longer brook my feet’s control,
And sped me through the winnowing gulfs of
air,
Till all the City lay beneath me there.
A while I hovered o’er it, and the sight
Of such delight
Sent rapture-tremblings through me, till I
thought
“No mortal hands this City can have
wrought;
It is a mirage only, and. distraught
From over-yearning vigil, I surmise
This Place of Dreams to be in very wise!”
But there, just like a lovely rose, it lay
Amid the grey,
Wide wastes of desert, blossoming alone
As though, with its rare beauty, to atone
For lack of other sweetness by its own.
And then, within the City, I descried
Her face.... and, seeing, saw none else
beside.
I saw Her, naught beside her did I see:
And, seemingly,
It was as queen in this enchanted place
She lay there sleeping, all Her body’s grace
Robed in rich crimson, overlain with dace
Of threaded silver, while its golden hem
Swayed with Her breathing, as in requiem.
Her naked feet with golden anklets dight;
Her hands’ delight
Clasped inward to the jewelled zone that
traced
The yielding sweetness of her rounded waist;
Her lips’ red curves, imploring me to taste
(Whom Fate had long allotted as Her sire),
Thrilled me, enamored, with a surge of fire.
I swooped unto Her on the wings of flame,
And heard my name
Fall from Her lips as if in happy dream;
Nearing Her, all Her body seemed to beam
With sweet expectance. Then She waked—
the gleam
Of my flame-pinions was too utter bright,
Falling upon Her in despendent flight.
She viewed me with the wonder of a child ;
And then She smiled,
Closing again in rich content Her eyes,
And through Her lips came such a rush of
sighs
As told, within her heart, what rhapsodies
Made happy tumult there; Her heaving
breast
And arms upreaching to me, love contest.
What space it was we loved each other so,
I do not know:
No night there seemed to be nor any noon.
Nor any time nor place—but in a swoon
Of luscious joying, planet, sun and moon
Floated beside, beneath and overhead,
Hued as of blended green and gold and red.
I only know I weakened was at last
By some great blast
As though a High God summoned all his
host
To arms around him ; ghost on paling ghost
Sped to my vision, nor dared I accost
One in his speeding. . . .Yet as they went by,
Each looked upon me, moaning fearfully.
There was no City, and my love, whose
charms
Had filled my arms,
Was not beside me. On a desert plain
I writhed in an infinitude of pain.
As something wet dripped on me. . . .Was it
rain?
Looking above, I spied a harpy brood
Clamant to Her, my Victress, for their food,
As 10, She held a Red Thing, oozing blood.
It was my heart!. . . .And then I understood.
QUEENSLAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 12, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
To-day was Brisbane d la Henley, and in
celebration of the honor Providence rested
from the labors of its slush, and sun-decked
our winter-frocked population. Talking of
rain and other dampnesses reminds me that
Queensland’s representative lady rowing
four defeated the oars-women from Albert
Park (Vic.) by a margin of inches, and so
retained the ladies’ inter-State rowing cham-
pionship.
Amongst others who watched the marine
argument were the Governor and a select
few stationed aboard the Lucinda. Mayor
Doggett floated on the accumulated waters
upon the launch Ena. The Brisbane Club
and its femininity swarmed on the Emerald,
and every available yacht and motor-boat
was packed with flags and girls. The United
Service Institution moored a house-boat
close to Merthyr bank, where swarms of
temporarily-workless citizens fraternised
with the permanently-employed bull-dog
ants.
The house-boat was a gay affair, and re-
freshment tents were conveniently planted
on the nearest projection of land. Here the
Vision, in white silk and a black hat, came
in search of the eompany that cheers, and
the cup that utters loud applause but doesn’t
inebriate. Amongst the said company was
Oscar Asche, in a dark-blue male frock. I
don’t know what Lily Brayton wore, because
she wasn’t present.
Some of the decorated boats looked very
fine, noticeably the Lurline, in nale mauve
and greenery, and the Ruby shone like an
animated pagoda on its last keel.
The Victorian girls took their sculling de-
feat with every appearance of philosophy,
but, as you know, my dear, feminine phil-
osophy covers a multitude of boiling lan-
guage. They said they would be round next
year for another race, and I presume they
will. They looked like it.
On Tuesday the. Brisbane Ladies’ Rowing
Club fed the Victorian rowers on board the
State derelict Lucinda. On Wednesday they
were taken to look over one or two of the
flourishing little farms at Nundah, after
which the relentless bun was spread for
them in the Wassell home at Teneriffe. On
Thursday another tea jag was given them
by Mrs. Cowlishaw, president of the Chil-
dren’s Hospital.
Sandgate’s new Mayor, Ferguson, and his
wife, shook some dozens of. hands at the
Sandgate Town Hall last Tuesday. A more
or less musical programme and a breathless
hop were flung in as makeweights. Teenie
Annan (now Mrs. Jordan) uplifted a rich
contralto voice at the former function. As
a lifter she was great.
Evelyn Wrench, founder of the Overseas
Club, and his sister, found their way into
this hamlet at the beginning of the week.
They are .at Toowoomba at present, but are
due fo return in a day or two. Miss Wrench
is a somewhat busy, clever woman, who re-
tains enough femininity to bother about her
dfessi She spoke at the National Council
of Women on Friday, and looked smart
and well turned-out.
At the same N.C.W. meeting one lady read
a long paper on “Heredity,” and endeav-
ored to- prove her arguments by relating in-
cidents of family habits in guinea-pigs and
cabbages, i It was very enthralling but not
so enthralling as ostrich plumes or pending
divorce actions.
Gaud Mayor Doggett recepted the Vic.
lady rowers in the Town Hall On Tuesday
morn, and with delightful impartiality
called on every male person present to “say
a few words.” Alderman Leslie Corrie,
when his turn came, struck the audience
dumb by emphatically remarking: “I hate
rowing, and know nothing about it !” An
honest man's the most difficult work
Providence to put up with.
The Asche-Brayton season has been verv
successful here. Asche is verv like Hienold
in his methods, and seems to have a similar
knack of making friends. Likewise, nobodv
has anything but pleasant things to mention
about Lily Brayton. She was It in “The
Tamine of the Shrew.” but her Desdemono
might be better. Also, it might be
worse, and when one thinks how much
worse it might be, a large, pallid pause
descends upon the critic. Asche, who shines
as Hajj, Petruchio and Othello, is not so
successful as Sh.vlock. Indeed, he is such a
dirty-looking Israelite, that one can under-
stand Antonio’s somewhat pointed objections
to his company. Still, Antonio may have
been prejudiced, or Shylock may have been
kept so busy by Antonio’s persecution that
he hadn’t time to wash.
Harold Forbes and little Marjorie Webb
one of the large clan of the medico Webb
family, took the matrimonial fence last
Wednesday with the usual rice and satin
accompaniments. Four pretty bridesmaids
were on hand in case of accidents.
Mrs. Ross Munro has left for England to
bring home a wandering daughter, and Mrs
.Tim Robertson, one of our handsome younger
matrons, is on her way to Sydney to meet
daughter Marjorie, w r ho is travelling under
the Taverner wing.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Probate granted in the will of the late
David Hay Dalrymple showed that that
politician left an estate valued at nearly
£34,000. The genial but caustic Tory jester
was evidently no small potatoes as a
financier.
“Solit-link”: Wienholt, who includes a
boxing exhibition as a side-line in his elec-
tioneering stunt, learned the trick from
Armstrong, the present Speaker of the
Bananaland Assembly. When Armstrong
first stumped the Lockyer 19 years ago, he
carried a pair of gloves in his saddle-pouch,
and whenever he met with a few husky
young fellows he would show them a punch
or two. The mittens and his promises to
run light railways through the electorate
(in place of the very bad roads that existed,
though Armstrong was chairman of the locai
Shire Council) put him into the House.
Needless to say, after Armstrong got where
he wanted to get, the railways never mater-
ialised, and the mittens were put away for
good. He pulls through well enough' now
on bluff.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hog-skin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of Tub
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J, P. Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

Why
does Odol absolutely supersede
all other preparations for cleansing
the mouth and teeth ?
Because of its remarkable power of
suffusing the entire oral cavity with
a thin but thoroughly effective anti-
septic coating which maintains its
protective influence for hours after
the mouth has been rinsed with it.
While tooth pastes and powders can, at the best, be
effective only during the few moments of application,
Odol continues co exert its antiseptic and refreshing
powers, gently but persistently, long after use.
It is this lasting effect that gives to daily users of Odol the
absolute assurance that their mouths are permanently protected
against the processes of fermentation and decomposition which,
if not guarded against, inevitably destroy the teeth.

Worse Every Day, Not Able to Walk,
Scratched Till Nearly Fainted with
Torture, Cuticura Soap and Oint-
ment Gave Instant Relief, Now
Completely Cured.
15 Prospect St., Waverley, Sydney, N. S.
W.—“ Several years ago I Was living in
Narrandera on the Western Line when I
suddenly contracted a bad leg. It first
started With slight itching, but it became
Worse and worse every day until the irrita-
tion became past endurance, I used to
scratch it till the blood came and I can as-
sure you I had no peace night or day and
in consequence became seriously ill,
“My leg getting worse I had to give up
work, not being able to Walk I had to lie
on a couch all day and at night was lifted
on the bed. I used to scratch my leg till I
nearly fainted with the torture, I used
everything I saw advertised but nothing
did me the slightest bit of good. I was get-
ting worse all the time and I was almost in
despair. I saw Cuticura Ointment adver-
tised and being only too glad to grasp at
anything that I thought might give me
relief I sent at cmce to our chemist for some
of the Cuticura Remedies- I washed my
leg at once with the Cuticura Soap then
applied the Cuticura Ointment and to my
Utter amazement I felt instant relief.
“After the third application, my leg was
decidedly better, and from that time my
leg kept improving every time I applied the
Cuticura Ointment until it got completely
cured and has remained so to this day/'
(Signed) Win. Bundock, Aug. 1911.

10,000 FREE TRIAL BOXES.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached" the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. 1 invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hai<r grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

South Aus., in unveiling a memorial to Ridley
as inventor of the wheat-stripper, claims to have
built the biggest harvester in the Commonwealth,
if not in the world. It has three independent
electric motors, to drive thresher,, beaters and
winnower; a 26ft. comb, in three sections, and a
lorry at one side to take the bags as they are filled.
It needs five men to run it, travels 2lt miles an hour
(kerosene tractor), and tackles 60 acres a day.
It was made by Illman, of Balaklava (S.A.), and is
used by Young and Ralli in the wheat belt of
N.S.W. And it all sounds very impressive, and
will no doubt go on sounding like that until some
day something will go wron<r with one small part,
and, the nearest engineering works being miles
away, the whole thing will be hung up, and the
wheat will call in vain for instant harvesting.

That particular brand of criminal Who adds 5 p.c.
of Water to his milk and is fined £2O has, a per-
fect right to Consider himself ill-treated when he
compares his case With this one. A Narrabeen
(N.S.W.) butcher Was proceeded against by a Pure
Poods inspector. The inspector’s evidence showed
that (1) a quantity of beef Was placed on the cart
very close to the horse’s hind-quarters, (2) the
horse was flicking the meat with its tail, (3) the
beef was only partly covered with hessian, (4) what
wasn’t covered With hessian Was covered with dirt
and flies, (5) the driver Was sitting on the hessian,
and had his boots o'rt the meat, (6) in the back of
the cart were 10 Carcases of mutton which were
partly covered with dust. And the magistrate fiped
him £2—a whole 40s. That ridiculous fine sug-
gests that it is about time butchers Were permitted
to work only under license, dirt and flies and other
trimmings to be considered when the ticket came
up for renewal. In fact, no harm Would be done if
all vendors of foodstuffs Were required to take out
a ticket, With the warning that if they didn’t stick
very close to the Pure Foods Act the thing would
drop dead at the end Of the year.
* * * *
James Mills, the Maoriland Union Steam Ship
magnate, has been giving a few figures about his co.
Eleven years back the red funnel firm possessed 56
vessels of 88,000 tons. To-day, including ships in
process of construction’, it owns' 79, of 230,000 tons.
Eleven years ago the average tonnage per steamer
was 1576; to-day it is nearly double that. Coal
consumption has increased in the time from 250,000
tons annually to 500,000 tons, while the wage sheet
has swollen from £196,000 to £400,000. Goiu t
back further than did the managing director, the
good luck and good management of the co. can be
traced from 1875, when it was formed in Dunedin
(M.L.). It commenced business as the owner of
three steamers, with an aggregate tonnage of 724
tens, conveying passengers and cargo to and from the
main town to neighboring small settlements. In
five years it had practically rubbed Out competition
in Maoriland’s coastal trade and in the trade between
M.L. and Australia. Then it absorbed the lines
engaged in the coal trade of the West Coast (M.L.),
and, later, the ships and business of the Tasmanian
Steam Navigation Co. Thence on, its. operations
spread throughout British and Californian waters.
For a youthful co. of about 38 years it has done
some considerable leaping.
F. M. B. Fisher, Maoriland Minister in charge of
Finance, does not look with favor on the proposal
to hold a proper inquest on every fire which eats up
a piece of the national wealth. He thinks it
would be too costly ; but, as a fact, in countries
where exhaustive inquiries are held, there has been
a diminution of the fire-stick enterprise which has
far more than made good the expense of the inquiry.
In addition to the interest on Cohen’s loans, Maori-
land is up against an annual fire-waste of over half
a million, and the expenditure of a few thousands in
the way Fisher doesn’t think wise might reduce that
total a lot.
“B.”: A metropolitan paper tells me that “pawn-
brokers, who have sold pledges, must give full par-
ticulars of the sale to the original owner of the
pledge and hand over to him any cash received in
excess of his legal charges.” And what’s the good
when the particulars are supplied ? The oawn-
, broker is not obliged by law to sell when the pledge-
time has expired. Unless the pledge is perishable,
he can hold it till the accumulating interest swal-
lows every penny the article is likely to fetch at
sale. He” can tell pretty well what it will bring,
and he doesn’t let go till the last minute. Some
pawnbrokers, it is true, act fairly promptly; their
safes are so full that they must empty them. But
in most cases Aaron looks on the forfeited pledge
as a fine interest-bearing asset, and when a sale is
finally effected he will produce.. his book to the
owner, showing that the interest and auctioneer’s
fees have not left a shred for distribution.
FRUGAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’Ve heard of meanest blokes among
The farming men from near and far,
But meanest told by pen or tongue
Is Ogilvie, of Candlebar.
Kate’s favor Sandy’s heart made glad;
In beauty Jane ranked far beneath.
He married Jane because she had
A set of artificial teeth.
Upon his wedding eve, to save
His bawbees, Tam in secret hied
And stole from off a baby’s grave
A white bouquet to give the bride.
VICTORIA.
S.S.
ON BENDIGO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And do you recollect the days
AVhen life of golden strands was spun,
When cheerily we trod our ways
From daybreak to the set of sun;
When Hope went with us, side by side,
While Fortune’s lamp was still aglow
To light our paths, whate’er betide,
On Bendigo ?
At night we made the rafters ring
With thunder of deep-chested song,
The chorus rolling with a swing
That seemed to heave the world along!
Or by some spectral poppet-head—
The warden of dim shafts below—
We yarned round camp-fires flickering red
On Bendigo.
They tell me that, at Eaglehawk,
You struck a reef—and kept your head!
I’ve only reaped the barren stalk,
While beards have blanched and years have
fled.
But well enough, and best of all
(For Hope must fade and Youth must go),
We’ve heard that rousing bugle-call
On Bendigo!
Come backward down the years and meet
In memory of that lordlier time
When all the hills were wild and sweet,
And all our pulses beat to rhyme.
Possession rings the curtain down—
The seeking is the joy to know;
And every future wore a crown,
On Bendigo!
I would not part with that red gold
That I have won (nor you, I’ll swear!),
Which, misered in my heart, I hold
As nuggets for the mind to wear;
These draw me back to splendid ways—
They quicken pulses, waning slow,
With music of old gilded days
On Bendigo.
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

Congo Damp Course is a
tough felt somewhat similar
to Congo Roofing, which is
immune from deterioration
by dampness or acids. It is

14 YEARS* TORTURE FROM
DYSPEPSIA* HEADACHE
Could Take Scarcely any Pood for Weeks Together———
Pains Were Dreadful— —Told there was no Cure for Her.
A Housewife's Story of Suffering, and how
Bile Beans Restored her to Health.
( Confirmed, by Sworn Declaration.)
Mrs. Annie Rooney, of 16 Vine Street, Redfern,
Sydney, was in a terrible state of health through
her stomach refusing to do its work, and, but for
the timely arrival of Bile Beans, the doctor’s pro-
nouncement that she was incurable may have come
true. To a special reporter Mrs. Rooney related
her interesting story as follows: —
“For 14 years I endured the utmost torture from
dyspepsia and indigestion. The pains in the pit of
the stomach, in the hack, and between the shoul-
ders were dreadful. With headaches I suffered most
cruelly—the sharp, shooting pains between the
temples, and on the top of the head—as if the scalp
were lifting off —being terrible. I lost all my
appetite completely, and could go for weeks
together with scarcely any food. I became thor-
oughly run-down and out-of-sorts, and fearfully
depressed, with a feeling as if something were
about to happen.
“Although under the doctor for 12 months I
continued to be fearfully wracked with these pains,
as if the brief respites I derived from his treatment
only made each succeeding attack more severe and
harder to bear. Eventually the doctor told me
■:> ,
r
i
MRS. ROONEY,
cured by Bile Beans.
that there was no cure for me.
“Just when my state of health was about at its very worst I was strongly recom-
mended to try Bile Beans. Fortunately I followed the recommendation, for the first
few doses made quite an improvement in my condition. Thus encouraged, I determined
to pursue a thorough course of this medicine. I did so, and each succeeding ”h. se
found a corresponding improvement in my health, which was daily improving. Bile
Beans acted on my system as a tonic. My appetite returned, the headaches and all bodily
pains vanished, and the liver and kidneys were restored to their normal action.
Finally my health completely returned, and I realised what a wonderful restorative
Bile Beans are. . . . . .
“I am pleased indeed to recommend Bile Beans to all needing a reliable stomach
and liver medicine, with the painful . symptoms
I have referred to; and I heartily testify to their
sterling merits.”
DECLARATION
“I, Mrs. Annie Rooney, of 16 Vine Street, Redfern, Sydney, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the above
statements re my illness, and the benefits I derived from BILE BEANS, and that said statements
are a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by the said BILE BEANS ; and I give my
full permission to the publication of the facts in any form you please, which facts I give volun-
tarily, without receiving payment or inducement of any kind; and I make this Declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the Oaths Act, 1900.”
“(Signed) ANNIE ROONEY.”
Subscribed and declared before me at Redfern, Sydney, this thirtieth day of September, One
Thousand Nine Hundred and Twelve. (Signed) L. BREARLEY, J.P.

Dawn at Mi son’s Point.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AN evil mist came up the bay,
A chill wind o’er the waters blew ;
The young dawn in a mantle grey
Sighed for the secret thing she knew.
On stilts of light the boats stalked by.
Half-seen amid the creeping gloom,
While like a faint star set on high*
A small light glimmered from your room.
Vast formless shadows came and went,
A troop of Bacchanalian ghosts,
Rude spectres that the east wind sent
To haunt Bohemia’s thirsty coasts.
The scented hopes that redly burned
In caverns gay with yesterbeer
The joyless feet of morning spurned
While death-day wailed about her ear.
The discord of a clanging bell
With horrid warning smote the air—
You dropped your blind : I knew full well
You screwed the paper from your hair.
Oh, heavy w r as the morn to me,
Beyond the waters care was king—-
Spent night, unrobed of mystery,
Drooped westward on a broken wing.
The foggy voice of hooting steam,
The damp cold biting at my heart,
llad torn the curtains of my dream,
And burst its silken webs apart.
So late the crayfish redly glowed.
The world was full of youngest beer,
Tall mirth with laughter for her load
Crowned the gay wetness of our cheer.
And after, ’neath the starlight pale,
I walked amid the purple dark
As one who with a magic sail
Glides joyward in an elfin barque.
I heard the thunders of tall waves
Upon the sleeping mountains press,
Closing with crystal bars the caves
Where mermaid dreams the weeds
caress.
And high above me on the steep
I saw your chambet far withdrawn,
The fragrant casket that should keep
Your whiteness treasured till the daw T n.
Too short the watch. The grey light sprang
Unbidden from the shrouded east.
The steamers screamed, the fog-bell sang
To bid day’s vultures to the feast.
But upward still upon the height
I saw your window through the haze,
And knew that by a candle’s light
You pulled the laces of your stays.
The graceless dawning swelled and grew,
One faint pub one’d its portal thin,
And like striped tulips in the dew
I saw the capsuled joys within.
Yet heavy was that morn to me,
Remembering all our yesterbeer.
While the chill breathing of the sea
Blew dankly in my place of cheer.
But you, with steaming coffee warmed,
Showed to the dav a rosy face,
And with a crystal laughter charmed
The mist that wrapped the old grey
place.
Till with light wings it rose and fled
As oreads flee the face of men.
The while with rising hope I sa’d :
“Ho. barman, fill-’em-up-agen !”
N.s.w.
GEORGE STREET.
An Episode in the Life of the
Drexels.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TERRY DREXEL would have given
anything to avert this misfortune. He
would have given half his money, but
as he had a great deal the loss of half could
not adequately represent his regret at the
unfortunate occurrence. He would have
given years of his life, he told himself, if
his wife had not chanced to come into the
quiet little tea-room while he was there
with Mrs. Crombie. And as he was at the
last of his thirties, years were very precious
to him. He was exasperated with himself,
with Mrs. Crombie, and with the law of
chances. His thoughts outraced his motor,
now running at high speed on the level
country road, and were with his wife. He
recalled her glance of surprise and startled
pain as she passed the table where he and
his companion were seated....to hurt the
being he loved best, and through such a woman
as Mrs. Crombie! Drexel struck his knee
with his closed hand and said something,
and the chauffeur, thinking he had heard an
order, looked round hurriedly and lowered
the car’s speed. “Go on, go on,” said
Drexel. “I did not speak.”
His wife was lying on a couch in her
dressing-room. A boy of four or five. years
was asleep on the floor, his face resting on
an open picture book. The lady turned her
head on the violet cushions that accentu-
ated her clear pallor, and were uniform with
the tone of her eyes.
“You are home early, Terry. Be careful"
—as he stooped to kiss her —“don’t step on
the Littlest One.”
Drexel seated himself, still holding the
hand she had held out to him.
“How is it with you, Ina? You are not
ill?”
“I don’t feel as well as I did at four
o’clock this afternoon.”
She was looking steadily at him, but he
only pressed closer the hand within his own,
while the vertical line between his eyes
deepened. When she moved as if to with-
draw her hand, he spoke : “Since four o’clock
this afternoon I doubt if tliere_ has been a
more wretched man in Australia than my-
self. Wretched on your account. I would
die to save you a heartache, only I know
that my dying would give you a worse one.”
llis smile was strained.
“But why are you wretched? Is if at
being ‘found out,’ so to speak? If I had
not seen you with that woman, you would
still have broken the promise—a promise I
did not demand—you made six months ago
on our wedding day.”
“Circumstances I could not foresee com-
pelled me to break it. But how ask you to
believe that you chanced upon the one and
only time I met Mrs. Crombie since I knew
you! I don’t think I should believe if I
were in your place. Yet it is true.”
His wife nodded. “Yesterday,” she said,
“the Littlest One promised me not to go
into the lake again, but in the afternoon
his brother pushed him in. It was like that,
Terry ?”
“Just like that, with the enormous differ-
ence that the Littlest Rascal stood on the
edge hoping to be pushed in. I was not
looking for my trouble.”
“And you are not going to tell me
further?”
“Not unless you force me.” He had
dropped her hand, and was holding her face
in his palms.
“How could I force you?” she asked.
“Well —-in 20 ways. You might not speak
to me, for instance.”
She smiled up at him. “Well, I won’t
speak to you again. Not until I have some-
thing to say.”
“Ina, to-morrow Mrs. Crombie goes to
India to be married. I have seen and heard
the last‘of her. If not, you shall know of
the next seeing or hearing. You will let the
dead past bury its dead, dearest?”
“I’ll help you roll the boulder on it; but”
—her direct glance challenged his—“if, at
anytime I should ask of you a like proof of
absolute trust, you will give it?”
Drexel’s laugh was so loud and spontane-
ous that the Littlest One awoke and claimed
his stepmother’s attention. She pushed the
hair from his forehead and looked into his
great brown eyes.
“What eyes to be wasted on a boy, Terry !
You haven’t brown eyes—neither had his
mother.”
“No. Eleanor’s eyes were grey. But my
sisters are brown-eyed. Here, you, Littlest
One, take your Zoo books and other truck,
and clear out to your nurse.”
* * * *
The law of chances decreed that Drexel
should answer the telephone in his wife’s
sitting-room late in the forenoon. A dis-
tinguished and over-worked professional man
would not be likely to be reading “The
Hound of Heaven” to his wife before lunch
on a working day. Probably the person at
the other end of the wire thought it
unlikely.
I dimly guess what Time in mists confounds ;
Yet ever and anon a trumpet sounds
From the hid battlements of Eternity;
Those shaken mists a space unsettle
Into the splendid harmony of the lines
broke the brisk and business-like whir of
the telephone. Drexel knew that his wife
preferred to answer her own calls, and he
would now have made no attempt to do so,
but as she rose she suddenly sank back with
the half-humorous, half-pained expression
that denotes a foot “gone to sleep.”
Drexel’s interrogative, “Yes? Yes?” into
the receiver was simultaneous with the other
speaker’s, “You there, I” the voice (a
man’s) stopped suddenly with Mrs. Drexel’s
first name unfinished. To her husband’s re-
iterated “Yes? Yes?” there was no re-
sponse. His wife, her clear pallor a little
tinged with pink, was at his elbow as he
was about to hang up the receiver.
“I think I know who it is,” she said.
Then: “Yes, Ina Drexel speaking.” A pause
of some length, and with the words, “Yes,
I’ll come this afternoon,” she rang off.
“A friend of mine is sick,” she volun-
teered. “Go on, Terry. ‘Those shaken mists
a space’ were the last words. Wasn’t I in-
terested to remember?”
She snoke with a kind of strained merri-
ment. Drexel read to the end of the poem,
and with the final word glanced over at his
wife. She was staring intently into space
at some mental objective which he felt had
no connection with him.
All through the reading of the superb
lamentation, Drexel had been so bored by
the poet’s theme that he was conscious of an
undercurrent of contemptuous irritation.
As a scholar he appreciated the perfection
of what he called a symbolic whine. As a
man of the world of action (and unbelief)
he was unconsciously thinking that a bullet
would have ended for him such utter deso-
lation. Not many days later, near the dawn,
after a sleepless night, he caught himself
voluntarily re-reading the booklet with a
new appreciation of the poet’s mental con-
dition. Here was a fellowship of acute
misery. His wife had not enlightened him
as to the identity of her sick friend, the man
who half-spoke her name through the tele-
phone. She had gently brushed aside his
inquiries and offers to be of use. He was
learning how keen is the unhappiness of
being shut out from any part of the life of
the one we love best. This was for him the
serpent’s tooth: his wife had a secret trouble
he might not share, and was not allowed to
understand.
“I'll want you again in half an hour, you
may as well wait.” Drexel spoke to his
chauffeur as he was going up the steps of his
city office.
“Half an hour,” echoed the chauffeur.
“That would just give me time to take the
fruit and flowers to Albury Chambers, sir.”
Drexel paused midway on the steps, and
asked over his shoulder: “The Albury
Chambers? What are they?”
“Residential flats, sir. T can be back
in”
“Go on, then. I’m in no particular
hsstG^
Of his confidential clerk a little later he
asked, “Do you think you could get me a
list of the tenants of Albury Chambers?”
To himself he said. “I may be able to help
her indirectly.” That was the film with
which he masked his white-hot jealousy. The
clerk reckoned the job an easy one.
Before noon the list was in his hand. At
a glance he saw that men only lived at
Albury Chambers. Scanning the list for the
first time, all the names were strange to
him. At the second and more careful read-
ing, that of Lacrosse seemed not unfamiliar.
Thinking back through his mental files, he
discovered a certain Captain Lacrosse. The
prefix to this name was “Mr.” Drexel rang
up a friend of his, manager of a shipping
company.
“Didn’t a Captain Lacrosse command one
of your vessels?” he asked.
“No,” was the reply. “We never had any-
thing so gay—and that’s saving something!
He was skipper of the ILM.S. Aztec.”
“Where is he now?”

UOAV.”

Standing at the head of the stairs and

the guilty and humiliated party—but he was

He wanted the story with the commonplace,

long gloves. In the lift he said anologeti-

why didn’t a 7 ou trust me as I trusted you?

The Siren and the First Mate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE caught my eye in the offing, and
bore down on me like a clipper on a
wind.
She was one of those large, white-skinned,
brown-eyed, expensive women Avho throw a
man a rope whether he wants it or not —-
and he never refuses it. There Avas no mis-
taking her smile of greeting, nor the in-
tention of her outstretched hand, which I
guiltily accepted, as a torrent of contralto
utterances poured from her full red lips
accusing me in one breath of being “a hor-
rid man,” and asking, without affording an
opportunity for reply 7, how I dared be in
Sydney without letting her know.
This had me beat; for, although Sydney
is often a port of mine for a fevv days, I
had no recollection of ever having met her
before. And she Avas not the type of woman
one readily forgets.
I was—or, rather, she —I mean we were
still holding hands (such a deliciously cool
hand. I could feel it through her glove).
“Now. no excuses,” she exclaimed. “I
won’t listen to them. I’ll forgive .you ; but
for goodness sake take me somewhere —we
can’t stand here like a pair of” I didn’t
catch the word because the spare boom she
carried had a lot of tinkling things hanging
from it. “Come in here, you wicked fel-
low !” And in spite of my feeble protests
she towed me into a tea-shop.
It was half-past four, and the rooms Avere
fairly full; but she piloted me to a corner
where we had a little table all to ourselves,
and as Ave sat down I began—“My dear
tnadam” ——
“Oh, rats!” she said, hitching het chair
stem-on to me. “There’s no place like a
crowd for being really alone. And now’’ —
here she dabbed at her chin with a little
dabbcr which she unshipped from the tink-
ling thing—“and now tell me everything.”
A large order —and I hate to disappoint
a lady. However, it was of no consequence,
for that woman was a regular talking
machine. She didn’t even stop while they
were taking our order. From her conversa-
tion I gathered that we had been, at least
friends, three years ago.
“Three hundred!” 1 gurgled wildly.
“Awfully nice of you to say so,” she
babbled. “But you ought to have found
some way of letting me know you were in
town. I can understand you not caring to
cail or ring up, or —er —even write while —
while George was there” —I hadn’t the
slightest idea why I should have objected to
George—“but you might have done some-
thing. You are so terribly clever, you could
easily have found a way.
“I’m glad I met you, anyhow,” she
chortled biting a biscuit with beautiful white
teeth. “I would never have forgiven you if
you had gone away without seeing me. But
you would never have done that!”
Here was the first really intoxicating
smile I had ever met, and it was succeeded
by a look of penitent sadness as she confided
to her tea cup that she sometimes thought
of the old days. “I know it’s wrong,” she
said, briskly, as if it Avas all.a thing of the
past now, and she would pever be guilty
again.
“Nearly all the nice things are wrong,” I
sighed.
“Is that why you are”
“Nice?” I suggested.
“No, wrong!” she whispered, with a play
of her eyebrows.
I was glad I had that reputation. We
laughed knowingly', and she poured me an-
other cup of tea.
“I’ve just been seeing him off to Tas-
mania,” she announced, in a tone she would
have used if telling the gardener to poison
the dog—“on business. If you promise to
be good-—-very 7, very much better than you
were last time (I was very, very cross with
you then)” —here her voice sank to a sibi-
lant whisper—“you may come and have din-
ner with me.”
Well? What would you have done?
Up to now I bad hardly had a chance to
speak a dozen Avords ; but I held her hand
in the taxi —it was near dark —and I kissed
her in the shadoAV of the porch. She
stamped her foot, and reminded me of my
promise. I pleaded guilty, and repeated the
offence.
There are some scents that intoxicate a
man. The draAving-room swam with them.
Not knowing how bad I had been “last
time,” I proceeded under reefed topsails,
heaving the lead every feAv minutes, for I
found I was in \ 7 ery shallow Avater. She
shoAved me “George’s room,” where I was to
brush up for dinner.
I don’t remember a thing about dinner
except the liquid glances and honeyed words
of the woman who sat opposite to me. I
know we had Avine, because I could feel my
eyes expanding and contracting—it’s a
habit of theirs Avhen I dine well—and she
kept on growing bigger and whiter, and her
lips redder and her eyes brighter. The
world might haA'e been spinning on its OAvn
axis—l certainly was gyrating on mine, as
oblivious of everything else I rose and leant
over the table to gather her into my 7 arms
... .and... .when I woke up I was in a
paddock near Coogee, and it was about
three o’clock on a frosty 7 morning.
I had no idea how I got there. Neither
had I a stiver left of the eighty pounds ten
(did I mention that it happened in Cup
week?) which I had collected at Tatter-
sail’s not ten minutes before I met—
George’s friend.
I walked back to my ship, and never said
a Avord. You see, I have a wife and two
kids in Auckland; and maybe it was no
more than I deserved.
D. H. SOUTER.
Kismet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE days are hawks, Belov&d,
That feed on hearts of men.
The camel-train goes westward,
And breast-high blows the sand;
And what am I that shudder
To quench the smoking brand?
The days are hawks, BelovSd,
That feed on hearts of men.
Last night the bamboo shivered ;
I heard the wind go by,
The homeless wind, Belov&d,
That hears the hidden things ;
I saw the bright steel redden ;
I heard the beat of wings
Last night. .. .Last night, Belovbd,
I heard the wind go by.
What flesh shall stand against ye,
O ye who cast the die?
I heard the stalking lion
Choke back his sobbing breath,
And crouch before the presence
That only men call Death!
What flesh shall stand against ye,
O ye who cast the die?
To-night is ours, Beloved !
To-morrow’s foes are far.
Your eyes are soft as lightnings
That play across the south.
And more than Paynim plunder
The red wine of your mouth.
To-night is ours, Beloved ;
To-morrow’s foes are far!
To-night the gods are laughing.
We listen and forget—-
Forget the slow tides ebbing,
Forget the creeping sand.
O Maya ! Maya ! moon-gift
Upon a beggar’s hand.
To-night the gods are laughing,
We listen and forget!
The days are hawks, Beloved,
That feed on hearts of men.
To-morrow is a young wind,
We follow where it blows.
But who shall reap the voting wheat
Or pluck To-morrow’s rose?
The days are hawks, Beloved,
That feed on hearts of men !
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
